HEADLINE: Parents as Leaders, Using the Systems

BYLINE: By MELINDA TUHUS 

 BODY:
AMID all the bad news of family and societal breakdown in millennial America, the Parent Leadership Training Institute is on the case: helping parents become advocates for their children.
The institute, which was founded in Hartford in 1991, wound up its first class in New Haven with a graduation last Monday. At a pregraduation session, parents traded success stories: one received an award from her child's school, another had had a letter published in the local newspaper, a third announced a scheduled meeting with the Mayor of New Haven to discuss issues of concern to parents. 
 This is not a parenting class in the usual sense of the term. "Direct parenting skills are more of a secondary gain," says Barbara Tinney, executive director of the New Haven Family Alliance, which is the lead agency for the training program in New Haven. "The primary gain is to be advocates for children and to understand the macro-level issues" of how social and political institutions function and how parents can have an impact on them.
This class includes 20 parents of diverse backgrounds, because, says Ms. Tinney, "We want them to learn that regardless of differences, they have a lot in common." Students are black, Hispanic and white; single, married and divorced, and include foster parents and grandparents. They are of varying socio-economic backgrounds from eight New Haven neighborhoods as well as from North Haven, Hamden and Shelton. They range in age from their 20's through their 50's, and include 2 fathers and 18 mothers.
The state's Commission on Children initiated the first Parent Leadership Training Institute with help from the American Leadership Forum. The first 10-session curriculum focused on leadership development and was designed by the commission's executive director Elaine Zimmerman and two Windham teachers, Catina Caban-Owen and Igor Espitiia.
"It went very well," says Ms. Tinney, "but parents said they needed information on the systems parents interact with, so the next 10 weeks were added." This civics portion of the program includes a practicum, in which each member of the class identifies an issue she or he feels passionate about. "They design the practicum to move the issue forward, hopefully to something they can implement in their communities," she adds.
For example, one mother in the Hartford training program identified a neighborhood library that was scheduled to close. She was able to keep it open. Another organized a program for children being raised by grandparents.
While New Haven class members have not yet completed the practicum portion of their training, they have identified such wide-ranging topics as voluntary prayer in schools, a family reading program, and an elementary school level drug prevention program.
Parents help define the curriculum and participate in program evaluation. Graduates serve as mentors to the succeeding class. Each of the 20 sessions works toward a specific goal: diversity; how change takes place within oneself, the family and the community; conflict resolution; community assets; how city, state and school systems work; becoming media literate; public speaking; public policy as a vehicle for democracy, citizen input and change for children; how laws are made, and outcomes and accountability.
Ms. Tinney works with a team of 25 volunteers from the public and private sectors to design the curriculum and formulate the desired outcomes. "I think it is one of the most well-developed, outcome-oriented, capacity-building initiatives that I have seen ," she says.
The application process is competitive. Recruitment is through community groups, schools and existing parent organizations. Many, but not all, of the parents who are chosen are already parent leaders at some level. "We look for a mix of some parents who have already demonstrated the desire to be on the forefront of children's issues and some parents who have potential but need encouragement, skill-building, the support of other parents," says Ms. Tinney.
Tequell Fernandez, a mother of three children aged 18 months to 7 years, says, "Everyone has their own idea of what they want to do." She was recruited through New Haven adult education, where she is working on her high school equivalency diploma.
Denise Smith has four children, aged 5 to 18, and has been active in her neighborhood and schools. "You get to meet different people from different communities," through the institute, she says, "but we are all coming together as a whole. What I hope to get out of this is to learn about all the different resources that are available. You learn to be a better speaker and a better writer so you can promote your own project."
At a recent class the students were learning about laws. A trainer, Sharon Williams, wrote several questions on the flip chart: What are examples of children's laws? Do laws protect children? What do laws give children, families and communities? Are these laws congruent or conflicting? How does one access law on the Federal, state or local level?
Then Ms. Williams gave the group 10 minutes to note in their journals examples of laws that help children, families, neighborhoods, schools, civil rights and public safety.
The students were full of questions. "You know more than you think you do," she responded, and called time after exactly 10 minutes. She asked how the exercise made them feel.
"Stupid," said one.
"Not prepared," said another.
"I didn't know the laws I need to know," said a third.
"Sometimes when you are breaking a law, you don't even know you're breaking it," said a fourth.
Some examples they came up with were Megan's Law (requiring community notification of the presence of convicted sexual offenders); laws against driving while intoxicated; probate laws to protect children's property rights, and truancy laws.
Michelle McDonald graduated with the fourth institute class in Hartford last spring. Previously, she earned a college degree, got married, had two children, got divorced, got a job that paid very little, was laid off and went on welfare.
"Then I discovered P.L.T.I.," she says. "I got the skills I needed to build a better future for myself and my kids. I was involved with my community before, but I know better now how to advocate for my children and for whatever my community may need. I am more involved with the policy issues."
After graduating from the program, she became a trainer for it, and eventually hopes to go to law school next year. She is applying to Yale and to the University of Connecticut.

Mary Rosario shares lunch with Maurice Edwards, center, and Rick Polanco. (Photographs by Gale Zucker for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Wall Street? Right Over There Through the Trees

BYLINE: By JAMES LOMUSCIO 

 BODY:
A COUPLE of hundred feet down the road from Orem's Diner, a Wilton mainstay since 1921, coffee, sugar and cocoa are changing hands. So are grain and pork bellies. In fact, since January, traders at the Louis Dreyfus Holding Company Inc., have been running a mini agricultural exchange out of an inconspicuous office building on Route 7.
It's a long way from the World Trade Center, not to mention South Wacker Drive and the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, and "the trading that goes on is pretty low key," says Bruce Radley, a Louis Dreyfus vice president. Still, what transpires in the laid back Wilton office, he says, can flood the floors of major commodities exchanges with yelling and frenetic hand signals. 
 In fact, since the early 1990's, hundreds of securities and commodities trading companies, asset management firms and research and financial services companies -- most of them boutiques with 10 to 150 employees -- have left Wall Street for Fairfield County.
Tucked away on Mason Street, a relatively quiet row in Greenwich's tony downtown, is an 8,000-square-foot, state-of-the-art trading floor owned by Weeden & Company, a member of the New York Stock Exchange, that relocated its 52 employees from Manhattan to Greenwich three years ago.
"We got some benefits in tax reductions, but that wasn't the motivating factor," says Donald E. Weeden, president. "The real reason was that most of our people lived in the suburbs, and they like the suburban life."
"It can get just as hectic here as it did in New York,"says Mr. Weeden, who lives in Danbury. But once his workers leave the office, says Mr. Weeden, "it's more relaxing because you're looking out on to a much quieter, greener landscape." And traders can now be home in time for their children's recitals, school plays and Little League games.
At Nyala Farms in Westport, acres of rolling, farm fields surround the stone foundation of an old farm building. Look again. Nestled behind the former farm are low-slung modern office buildings, a nexus for financial service companies -- A.I.G. (American International Group) Financial Products, Phibro Energy Inc. and Smith Barney's northeast regional headquarters. A division of Salomon Brothers, Phibro Energy has a two-level trading floor on site.
"We can accommodate financial services companies requiring significant power," says John Sheehan, principal of Delmhorst & Sheehan Inc., a New York City real estate advisory firm representing Nyala Farms. Fiber optic cable is backed up by generators to provide trading floors uninterrupted power.
From Greenwich to Westport and in unassuming buildings that seem to blend with the landscape, the newcomers include also include Nat West, Gabelli & Company and Price Waterhouse. According to the most recent statistics from the Fairfield County Information Exchange, 186 commodities and securities trading companies moved to Connecticut between 1990 and 1994, and similar growth is believed to have continued into this year.
"A lot of these people lived here already, and they didn't want to commute anymore," said Janine Johnson, director of the Fairfield County Information Exchange. "And the technology has made it so that you can function in the financial services sector from virtually anywhere. So people are moving their jobs closer to their homes."
"Many other clusters are growing here like high technology, publishing and health care," she added, "though financial services is out in the lead in terms of the number of jobs."
And that lead will be cinched this fall, says Ms. Johnson, as SBC Warburg, a division of the Swiss Bank Corporation, is scheduled to relocate an estimated 2,000 employees from New York City into a newly constructed, multibuilding complex in downtown Stamford. According to company officials, one building will house a two-story, 60,000-square-foot trading floor that will accommodate 600 traders. It will be the largest trading floor in Connecticut.
While the Swiss Bank Corporation was offered many tax incentives to move, that was not the case with the smaller financial service companies, she said.
"This trend was happening long before the state was offering any kind of assistance," she said.
Mr. Sheehan, for one, cautioned that Fairfield County not become overly dependent upon the financial services industry, and suggested that government officials continue to woo other businesses to the region.
"One of the key ingredients over time is diversity," he said, "and it's in Fairfield County's interest to maintain that diversity; not become too geared to financial services."
Andrew R. Kaplan, senior vice president and general counsel for the A.I.G. Trading Group in Greenwich, said the ability to tap into a good work force and lower personal income taxes were among the reasons for his company's move from New Jersey three years ago.
"It used to be that if you were in financial services the customer expected you to be in Manhattan," said Mr. Kaplan. "That's not true anymore."
Thomas Scheuer is legal counsel for Louis Dreyfus, which also has marketed petroleum products and natural gas from an office campus in the woods off Westport Road in Wilton. Mr. Scheuer also credits computer technology with bringing many of these new companies from Wall Street to Main Street.
"No one is picking up bags of grain here, and no one is picking up bags of grain in Manhattan," said Mr. Scheuer. "Look at Warren Buffet. He's in Nebraska, not on Wall Street, and he's made more money than any of us. With computers we could be in Kansas City or Minneapolis."
The influx has been a boon for the Ashforth Company, a family-owned Stamford-based developer and real estate brokerage agency whose A.P. Construction division has built more than 10 trading floors along the Westport-Greenwich corridor.
The most recent addition is Creditanstalt, the second-largest Austrian bank, which is scheduled to move to Greenwich from New York City in the fall. The bank's corporate finance division will occupy two floors totaling 46,000 square feet. A.P. Construction has renovated one entire level of the building into a trading floor.
"Trading floors are starting to stress the boundaries of what Connecticut is used to putting in," said Andrew B. Ashforth, president of A.P. Construction. "In general, it takes more service."
Speed must be matched with caution, he said. "We've spent a lot of time just working on lighting so they don't get migraines looking at the computer screens," he explained.
One of the biggest concerns for companies relocating to Fairfield County are power outages and brownouts -- problems with the Connecticut Light and Power Company.
"They're so worried about C.L.&P. that we have redundancy, generators backing up generators," said Mr. Ashforth. "You need uninterruptable power sources, because in this business if you're down for 10 minutes, it can cost you $10 million."
Or as Mr. Weeden of Greenwich put it: "If somebody backhoes an underground line, we have a backup, batteries and generators, that take over instantaneously. It's self-sufficiency."
Since many of these trading floors are near residential areas, noise containment is another concern.
Kenneth Tropin, who lives in New Canaan, gave up the Wall Street commute three years ago to start up Graham Capital Management, which has a 1,000-square-foot trading floor staffed 24 hours a day in Stamford.
"I think the technology is a minor element," said Mr. Tropin. "What you have is a number of people who were successful on Wall Street, they live outside New York City, and their family becomes important to them. And part of that equation is simply not commuting."

HEADLINE: Regents Standards Facing Change

BYLINE: By MERRI ROSENBERG 

DATELINE: YONKERS

 BODY:
THIS year's Regents examinations may be fading into a mercifully dim memory for students who took them earlier this month, but concerns about the future of the state exams -- and what they will mean for students and schools -- loom large for educators.
Dr. Richard Mills, the state's Commissioner of Education, and the Board of Regents, have indicated that New York students need to meet more demanding standards to receive a more meaningful high school diploma, one that might prove worthwhile in economic terms. 
 "We want to make our children more competitive in the global economy," said Dr. Mills at the Museum School here last month at a meeting called to discuss the new standards and proposed graduation requirements. "This is for real. Some students tell me they drop out not because it's too challenging but because it's too easy. We need to all lock arms in that endeavor of meeting higher standards, having stronger assessments, higher graduation requirements and more comprehensive teacher training. This is about getting standards right and showing results. We're most interested in results, not telling people how to reach them."
Although the exact form of the new requirements has not been settled, proposals call for increasing the total number of credits a student would have to earn for graduation, increasing the number required in math and science and, in a five-year phased-in program, requiring all students to pass Regents' exams in English, mathematics, global studies, science and United States government and history. The exams would also be more rigorous. The Regents have already voted to eliminate low-level competency tests, which students have been able to substitute for Regents' exams.
Such upgrading is long overdue, some experts say. "We want to encourage kids and schools to teach to a higher level," said Dr. Shirley L. Mow, executive director of the Westchester Education Coalition, a nonprofit organization in White Plains.
For many educators, there are more questions than answers about the proposed changes. Some are worried about students who will not be able to meet the new requirements and about how districts will find money to provide support for such students. Also there is concern over how non-English-speaking students will manage and what will happen if some students need more than four years of high school to graduate. Others are concerned that some students will not be adequately challenged even by revamped Regents' exams, and they also wonder what alternative tests might be approved.
"We don't know what the state graduation requirements will actually be," said Dr. Donald Kusel, Superintendent of Schools in the Tarrytowns. "In a district where you have a diversity of backgrounds of kids, representing a wide economic range, it's a major challenge. The issue is getting kids ready for an all-Regents' high school. We're not sure what the Regents' exams will look like, but whatever it is, we have to make sure we meet the requirements."
Dr. Kusel continued: "We've been working to upgrade the program from the elementary school. For example, we have a new writing program for grades kindergarten through 12. And we're updating the middle school. We want to make sure we're doing our job in terms of providing serious content, study skills, computer skills and writing skills. Most of us agree with the concept that a child must pass Regents, but what do you do with the youngster who fails? And a more powerful question is, What do you do with the kids who fail and just decide they can't do it and want out?"
Parents and teachers who work with special-education students are also uncertain about what impact the Regents' directive will have on those children. "Boces programs will be affected by the standards," said Dr. Vincent Beni, Superintendent of the Board of Cooperative Educational Services of Southern Westchester. "There is an expectation that more kids will be meeting the standards, with an attempt to give those kids an opportunity to have a more significant educational curriculum. There will have to be some recognition of kids with severe disabilities."
For other districts, there are other issues to explore with the Regents. "We're very supportive of the Commissioner's efforts to set clear standards to attain a diploma,"said Peter Clarke, principal of Bronxville High School. "We're still discussing with the State Department of Education increasing the range of exams that are more appropriate to the needs of our students. We want to see a variety of vehicles used." Among them, Mr. Clarke said, could be an Advanced Placement exam or S.A.T. II test in a particular subject, or the International Baccalaureate, the equivalent of the Advanced Placement Exam.
In districts that have already embraced an all-Regents program, the concept appears to be working. "We believe in the Regents' standard," said Dr. Barbara Ferraro, principal of Rye Neck High School. "We've had all-Regents for the last three years, and it works. All children need to participate in a higher program. We're still building. We keep the goal in mind."
In Harrison, where this year's 9th and 10th graders took six academic subjects and next year's 9th-through-11th-graders will do the same, there is a concerted effort to raise standards.
"We think more kids should be enrolled in more courses,"said David Trizano, principal of Harrison High School. "We're ahead of the Regents, but the Commissioner is right in asking us to challenge all students. The Regents' core curriculum is a rich academic curriculum, and it's a better educational program. Your expectations have to be known."

HEADLINE: Commercial Property;
Hotel Projects Flourish in Philadelphia's Downtown

BYLINE: By DAVID J. WALLACE 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA

 BODY:
MORE than 30 proposals for hotel construction or renovation have been made for the downtown business district as this city tries to capitalize on its growing popularity for conventions, corporate meetings and tourism.
Property owners are responding to a shortage of hotel space and a challenge by Mayor Edward G. Rendell, who has said that the city needs 2,000 new rooms to serve its convention center and to compete for a national political convention in 2000. 
 With scarcely any activity in downtown office or residential development, attention has turned to converting obsolete office buildings to hotels. Construction financing is difficult to find, however, causing delays for several developers.
But six hotel projects have been selected for city tax-increment financing, Federal historic-building tax credits or loan guarantees to leverage private investment. Those hotels would add 2,300 new rooms, most of them near the Pennsylvania Convention Center, which opened four years ago on Arch Street between 10th and 12th Streets.
One project near construction after having cobbled together financing from various sources involves the former Pennsylvania Savings Fund Society headquarters, known as the PSFS Building, at 12th and Market Streets. The Rubin Organization, a Philadelphia developer, has formed a joint venture with Loews Hotels, which will operate the 590-room hotel after a complete renovation. The glass-and-granite building, built in 1932, has 625,000 square feet of space on 36 floors and is a block from the convention center.
Financing for the project includes $26 million in partnership equity; a $40 million mortgage, half of which is guaranteed under the Department of Housing and Urban Development's Section 108 program; $16 million in bond revenue generated by a tax set-aside in a special district that includes the hotel property, and a $2.2 million loan from the Philadelphia Industrial Development Corporation.
The property could be open within two years because the existing superstructure is being used, said Carl Dranoff, president of the Rubin Organization's residential division and director of the project. And because the PSFS Building is on the National Register of Historic Places as the first tower built in International Style architecture, it qualifies for tax credits for Loews Hotels.
"The capital markets are not exactly a thundering herd chasing downtown construction," Mr. Dranoff said. "We engineered some special financing to augment the first mortgage and meet everyone's needs."
Hotel occupancy rose to 75 percent last year from 63 percent in 1992 despite a nearly 30 percent increase in rooms, an indicator of long-term growth, according to a report by PKF Consulting.
Two of the largest recently completed hotels are the 1,200-room Marriott Hotel serving the convention center and a 500-room Marriott Hotel at Philadelphia International Airport. Average room rates in Philadelphia rose to $115 a night last year from $88 in 1992, PKF Consulting said. Convention visitors have been joined by travelers to such national events as last summer's baseball All-Star Game, an exhibition of Cezanne paintings and the Presidential summit conference on volunteerism.
Last fall, city officials hired Peter Tyson, president of Horwath Hospitality Consulting in Kennett Square, Pa., to identify hotel proposals that would have the biggest impact without flooding the market with rooms and creating discounting. He said the city was slowly shedding its reputation as "a bargain city in the Northeast." That was the perception in the 1970's and 1980's, when older hotels dominated the city and before the Ritz-Carlton, Four Seasons and other luxury hotels opened here. "The future looks good, but if too many hotels get built in the wrong place at the wrong position in the market, there could be dilution," Mr. Tyson said. "What's really lacking is rooms in the lower mid-market, something in the $80-$100 range. But it's hard to make the economics work with that sort of room rate."
IN addition to the PSFS Building, the five other properties selected for assistance are a 450-room Courtyard by Marriott Hotel at Penn Square East, near City Hall; a 290-room Hawthorn Suites in Chinatown at 11th and Vine Streets; a 370-room Westin Hotel at Broad and Chestnut Streets; a 215-room Marriott addition to its hotel at 12th and Market Streets, and a 350-room Hyatt Hotel at Penn's Landing, at Delaware Avenue and Spruce Street.
The Hyatt would be the only new construction project; the others would renovate existing structures. Several property owners, including Jerry Pantelidis, a developer, were approached by hotel operators looking to create rooms to let. Mr. Pantelidis is renovating the vacant and unsold condominium units in the Barclay, a former hotel at Rittenhouse Square at 18th Street near Locust Street. But after an overture from Miami-based Carnival Hotels and Resorts, he has agreed to designate 200 of the rooms for hotel use. He said large institutional lenders and real estate investment trusts that lost money on past hotel projects were now setting stricter terms for developers.
"They want everything they can possibly get, from completion guarantees that can be bonded or a percentage of the liabilities guaranteed," Mr. Pantelidis said. "Because of the amount of money they are investing, they can make some major demands."
So city and Federal incentives are "basically solving an access-to-capital issue," said William Hankowsky, president of the Philadelphia Industrial Development Corporation. The corporation and the city agreed to offer financial assistance only to hotels that would directly serve the convention center or related tourism.
Mr. Hankowsky said that these hotels would create jobs and generate tax revenue and that their creation would lead to more hotels being built downtown and near the airport without government aid. He said the 3-to-1 ratio of $360 million in hotel development to $110 in total subsidies was sound.
One development site identified by Mr. Hankowsky is a parcel owned by Amtrak adjacent to its rail terminal, 30th Street Station, at the western end of Center City. This month, five developers responded to Amtrak's request for proposals to build a hotel, meeting space, a restaurant and a movie theater on the five-acre site that could be expanded to include construction above the tracks. Amtrak retained Granite Partners, a New York-based consultant, and Prudential Corporate Real Estate Advisors.
"The development climate in Philadelphia is unlike anything I've seen there in a decade," said Gerard Mason, senior managing director of Granite Partners. "The station underwent an $80 million renovation several years ago and it's poised for this new cycle of development. It will be a gateway with the finest intermodal transportation that simply doesn't exist anywhere else. It sits at the center of two interstate highways, commuter and subway lines and inter-city trains."
The station is Amtrak's second-busiest in the Northeast, after New York City, and could be a hub for meetings and day travel once Amtrak begins high-speed service between Boston and Washington in late 1999, said Sally Bellet, Amtrak's vice president for development of the Northeast Corridor. "That station is directly connected to Center City and the airport or the suburbs because it shares the station with subway and regional rail lines," she said. "We hope to pick a developer by the end of June and begin with a lease arrangement for a phased project starting with that first site."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Former Pennsylvania Savings Fund Society headquarters, left, at 12th and Market Streets, is to become a 590-room hotel. Above, Amtrak parcel adjacent to the 30th Street Station may be site for a hotel complex. (Photographs by Steven M. Falk for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: In the Region/Connecticut;
Old-Time Norwalk Diner Adds Cloning to Its Menu

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES 

 BODY:
FOR half a century a 35-seat, no-frills diner called Swanky Frank's has done a thriving business on U.S. Route 1 in Norwalk.
Since its inception as a hot dog stand, through changing ownerships and a few modest enlargements, it has maintained a broad-based clientele reflected by the vehicles in its parking lot -- pickups, Rolls-Royces, 10-wheelers, station wagons, vans and teen-agers' fixer-uppers, to name a few. 
 In the 1950's, business increased markedly when construction of the new Interstate 95 placed Exit 14 directly opposite the diner.
Until now, Swanky Frank's has, like most diners, been one of a kind. But earlier this month, a second Swanky Frank's opened at 44 Stony Hill Road in Bethel.
Robert Manere, who owns the Norwalk diner, and his Bethel partners -- Peter Schattenfield, a financial planner, and Brandon Miller, a former commodities broker who has owned the Tucson Cafe in Greenwich since 1994 -- are seeking five or six more locations in which to open Swanky Frank's clones elsewhere in Connecticut.
Mr. Manere has a 51 percent ownership of the Bethel location with his two partners having 49 percent. The three men will share a minimum of 51 percent of any new Swanky Frank's.
The diner was founded by Frank Christiano in 1947, "right after World War II, when people were taking off on the highways and roadside restaurants began to spring up," Mr. Manere said. He bought it ten years ago from Leonard Conlon, and took a 10-year lease, which has now expired, from Al Renzuella, who has owned the property since 1953.
"We're looking for existing places like this," Mr. Manere said, "a thousand square feet, free-standing -- not in a mall or strip mall, on a well-traveled road in towns like Monroe, Brookfield, New Milford, Clinton, Old Saybrook, Lyme -- where rents are reasonable."
He would not disclose the price he had paid for the business nor his current rent, but he said that it was "low."
Believing that a well-worn appearance is part of the charm, he has not spent major sums on renovations. Six Formica tables have been rubbed clean of their original finish, the floor beneath them does not match the floor in the counter area, and spots where the asphalt tile has worn through only add to the homely cachet.
Bargain-priced as the place and its food may be, Mr. Manere says that that he can make more than $100,000 a year, with enough left for operational costs and repairs. It is not surprising, therefore, that he is trying to replicate the concept, although his landlord has taken issue with his plans.
"I bought the property and the name from Christiano -- it's my name and Manere has no right to use it," Mr. Renzuella said. "I could have franchised 30 years ago, but that's not what I want. He should have contacted me about this. He has no lease now, and I don't care who I rent it to."
He could not, however, recall anything on paper regarding ownership of the name, but Mr. Manere could. "I own the name," he said. "I had it copyrighted."
One of his customers, Pamela Ballard, is among hundreds of first-, second- and third-generation patrons who have been coming to the diner since childhood. "My family took me to Swanky Frank's every Friday night for a fried shrimp dinner that cost $4.95 -- its $8.95 today," she said. "Then we'd go next door to the drive-in movie where the Wiz is now."
The Wiz is one of many stores that have boosted property values on that strip of Route 1, said Michael Greene, Norwalk's City Planner. "It's one of the hottest retail areas in the state," he said. "We are seeing consolidation of parcels similar to the four or five properties that were acquired for the new Home Depot."
But the market value of Swanky Frank's .26-acre Norwalk property is only $200,000, according to the most recent city assessment. "Because of the small amount of land, there isn't a lot you can do with it," said Ken Whitman, the assessor. "The topography goes up sharply in back to a residential neighborhood so it's not easily combined with other parcels."
Swanky Frank's in Bethel seats 45 and takes up 10 percent of the nine-tenths-of-an-acre site, which is shared with seven apartment units in a former motel, a rented cottage and a two-unit house. The entire property has a market value of $455,600, according to the town assessor, Kristina Maddocks. Renovations and equipment cost about $40,000, and the rent for 7 remaining years of the 10-year lease the restaurant took over is $750 a month, according to the property owner, Michael Sproviero.
"In the food industry, you should never pay more than 6 or 7 percent of your gross income on rent," said Steve Matsis, the partners' real estate broker. "You have to estimate what you will be able to do, including take-out -- that's where the money is."
His company, S.A. Matsis Restaurant Associates of Newtown, deals only in restaurant properties. "I've done a lot of research," he said, "and there is a big trend today in getting back to diner-style dining. People are looking for simple, American food. Of course, fine dining is alive and well, but these types of eateries are for families -- especially in the warmer months. We call it impulse dining. These are not destination locations."
Mr. Matsis rates Stony Hill Road as an up and coming area for retail, and Raymond Rubley, Bethel's building official and zoning enforcement officer, agrees. "Exit 8 of Interstate 84 at one end and Exit 9 at the other end lead into Danbury and Newtown," he said. "There are a few pieces of land left, but the major parcels are gone." He noted that the road recently had sewers put in, adding to its attraction.
IN addition to several small businesses and restaurants, including a large stone-fronted establishment called the New Colony Diner, there are four motels on the road: the Stony Hill Inn, Best Western, Seagull Motel and Red Roof, and a proposal for a Microtel motel is under consideration. Bethel's industrial park containing the recently built $70 million Duracell world headquarters, MCI's telecommunications and conferencing center "and other smaller companies provide good business for restaurants," Mr. Rubley said.
Diners were memorialized in "The American Diner," a book of photographs and text by J.S. Gutman and Elliott Kaufman, published in the late 1970's by Harper & Row, at a time when diners were disappearing.
It traces their evolution from turn-of-the-century, horse-drawn lunch wagons that dispensed food at amusements parks, beaches and construction sites. Relatively few diners were converted from actual railroad dining cars, but thousands were manufactured to resemble them. In the 1920's, they reflected Art Deco architecture, in the 30's they mimicked the new streamlined trains, and in later decades they grew into large, elaborately designed restaurants that retained the title of diner for its mass appeal.
Unlike houses, which, from an economic point of view, are usually better owned than rented, restaurants are better rented. "I deal with a major chain that signs only 5-year leases, with options to renew," Mr. Matsis said. "If a trend fades, they have the option of staying or leaving. If they own the property, they are stuck with it and must find another kind of tenant or buyer if a restaurant is no longer profitable. All a restaurant cares about is the bottom line, property ownership is a burden."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Robert Manere, owner of Swanky Frank's in Norwalk, and two partners have opened a second diner in Bethel. (Photographs by George Ruhe for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: PERSPECTIVES;
Reshaping the Rent Law for the Next Six Years

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER 

 BODY:
IN the 19-unit condominium at 21 East 66th Street, there is no dispute between an owner and a clinical psychologist who has lived as a rent-stabilized tenant in an eight-room apartment on the sixth floor for over 18 years. The rent is $2,200 a month. But should it turn out next year at lease-renewal time that the tenant's income exceeded $175,000 a year in 1996 and 1997, the tenant will face a decision: whether to pay much more rent, move out, or possibly buy the apartment.
Walter & Samuels Inc., the real estate company that manages the apartment, puts its free-market rental value at $12,000 a month and its sale value at $1.5 million. 
 In the 10-unit, five-story walk-up at 333 East 52d Street, there definitely is a dispute. The landlord's version is that the tenant arrived in January 1996, agreeing to pay $2,000 a month in rent. Payment stopped two months later. Taken to court, the tenant claimed habitability deficiencies. Repairs were made. Still no rent. The tenant declared bankruptcy two months ago, owing $28,000 in back rent.
The Rent Regulation Reform Act of 1997, adopted by the Legislature on June 19 and promptly signed into law by Gov. George E. Pataki, bears on both these situations.
The law is a modest revision of existing regulations, running six years instead of three. It is far from the full phase-out in a specific period of time initially advocated by State Senator Joseph L. Bruno, the Republican majority leader. It is not even the much slower phase-out through vacancy decontrol proposed by Governor Pataki.
Nevertheless, it may alter the shape of the high end of the Manhattan market. And if its mandate for rent deposits with the court during housing disputes survives a likely constitutional test and is implemented by judges in Housing Court, owners will get relief from a long-standing grievance. Owners have long argued that the Housing Court system is too easily abused by those who willfully seek to avoid paying rent.
During the dispute on East 52d Street, the tenant and his wife paid no rent for over a year, said Dr. Mohammad M. Dastghaib, chief of anesthesiology at St. Clare's Hospital and Health Center, who owns the building. "I found out after the first hearing in Housing Court that I'm the fifth owner this tenant has stiffed," he said.
The condominium at Madison Avenue on East 66th Street shows the law's deregulatory impact on occupied apartments. Previously, the income threshold for deregulation in an occupied apartment was $250,000 a year for two years. That was lowered to $175,000 but the rent threshold -- $2,000 -- was left unchanged. As for vacant apartments, they are decontrolled if their legal vacancy rent is at least $2,000.
A new wrinkle is that if the owner receives a decontrol order for an occupied apartment, the tenant must be offered the apartment at a rent no higher than the market rent. The tenant has 10 days to decide whether to accept or reject it.
Another change concerns succession rights. Once the tenant moves or dies, succession is limited to the spouse, children, stepchildren, parents, stepparents, siblings, grandparents, grandchildren, children-in-law and parents-in-law. Inclusive as the list is, it now excludes aunts, uncles, nieces and nephews. Nontraditional family members also retain potential succession rights.
After one generation of succession, the owner can get a vacancy-rent increase for subsequent successor tenants in the family.
VACANCY rent increases themselves have grown more generous: 20 percent, instead of an amount set annually by the Rent Guidelines Board. For very-low-rent apartments, a special vacancy allowance has been established that could lift the rent for a new tenant by about $100 if the old rent had been under $500.
In a change meaningful to developers, the law makes it easier to obtain demolition permits for buildings that still have a few rent-controlled tenants. If there are three or fewer such tenants, occupying 10 percent or less of the total number of apartments in the building, the owner no longer has to make the all-but-impossible case, to qualify for a demolition permit, that the building is not economically viable. The owner still has to provide comparable alternative housing to vacate the building and start demolition.
Many well-to-do renters will find it harder under the new law to maintain their regulated status. Their incentive to own rather than rent their long-term primary housing may rise. (Apartments in newer buildings that still receive the tax benefits that fostered their construction will generally continue to be regulated for the current or a future tenant, regardless of rent level, as long as the tax abatement remains in effect.)
However, figures developed by the Center for Real Estate and Urban Policy at New York University Law School indicate that there are only about 2,700 regulated tenants in New York City who both pay more than $2,000 a month in rent and have incomes of $175,000 or more. There are apparently none outside Manhattan, or at least too few to be measured by sampling methods. The figures are derived from the housing vacancy survey of the Census Bureau last year.
About 8,900 rent-stabilized tenants earning $175,000 or more pay less than $2,000 a month, the figures show. (The number of rent-controlled apartments in this category were too few to be quantified in the survey.) Many of the high-income rent stabilized tenants will probably never reach a $2,000 rent through guideline increases; about half of them pay less than $1,000.
But once there is a vacancy, any owner with an apartment worth $2,000 will probably find it possible to reach the $2,000 threshold, deregulating the apartment regardless of the former rent. The state law supersedes a City Council law under which vacant apartments after April 14 were to become deregulated only when the last regulated tenant was paying at least $2,000 a month.
There are several ways to reach a $2,000 legal vacancy rent. First, there is the 20 percent vacancy increase authorized by the new law on a two-year lease. Second, there is a bonus if the apartment has had no vacancy increase over the legal regulated rent for eight years. The bonus is six-tenths of 1 percent per year. That means 6 percent for 10 years and 12 percent for 20 years, calculated on the basis of the last regulated rent.
The law also specifically allows owners to raise vacancy rents by one-fortieth of the cost of improvements made to the apartment. These can be substantial in a long-occupied apartment that becomes vacant. Each $10,000 in improvements permits a rent increase of $250 a month.
Thus, a two-bedroom West Side apartment occupied by the same tenant for 20 years and vacated at a rent of $1,200 could reach the $2,000 threshold like this: a $240 (20 percent) vacancy allowance; a $144 bonus (12 percent for 20 years with no vacancy allowance), and a $500 increase for $20,000 in apartment improvements. That gets the new legal rent to $2,084, and deregulates the apartment so that its owner can charge whatever rent the market will bear.
Under the current guideline in effect until Sept. 30, there is also a 9 percent vacancy allowance for rent-stabilized apartments. It becomes zero as of Oct. 1. But until then, a vacated apartment will apparently also qualify for this increase.
The new law requires judges to hold rents in escrow during disputes that have legally interrupted collections, once a judge has granted a tenant two adjournments, or 30 days after the tenant's first appearance in court, whichever is sooner.
For buildings of 12 or fewer apartments, only the amount of rent in dispute needs to be held by the court. The rest would go directly to the owner.
Douglas K. Rosenberg, president of B.P.C. Management Corporation in Brooklyn, said that 10 percent of the 3,000 housing units the firm manages are involved in Housing Court actions each year. In roughly half of them, Mr. Rosenberg said, an escrow system would have speeded the resolution of the cases and limited the loss of rent.
At 184 State Street in downtown Brooklyn, a law student who rented a studio apartment in 1994 for $625 a month "used the court system as a bank -- for free credit," Mr. Rosenberg said, suggesting that the court system forced the owner to advance housing cost-free and try to collect later. After many court dates, hearings, adjournments and stipulations, the tenant was evicted owing $9,000, Mr. Rosenberg said. Legal fees incurred by the owner totaled $2,000. Efforts to collect back rent continue.
The effect of the escrow provision remains to be seen. An effort in 1983 to control legislatively how judges act in housing cases was considered a failure. Time will tell whether the more explicit approach of the new law has a different outcome.

HEADLINE: GOVERNMENT;
Making Social Policy by Mixing the Old and the Young

BYLINE: By BARBARA STEWART 

DATELINE: MEDFORD

 BODY:
Because a residence for older people got involved in the child-care business, Fred Greenley, 86, a retired financial manager, is able to pursue the hobby he prefers to progressive bridge or birding.
On a recent afternoon, he silently opened the door of a darkened room where two toddlers lay sleeping on mats and a baby dozed in a crib. He gazed at them, his eyes softening, his face lighted with quiet delight. 
 "Kids are nice," he said, waiting patiently for them to awaken. "It's so nice to watch them develop. To watch them smile."
Mr. Greenley -- Pop-pop to the children -- regularly drops by the day-care center for children of employees of the Medford Leas Continuing Care Community here in Burlington County. Some 50 children, ranging in age from 6 weeks to 6 years, are cared for on weekdays at Medford Leas, which has 300 employees and 500 residents. The program has inspired some state legislators to try to promote similar efforts at senior residences around the state, as part of a package of bills to alleviate a chronic shortage of affordable day care.
The child-care center gives Medford Leas' workers reasonably priced day care and the chance to visit their youngsters during breaks. The children get extra attention and informal lessons from volunteer senior residents. And the older people have the pleasure of the proximity of children -- as much or as little as they want.
"It's got to be a good thing for so many adults to think you're special and wonderful," said Lois Forrest, executive director, explaining why, in her view, Medford Leas' children are especially well adjusted.
The bill designed to encourage other senior residences to start employee child-care centers was introduced earlier this month by two members of the state Assembly, Loretta Weinberg, a Democrat from Teaneck, and Carol Murphy, a Republican from Morris Plains. It would provide low-interest construction loans and give volunteers training by the Division of Youth and Family Services.
Other bills in the package would increase from 5 to 10 the number of children allowed at small home day-care centers; provide training and grants to schools and churches to open day-care centers, and provide low-interest loans and tax incentives so smaller businesses can help their workers find day care.
Currently, 12,000 parents are on a waiting list for one of the state's 7,400 day-care slots reserved for low-income families; some of the parents register at more than one center. There are no statistics on nonsubsidized day care, but experts say infant day care is especially scarce, no matter what the family income. Welfare recipients going back to work are guaranteed child care for two years after finding a job and currently account for a few thousand additional subsidized slots.
The real pressure is on parents with low-income jobs.
"It forces people who've gotten jobs and gotten off welfare to go back on," said Gail Rosewater, director of the Bergen County Office for Children. "It forces parents to choose unregulated care in places where health and safety standards may not be met, to maybe have to put their infants and toddlers in somebody's house with 30 kids, unregulated."
Senior-living complexes in New Jersey could, in theory, offer day care to tens of thousands of children. The cost of providing child care in an institutional setting isn't high, said Mrs. Forrest, a board member of the New Jersey Association of Nonprofit Homes and Services for the Aged. But most are for-profit institutions and would probably be unwilling to spend the money, said Mrs. Forrest.
Medford Leas, a nonprofit Quaker center with cottages and enclosed walkways set among its wooded grounds, opened its child-care center in the hope of attracting and keeping employees at a time when good ones were scarce. The cost to employee parents is about $13 a week. Neighboring parents, whose children are admitted when there is space, pay twice that.
But what is most striking is not the quantifiable cost of the care but the unquantifiable benefits of mixing children with elderly people.
On a recent morning, outside on the grass and pine needles, children and older residents in wheelchairs did hand and arm exercises together. They grabbed handles on the sides of a big red, blue and yellow parachute, bouncing it up and down. For the elderly people, this physical therapy is brightened by the children, who think it's a game. The children, meanwhile, get time with a few dozen grandparents.
The children become familiar with old age and wheelchairs and disabilities. No Medford Leas graduate would ever embarrass a parent by yelling "Why does that old lady have to walk with a stick?"
And the children add another dimension to residents' lives. Margot Young, 89, a Bronx schoolteacher of 43 years who is now blind, has been outlining in her mind a big project: a puppet theater for the children. She would like to make the hand puppets as she did when she was teaching. The men could make the theater in the wood shop. She has scripts for Pinocchio and many other shows. "It would give me great joy," she said.
Mr. Greenley doesn't say that, exactly. But as the toddlers awaken, the sensible voice he used when talking to adults softens. And he smiles broadly when a small girl, swinging on the doorknob, yells: "That's Pop-pop! Hi, Pop-pop!"

GRAPHIC: Photos: Playing with a parachute is physical therapy for residents and fun for children. Inge Raven, a resident of Medford Leas, teaches music at the child-care center. (Photographs by Frank C. Dougherty for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
Business Loan Program To Aid State's Women

BYLINE: By KAREN DeMASTERS 

 BODY:
Responding to a small downturn in the number of New Jersey women applying for small-business loans, the Federal Small Business Administration started a program last week to expedite applications.
The number of women who propose new businesses and get approval for loans had increased until this year. So far in this Federal fiscal year, which ends Sept. 30, 184 loans have been made in New Jersey to women starting or expanding small businesses through the administration, which guarantees to pay at least 75 percent of the loan to the bank in case of default. In the same period last year 188 loans had been made. 
 Under the new Women's Pre-Qualification Loan Program process, the Small Business Administration will screen loan applications to determine if the business owner qualifies for its guarantee. Then when the applicant goes to a bank for a loan, she is more likely to get approval, according to Harry Menta, a spokesman for the Small Business Administration's New Jersey district office in Newark.
Normally, the financial institution applies to the administration for a loan guarantee on behalf of the applicant. This can often come after a time-consuming process of applying at several banks before finding one willing to go to the Small Business Administration, Mr. Menta said.
To qualify for a loan of up to $250,000, a woman must own a majority of a company and employ fewer than 100 people. KAREN DeMASTERS

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Bethany Neubauer, Nicholas Tobier

 BODY:
Bethany Lynn Neubauer, the daughter of Susan and Perry Neubauer of Cambridge, Mass., was married at her parents' house yesterday to Nicholas Adam Tobier, the son of Nina and Emanuel Tobier of New York. The Rev. David Newton, a nondenominational minister and a friend of the couple, officiated.
Ms. Neubauer, 29, who is keeping her name, is a candidate for a Ph.D. in United States urban history at Columbia University. She graduated from Bryn Mawr College. 
 The bride's father is the director of institutional projects at ADD Inc., architects in Cambridge. Her mother is the head of technical services at the Robbins Library in Arlington, Mass.
Mr. Tobier, also 29, is a sculptor in Cambridge. In September, he is to resume studying for a master's degree in landscape architecture at Harvard University. He graduated from Swarthmore College.
His mother is a freelance medical editor in New York. His father is a professor of economics and urban planning at New York University.
 


LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

INDUSTRY: SIC8712 ARCHITECTURAL SERVICES (71%); SIC2711 NEWSPAPERS: PUBLISHING, OR PUBLISHING & PRINTING (71%); 

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%);

HEADLINE: Alabamians Don't Mind

 BODY:

To the Editor:
"O.K., So How Many Alabamians Does It Take . . . " (Alabama Journal, June 24) is accurate in saying many Alabamians are embarrassed by the how the country sees us. However, my friends and family in Birmingham don't mind being the butt of jokes. They live in one of the fastest growing counties in the United States, with an unemployment rate of 1 percent. Malls and office complexes are springing up on former farmland. My friends worry that the city will turn into a suburban agglomeration like Atlanta. 
 I have concluded that Gov. Fob James, the crazy Alabama political machine and the ABC television affiliate that censored "Ellen" are continuing to commit public relations blunders on purpose. It's a ploy to keep Alabama's treasures a secret from the world.
 
CODY LYON
New York, June 24, 1997

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
In Japan's Rebound, Exporters May Be the Stars

BYLINE: By MARCIA VICKERS 

 BODY:
IT'S advice that is reminiscent of the 1980's: American investors should take a close look at some of Japan's corporate giants, particularly the exporters.
At home in Japan, these companies operate in an economy that is finally looking up after a seven-year recession. In 1996, Japan's real annual economic growth rate was 3.6 percent, compared with 2.4 percent in the United States. And the Nikkei index of 225 issues has gained about 6 percent this year, in contrast to a loss of 6 percent in 1996. 
 For dollar-based investors, the yen makes the exporters look particularly attractive. The dollar trades at around 114 yen, making the Japanese currency worth 43 percent less vis-a-vis the dollar than in April 1995, when the yen hit its postwar high. This means Sony and Canon can sell their compact disk players and copiers at relatively low prices to American consumers.
To be sure, many experts, like Daniel J. Duane, managing director of Prudential Investments, say the yen "bottomed out" against the dollar in April, in the mid-120's. Thus, they say, Japanese exports will only become more expensive in the United States. "The last thing you want," Mr. Duane said, "is a company that makes stuff in Japan in the midst of a rising currency, then sells it in the United States in the weaker currency."
The economic rebound in Japan has also made the exporters' stocks more expensive. Sony's American depository receipts, which are certificates representing the company's shares, have risen from $65.25 to $87.875 so far this year, for example.
But Japanese exporters have many American fans, including Michael Metz, the chief investment strategist at Oppenheimer & Company. "There are still excellent opportunities for U.S. investors in Japanese export stocks," Mr. Metz said. "Though Japan's economy and currency are improving, the exporters are prepared for that, so we remain bullish on them."
He and others point to several advantages of these investments, many of which can be bought in the United States as A.D.R.'s.
If the yen continues to rise against the dollar, for instance, then American investors in Japanese companies can make money even if the stock price stays still. Although the dollar-based business of these exporters will somewhat dampen this currency effect, the yen has gained nearly 10 percent on the dollar since April. At the same time, investors who buy Japanese stocks with dollars today are still getting a lot for their money.
To survive when the yen was trading at 80 or 90 to the dollar two years ago, the companies cut costs sharply -- and their new leanness should help them now.
"Because the yen's strength is already built in, these companies will remain in great shape for U.S. investors as Japan's currency gets stronger," Mr. Metz said.
Finally, he said, despite their recent run-up, the stocks of some Japanese exporters are still inexpensive under two common measures for foreign stocks -- the ratio of stock price to sales per share, and the ratio of stock price to cash flow per share.
Consider Sony, the consumer and industrial electronics giant. Its ratio of price to cash flow is 7.9, versus a median ratio of 13 for the Standard & Poor's 500.
Sony looks good in other ways, too. "The attractive thing about Sony is that it is so strong in digital consumer products, which are the big wave of the future," said David Warren, portfolio manager of the T. Rowe Price Japan fund, which holds Sony shares. Sony's new products include digital video disks and digital camcorders, and it is developing digital televisions.
In its most recent quarter, Sony's earnings were $99.5 million, up from $8.2 million a year earlier. Sales of the Sony Playstation accounted for much of the gain.
Moreover, Sony's revenue in Japan rose 15.3 percent last year -- a sign of the buffer that many Japanese exporters have against loss of foreign sales because of a rising yen. Many Japanese exporters get 30 to 60 percent of revenue from domestic sales.
The Ito-Yokado Company, which owns supermarkets, discount department stores, restaurants and convenience stores, is another favorite exporter among money managers. One of the world's largest retailers, it has a majority stake in Seven-Eleven Japan -- whose stores sell staples like rice balls, Coca-Cola and Pocari Sweat, a Japanese sports drink -- and in the Southland Corporation, the parent of the 7-Eleven chains in the United States and Canada.
True, an increase this spring in Japan's sales tax, from 3 percent to 5 percent, may dampen this company's domestic earnings, and the recent run-up in its A.D.R. price has been particularly sharp. But investors say Ito-Yokado, with its moderately priced goods, is particularly well-positioned.
In North America, Southland has been turned around in the last few years. It has refurbished 5,200 7-Eleven stores and has added gas stations to many of them. "With the remodeling, you saw a major increase in the quality of 7-Eleven's," said David Ishibashi, portfolio manager for Asian equities at Smith Barney. Annual operating earnings at Southland increased 29 percent in 1996.
Back at home, Ito-Yokado is also strong. "Now that Japan's economy is recovering, consumers are purchasing both durable and nondurable goods," Mr. Ishibashi said. "And since they are looking for good value, they are flocking to stores like Ito-Yokado, which is somewhat like Wal-Mart in the United States."
Mr. Warren added that, to counter the opening of Japanese superstores by other companies, Ito-Yokado "is now looking at aggressively opening new stores and that's certainly a positive."
Fuji Photo Film, the second-largest maker of film, after Kodak, is another appealing A.D.R. The company, which also makes cameras, video cassettes and other products, controls 70 percent of the Japanese film market, and its American business is growing. Last year, Fuji bought six Wal-Mart photo-finishing plants and the exclusive rights to supply photographic paper and to process film in Wal-Mart stores -- moves that have increased its share of the American photo-finishing market.
Given such growth, said Andrew Teufel, research analyst at Fisher Investments in Woodside, Calif., "we think Fuji is now on a level playing ground with a lot of the U.S. large-cap consumer product companies," like Kodak and Procter & Gamble.
Fuji is also expanding elsewhere. It has a distribution agreement with 2,200 film-developing stores in China, which will soon surpass Japan as the second-largest film market. And, Mr. Ishibashi said, "Fuji is brilliant in its marketing of throwaway cameras" to the third world, where people "can't afford regular cameras."
Canon, the world leader in sales of color copiers and cameras, is also worth a look, analysts say, despite a run-up of 26 percent this year. Its A.D.R.'s trade at a price-to-cash flow ratio of 11.7, versus the median of 13 for the S.& P. 500. And Canon has multiple sources of profit -- including the innards of its own competitors' machines.
"Even if you buy a Hewlett-Packard laser printer, it uses a Canon engine," Mr. Ishibashi said. "And then, of course, Canon also makes a ton of money on printer and copier supplies to go with its office machines."
Rika Yoshida, an editorial analyst at Morningstar Inc., says Canon is dedicated to new technology. "It's a solid company that spends roughly 6 percent of its revenues on research and development annually," he said. "That's a very large percentage for such a large company." Canon had the second-highest number of American patents awarded last year, after I.B.M.
The recent price gains in these and other Japanese A.D.R.'s do give some analysts pause. "I'm always a little bit leery of being too enthusiastic when something just performed very strongly," Mr. Warren said. That makes a long-term perspective particularly important, he added.
"If one has the patience," he said, "many of Japan's international blue chips are stocks we're happy with."

PUB-DATE: June 29, 1997

SECTION: Section 13; Page 1; Column 1; The City Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 2944 words

HEADLINE: The June of Loren Easton

BYLINE: By ANDREW JACOBS 

 BODY:
THEY were, by some accounts, the class of mediocrity. For Horace Mann School, an institution that routinely sends nearly half its graduates to Ivy League colleges, the class of '97 had stumbled badly. Instead of the usual 8 or 10 admissions to Harvard, the prestigious private school in Riverdale could boast of only 3 this year. Just one student would enroll at Dartmouth, down from five in '96. The number bound for Princeton had dropped to one from three.
They were the kind of numbers that weighed heavily on Loren Easton, the elected valedictorian, who was trying to write a commencement speech that would make sense of his class's middling performance. "All we cared about was getting into good colleges and we didn't even do that well," he said, walking past Horace Mann's expansive playing fields, the steamy June air thick with the scent of newly cut grass. Never mind that statistically, there was little difference between the classes of '96 and '97; 44 percent of his class was admitted to Ivy League schools, nearly the same as the year before. But the class of '97, Loren and other students felt, just didn't shine. "We're not like last year's senior class, which had so many geniuses, so many stars," he said. One '96 graduate of the north Bronx school had got the prestigious Presidential Scholar award, he said. 
 After 16 years at Horace Mann, Loren and many classmates had come to see themselves as the chosen ones, the academic elite, destined to become college presidents, C.E.O.'s, politicians and Pulitzer Prize winners (Horace Mann has 12 recipients among its alumni). Long considered one of the city's most rigorous private academies, the 110-year-old school isn't dubbed "Harvard Mann" without reason. Even its colors, crimson and white, reflect the school's expectations for its 1,600 students, many of whom enrolled as 2-year-olds. There are 10 applicants for each nursery school slot.
As he sat with the class dean, Barbara Berg, trying to hammer out his valedictory, Loren struggled with his topic, mediocrity, which he had come up with during spring break in Cancun.
"I think this is a wonderful opportunity to tackle something that is destructive to so many kids," Dr. Berg told Loren, a lanky 17-year-old, as he sat fidgeting in her office, a baseball cap shading his eyes. Even though she said she was pleased with the first draft of his speech, she thought he could go further, analyzing, perhaps even challenging, tightly held beliefs about how Horace Mann students measure achievement. "Even if you hold the same values, there's no reason you can't reflect on them in your speech," she said. But Loren was resisting anything too provocative, worried about upsetting parents, teachers and administrators of a school he loved. "It's a touchy subject," he said. "And besides, I wouldn't want to sound like a hypocrite."
In many ways, Loren Easton is not unlike thousands of other students who attend New York City's 70 independent schools. Driven, privileged and wise beyond their years, they spend their adolescence concerned with Scholastic Assessment Test scores and grade point averages, hoping to win a coveted spot in a blue-chip university. Theirs is a fiercely competitive world that prizes an Ivy League education above all else.
For some parents, a child's admission to a top-notch college validates the grueling application process imposed by the city's private schools, one that begins in nursery school, when 3-year-olds undergo aptitude tests and interviews and parents write essays about an offspring's precocity. Others see it as a long-awaited payback for years of tutors and college advisers, the four hours of homework a night and the $15,000 and up the best schools charge for tuition.
For others, it is a matter of status.
"Having a kid in Harvard makes great cocktail party chitchat," said Catherine Hausman, a co-author of "The Manhattan Family Guide to Private Schools."
Like New York itself, the city's leading private schools can seem like a Darwinian universe that embraces the richest and the smartest, a crucial first step in separating society's wheat from its chaff. Janet Jacobson, whose 10-year-old son was ejected from an all-boys school last month for problems resulting from attention deficit disorder, put it this way: "These schools mimic the corporate world: the weak and the flawed just don't make it. They feel like they're preparing you for the outside world, but I don't think these rules should apply to children."
As the cost of a private school education has grown -- Horace Mann's tuition doubled during the last decade, to $15,600 -- the pressure to produce Ivy Leaguers, some administrators say, has intensified. "It's tricky," said Horace Mann's head of school, Eileen Mullady. "As much as I hate all this emphasis on college acceptance, parents sometimes demand it."
In the end, students feel the pressure most heavily. All spring, talk of who got into what college fills Horace Mann's cafeteria. In May, the school newspaper publishes a map showing where each graduate plans to enroll. "There is something really weird about kindergartners wearing Harvard sweatshirts," said Ariana Mangual, 18, a student from East Harlem who attended Horace Mann on a scholarship. "I've never seen a place where people are so intent on knowing your grades. It some ways, it defeats the purpose of what an education is all about."
 
Aiming for the Top At an Early Age
 From an early age, Loren Easton knew where he belonged. When he was in sixth grade at Horace Mann, his father tried to persuade him to apply to Stuyvesant, the public high school for top students that he had attended. Stephen Easton, who said he wanted his son to experience a less sheltered, more heterogenous environment, even tried enticing Loren financially. "My dad said he would put four years' worth of tuition into the bank for me if I would switch to Stuyvesant," recalled Loren, a freckle-faced teen-ager who wears his copper hair in bangs. "But I knew no public school could offer what Horace Mann does."
Besides giving him the best education money can buy (students begin studying French and Spanish in first grade), the school has left him with a sense that the world is his oyster. "People say that the first year of Harvard is easier than Horace Mann," said Loren, who will attend the University of Pennsylvania in September.
The school also gave him an extra push when he needed it. Loren began his Horace Mann career with a speech impediment and poor reading skills, but the specialized staff turned an average student into Ivy League material. "Loren may not be brilliant -- can you believe a Jewish mother would say that? -- but Horace Mann has made him incredibly well rounded," said his mother, Anne. "I always tell Loren that the world is run by C students."
The only child born to Stephen and Anne Easton, Loren Scott Easton grew up on the 25th floor of a white brick apartment tower on 82d Street at York Avenue. Summers were spent at sleepaway camp, on trips to Europe or at the family's weekend home in East Hampton. "I always joke to him that in my next life, I want to come back as Loren," said his mother, who, like her husband, is a successful real-estate executive. But despite his comfortable upbringing, her son, she insists, was never overindulged.
Standing in his bedroom while he tinkered with his graduation speech, Mrs. Easton said that as a parent she encouraged independence and self-reliance at an early age. "When he was in first grade, I showed him the emergency button in the elevator, gave him $10 mugger's money, taught him the difference between 'Walk' and 'Don't Walk' and sent him on his way," she said. "I believe that if you fall on your face and pick yourself up, you're ahead of the game. But you can't learn anything if you don't fall."
The daughter of Holocaust survivors, Ms. Easton was born in Germany, grew up in Israel and immigrated to the United States with her parents at 13. The family settled on Pelham Parkway in the Bronx. She graduated from Taft High School and enrolled at Hunter College. When she became pregnant with Loren, she was working as an executive recruiter, and her employer, she said, asked her to conceal her pregnancy. "In those days, you couldn't be a mother and a successful executive," she said.
The son of a lawyer, Stephen Easton grew up on the Upper West Side and graduated from Columbia University. Since 1969, he has run his own commercial real-estate firm, Financial Packaging Companies. He has two children from his previous marriage and admits that his wife was more involved in Loren's upbringing than he.
This has been a particularly trying year for the Easton family. Mr. and Mrs. Easton are divorcing, and Mr. Easton says he plans on returning to his first wife. Although they intended to wait until Loren graduated before telling him they were separating, Ms. Easton broke the news the week her son received his rejection letter from Dartmouth, his first-choice school. "I guess I jumped the gun when I found out there was someone else in the picture," she said.
While Mr. Easton said he would have liked his son to go to Dartmouth, because of "the chic name" and its proximity to good skiing, he said he is content that Loren is going to Penn. Loren seems to have got over his rejection from Dartmouth. "It's not like it crippled me," he said, "but it was the first time anyone ever said no to me."
 
Senior Prom At the Pierre
 As in years past, Horace Mann's graduating class had their prom at the Pierre Hotel. Some students came in taxis; others arrived in stretch limousines, which idled at the curb like a school of beached whales. Inside, students dined and danced in the Pierre's princely ballroom overlooking Fifth Avenue. The girls wore Vera Wang or Nicole Miller dresses they had taken weeks to select. Many boys, like Loren, wore tuxedos that had hung in their closets for months.
"No other school has a prom like this," said Loren, as he escorted his date, a friend since early childhood, up the marble steps to the ballroom. After dessert, a group of 10 teen-agers piled into a rented limousine and were driven to the Eastons' Georgica Pond home for the weekend. Although there were no chaperones, Ms. Easton joked that the kids were so well behaved, they didn't even touch a bottle of vodka that had been left in the freezer.
Like most of his friends, Loren is serious, focused and well mannered. "I'm not a big partier," he said, as he drove his mother's white Jeep Cherokee through Harlem on his way to school. "I'm the kind of guy who has to be in bed by 10 o'clock." Loren, who turned 18 a week ago, will spend the summer as a research intern at Sotheby's. He said he was the only high-school student accepted to the program, after a friend's mother helped him get an interview.
A member of Horace Mann's Young Republican Club, Loren shares the conservative politics of his parents, who support the death penalty and oppose affirmative action. At school, he kept himself busy with extracurricular activities; he played baseball and football, was sports editor of the school newspaper and photo editor of the yearbook, and was in a half-dozen clubs. A tireless booster of Horace Mann, he often gave tours to prospective students.
Alternately gregarious and shy, urbane and naive, Loren has yet to have a serious girlfriend. Friends say he has come out of his shell only recently. "When he was younger, Loren was a bit chubby and had low self-esteem," said his best friend, Scott Alter, who lives at Park Avenue and 90th Street. He won the seven-way race for valedictorian, classmates said, because of his devotion to Horace Mann, his easygoing nature and his ability to move among the school's various cliques. "Loren epitomizes Horace Mann," said Jamie Catalano, a lifelong friend. "He's a real high achiever."
In a full-page congratulatory message they took out in his yearbook, the Eastons toasted a son who they once feared might not make it to graduation. "When you were young, we had our doubts," they wrote. "But as you went on, you proved yourself."
 
Trying to Change School's Stereotype

Perched on a hill overlooking Van Cortland Park, Horace Mann looks and feels like a small college campus. There are seven tennis courts, three playing fields, an impressive gymnasium and buildings with names like Tillinghast Hall, Loeb Library and Van Alstyne Theater. The main entrance is watched over by a crew of cardigan-wearing security guards.
Once solidly white, Jewish and affluent, Horace Mann has been changing in recent years. Members of minority groups, most of whom receive financial aid, now make up 20 percent of the student body. As the first woman to head the school, Eileen Mullady has embarked on significant changes since she was hired two years ago, encouraging the early retirement of some veteran teachers and spearheading construction of a new middle school. She is also trying to chip away at the perception of Horace Mann as an academic pressure cooker and an "Ivy League feeder." "I'd love to change the stereotypes, but reputations seem to have a 10-year life span," she said.
Some administrators were uncomfortable with Loren's valedictory, which they said only reinforced the stereotypes. The director of college counseling, Stephen Singer, said that in the last four or five years, the pressure for Ivy League admissions has only intensified, fueled by what many perceive as an increasingly cutthroat job market. "Parents and kids fall back on brand names," he said, "believing it is their security in an increasingly insecure economy." The president of Horace Mann's board, Michael Hess (class of '58), said he was "taken aback" by the topic of Loren's speech. "I'm at a loss to understand this obsession with Harvard and Yale," said Mr. Hess, a lawyer. "I don't remember the parents being this hysterical when I was at school."
For students, the hysteria peaks during January, around the time of early admissions. "You see kids running through the halls screaming with joy and others crying," said Jason Caldwell, who is going to Swarthmore. "It's shameful." Ariana Mangual agrees, adding that the stress sometimes turns friends into rivals. "Kids see each other as competitors, and they're always looking to see what extracurricular activities you're doing," said Ariana, who is going to Brown this fall. "If someone gets rejected from Harvard, all their friends will hate the person who got in."
Many students become aware of the college application game as freshmen or sophomores and join clubs, some confess, because they think it will beef up their college applications.
"Why do you think we have 65 clubs?" one student asked.
One faculty member said he knew of several juniors who had paid $3,000 to attend a summer program that helps underprivileged American Indians on reservations.
"Believe me, they would have rather been in the Hamptons, but they think these kinds of activities give them an edge," he said.
 
Standing Ovation For Nearly Perfect Speech
 Graduation day arrived and if Loren was feeling nervous about his speech, he wasn't showing it. "I just hope people laugh at my jokes," he said, sitting at his bedroom desk, with its bird's-eye view of the East River and the Triborough Bridge. Spread around his well-kept room, with its matching white furniture, were trophies, board games, a jar of pennies and a mound of baseball caps.
In contrast to Loren's calmness, Stephen Easton was feeling far from serene. "This is an emotional day for me," he said, trying to adjust his son's tie as he pulled away. It had been some time since the two had been together, and Loren seemed distant. Afterward he explained that he is still angry about the divorce, for which he blames his father. "I guess I get a little impatient with him at times," he said, then quickly changed the subject.
An hour later, the family was sitting under an immense blue and white tent pitched on the school's football field, fanning themselves in the 90-degree heat. After the Horace Mann Orchestra played "Pomp and Circumstance" and "America the Beautiful," Dr. Berg stood at the podium and introduced the valedictorian. As a few of his friends cheered and hooted, Loren bounded to the stage, his black tassel waving in front of his tortoise-shell-rimmed glasses.
His delivery was nearly perfect and the crowd chuckled at his well-timed wisecracks. But in the end, he chose to deal with the issue of mediocrity in a few short paragraphs. "Surely who we are is not measured at the ages of 17 and 18 by such an arbitrary thing as college," he told the audience. "The assessment says that you as a person are defined by where you will go to school. Does that mean I am also defined as a person by where I live and the color of my skin? I certainly hope not." He went on to highlight a handful of classmates -- a musician, an actress, a Web page designer -- who had excelled outside of school. "We have followed our passions and succeeded," he said near the end. "And that is what we should be measured by." He received a standing ovation. Several teachers told him it was the best valedictory in years. "Listening to him, I kept thinking, 'Loren should be a politician,' " his mother said.
Afterward, Loren explained why he had avoided more vigorously challenging the commonly held notions of mediocrity. "I thought it would have been inappropriate to shove it down their throats," he said. "A lot of people think that getting into the Ivy Leagues is very important. I guess I think that way too."
