HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
New Jersey Is Betting The Future On Wall St.

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
In the summer of 1982, with the stock market 22 percent below where it had been in 1966, stocks were understandably unpopular. In the 15 years since, the Dow Jones industrial average has risen 895 percent. Now, stocks are so popular that a state government has opened a margin account.
New Jersey last week sold $2.8 billion in state bonds -- the largest single long-term municipal financing in history. The money will not go to build roads, schools or even sports stadiums. It will buy stocks. 
 And, Gov. Christine Todd Whitman is sure, those stocks will go up so fast that the state will come out ahead.
To be fair, this is not really a margin account. In such an account the loan is secured by the stocks themselves and by the borrower's credit. This borrowing is not secured by the stocks. Nor has New Jersey pledged any revenues or even its general obligation.
This borrowing is secured by nothing. It is what is known as an "appropriation bond." That means interest will be paid if, and only if, the state government decides to pay it.
"It does sound a little alarming, initially," conceded Sam Corliss, the chief muni bond official at Merrill Lynch, which was the lead underwriter for the bonds. But he quickly added that investors should have no fear. The bonds are insured by MBIA, Ambac and Financial Security Assurance, which share $27 million in premiums. They will have to pay if the state doesn't.
New Jersey's new bonds are not exempt from Federal income taxes, so it is paying rates as high as 7.6 percent, about two-thirds of a percentage point higher than Treasuries.
This deal reflects the state of both public finance and the financial markets in 1997. The state needed to do something to shore up an underfinanced state pension plan, but it could have borrowed far less had it not decided that it would also forgo making its regular contribution to the fund this year and next. That alone added $590 million to the borrowing, and freed up the same amount to be spent on other things.
The financial market story is familiar. What once would have seemed rank speculation now seems wise. Insurers see no problem in bonds unsecured by anything, and with no recourse if they are not paid. More and more investors believe stocks are a sure thing. A commission thinks the Social Security system should join New Jersey in betting its future on the corner of Wall and Broad Streets.
Tomorrow, New Jersey will get a check for $2.75 billion (what is left after fees) and will put the money into its pension system. That buying power will help push up share prices a bit more, as will purchases by stock mutual funds, which now bring in that much money every four days.
There is no particular sign that this great bull market will end soon. But there is a real question whether it will keep churning out big profits over the life of the New Jersey bonds. A future New Jersey governor may curse the day Governor Whitman found this clever way to balance the state budget.
But perhaps that governor will decide that since the speculation didn't work out, the pain should be shared with those who financed it. After all, the bonds carry only the promise that the Legislature will consider paying them. So the state might decide to meet its obligations by distributing depreciated shares of stock. It could tell the insurers to make up the difference.
Unthinkable? Of course. But that's also what people would have said in 1982 about a state borrowing to take a plunge in stocks.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 10; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
Sober Speculation Amid the Partying About Hong Kong

BYLINE: By JON HILSENRATH 

DATELINE: HONG KONG

 BODY:
THE long-awaited hand-over of Hong Kong to China, now just two days away, will be marked by repeated shows of fireworks and all-night parties on the colony's picturesque mountain peak. But the party has already started for mutual funds investing in Hong Kong and China.
Powered by a bull market in Hong Kong, nearly all of the 10 mutual funds listed in the United States that focus their investments in Hong Kong and China have experienced gains of more than 30 percent in the last 12 months. That places them among the top-performing country funds in the investing world. The stellar performers -- including the open-end Fidelity Hong Kong and China fund and the closed-end Greater China fund -- have had returns of 45 percent or more in this period. 
 Despite the political uncertainties created by the hand-over, Hong Kong has become a safe haven of sorts for many Asian investors, who have been scared out of wobbling markets in Thailand, the Philippines and Korea. Investors have been reassured by China's slowing inflation rate and by the expectation that Chinese Communist Party officials will leave Hong Kong's markets alone.
These markets are an increasingly important source of capital for the mainland Chinese companies known as red chips, which have been the Hong Kong market's top performers in the latest leg of its two-year rally. The stocks of these companies, which are listed in Hong Kong but whose mainland assets are typically controlled by party officials in Chinese cities and provinces, have risen an average of 167 percent in the last 12 months, even though the companies' earnings have been paltry.
Ignoring the income statements, investors have pushed up the red chips on hopes that the companies' mainland connections will let them continue to get good deals on prime Chinese assets.
The question now is whether the party is about to end.
"There is a feeling of nervousness in the market at the moment," said Shane Norman, managing director of RCP & Partners, a consulting firm in Hong Kong that tracks Asian mutual funds.
"A lot of the institutional managers are saying that the market is in the grip of a red-chip fever," he said, adding that they think "the market might very well boil over and consolidate over the short term." Indeed, mainland regulators jolted Hong Kong investors earlier this month when they said they would begin scrutinizing red-chip deals more closely.
China funds, whose holdings tend to be concentrated in Hong Kong, would of course be vulnerable to a downturn in the market, although they are protected somewhat because their stakes in the volatile red chips tend to be limited -- typically constituting less than 30 percent of their total portfolios and often less than 10 percent.
A market decline would be nothing new for China fund managers. Since China began opening its financial markets in the early 1990's, investing in this growth area has been a roller-coaster ride. Many aggressive China funds that are doing well today -- like Jardine Fleming China Region, a closed-end fund whose net asset value has risen around 29 percent so far this year -- tumbled a few years ago when Chinese managers of listed state-owned enterprises did not deliver what they had promised and when Chinese authorities started slowing an overheated economy.
China Region saw its net asset value plummet 47 percent in just one year. "We sat through a blood bath," said Alexander Au, who manages the fund.
More conservative funds, run by the likes of Fidelity, Templeton and Guinness Flight, have been limited by their own large size and caution. Unable because of their size to invest a big portion of their portfolios in China's young, illiquid markets and wary of hybrids like the red chips, they have nested in less-volatile Hong Kong investments -- local property and financial stocks.
Some funds, like Templeton's two closed-end China funds and Guinness Flight's China and Hong Kong fund, have missed much of the most recent surge and have found again that the markets change quickly in this land of radical transitions.
"Yes, we have suffered in the short term," said Edmund Harriss, the manager of the China and Hong Kong fund. "But in the long term, a prudent policy is the more sensible one."
As of June 20, his fund was up 13.1 percent for 1997, a bit less than the 14.6 percent increase in Hong Kong's benchmark Hang Seng index and far behind the 60 percent rise in red-chip shares.
All this may not be too tempting to investors with no appetite for risk and who are making short- or medium-term investments. But even after last year's big run-up, these markets might be ripe for long-term investors to add to the $2.9 billion already placed in the United States-listed China funds.
Mr. Norman of RCP, who saw potential dangers in the short term, is among those who see good possibilities for long-term investing in China. "You'll still see overall better performance in these markets than you will in other parts of the world," he said.
William Tathan, director of Towry Law Investment Advisers in Hong Kong, agrees. "China has to be one of the great growth stories in Asia," Mr. Tathan said. "It may take many years to be successful, but a serious investor would be unwise not having some exposure to China."
CHINA'S economic policy is much more friendly to investors than it was a few years ago. In 1994, with inflation surging to more than 20 percent annually, China began tightening monetary policy, creating a cash shortage that squeezed the funds invested in the mainland's illiquid and volatile B-share market, the market open to foreign investors. But today, inflation has slowed to less than 10 percent, allowing the People's Bank of China to ease monetary policy, which in turn has fueled the market with cash. Fund managers say that will keep the China and Hong Kong stocks buoyant and the area's economies growing vigorously while many Asian markets struggle.
Moreover, Hong Kong's traditional blue-chip stocks -- the local finance and property companies that are so heavily represented in the portfolios of most of the China funds -- are still relatively cheap. Stocks on the Hang Seng index, which is dominated by the blue-chip stocks, are trading at an average of 15 times projected 1997 earnings, below the average of 17 for all of Asia minus Japan, according to Jardine Fleming.
Perhaps most important, fund managers are increasingly confident that China is serious about improving its financial system.
Consider the experience of J. Mark Mobius with Shanghai Tire and Rubber, a state-owned manufacturing company listed in China.
Mr. Mobius, manager of the Templeton Dragon fund and other emerging-markets funds, said that when he visited Shanghai Tire three years ago, it was overstaffed and its offices were in disarray. "Then, when you went in and asked questions about the company and what its plans were, they said, 'It is secret, why do you want to know this?' " he recalled.
But three months ago, he returned with plans to invest, and everything seemed to have changed. "The office was clean and orderly," he said. "People were busy at work. And management ushered us into the conference room and answered questions."
He added: "I think the Government told these guys there is no free lunch -- you are not going to get funding without getting results. They realized they needed capital to grow."
INVESTORS who are ready to board the China fund roller coaster might find some lessons in the recent past. Perhaps most important, they should realize that China funds come in significantly different shapes and sizes.
The larger funds, like Templeton Dragon, Guinness Flight China and Hong Kong and Fidelity Hong Kong and China, have their assets concentrated in Hong Kong. They tend to be less exposed to China's market -- and also have missed much of the recent rally tied to red chips. Smaller funds, like China Fund Inc. and Jardine Fleming's China Region fund, are more willing and able to put a higher percentage of their portfolios in the mainland. They might have good years, like 1997, but they can also have very bad ones, like 1994.
Investors should also be aware there are differences between closed-end and open-end funds that are particularly significant in this market. It might make good sense to invest in closed-end funds, which have a fixed number of shares and trade on exchanges, rather than in open-end funds, said William Whitt, an analyst at Morningstar Inc., the Chicago-based fund trackers. Closed-end funds are now trading at unusually high discounts to their net asset values, meaning that the market is placing less value on the funds than it is in the stocks the funds own. Investing in such a fund could offer investors a chance to get into these markets cheaply.
Mr. Whitt particularly favors the Greater China fund, which is trading at around a 24 percent discount to its net asset value, compared with a five-year average discount of 4.5 percent. He sees it as a good middle-ground fund -- somewhat aggressive but not taking wild chances in red chips.
Closed-end funds are also easier than open-end funds for managers to handle, Mr. Whitt said. When markets turn down, as they did in China a couple of years ago, open-end funds often suffer from redemptions by skittish investors. Sudden mass redemptions can force managers to sell their stocks, which can be difficult in illiquid markets. But a closed-end fund does not redeem shares; they trade on stock exchanges. So when investors sell, fund managers are not forced to sell shares of companies in their portfolios.
No matter what choices investors make, they should make their moves carefully, advisers say. "Invest some money now, and invest the remaining two-thirds over the next six months," said Towry's Mr. Tathan. "Some degree of prudence is required."
Patience and a seat belt seem to be in order, too.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 3418 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Why Some Auditors Like the Taste of Leftovers

BYLINE: By REED ABELSON 

 BODY:
WANTED: auditor. Technology company, former target of class-action lawsuit, under investigation by the Securities and Exchange Commission, has lost two Big Six accounting firms within the last year. Needs to resolve questions over its financial statements. Quickly.
When Pinnacle Micro Inc. announced in February 1996 that Coopers & Lybrand had quit as its auditor less than a year after Ernst & Young had resigned, its stock plummeted nearly 40 percent. Even so, Pinnacle's former chief executive, William F. Blum, assured investors that management was "confident" about the future and would find a new auditor shortly. 
 His assurances struck some listeners as wishful thinking. Auditors rarely walk away from their bread and butter, and resignations are often viewed by other auditors as a sign that something may be amiss at a company, anything from funny bookkeeping to personality clashes to questions about management's integrity. "It certainly raises enormous red flags," said Peter Frank, a vice chairman at Price Waterhouse. And after years of being embroiled in ugly lawsuits accusing them of failing to detect clients' accounting irregularities or securities frauds, the best-known and largest accounting firms are loath to take on companies that show the slightest hint of risk.
But despite being bounced by two of the nation's best-known accounting firms in relatively short order, Pinnacle Micro, a manufacturer of optical disk drives based in Irvine, Calif., was able to hire another auditor the next month: BDO Seidman, the ninth-largest accounting firm in the country.
Although the Big Six firms each have at least four times the number of partners as BDO's 223, BDO has emerged as the national firm that has inherited the greatest number of castoffs.
Competition in accounting is intense, and everyone takes a share of the rejects. "You have to grow this business," explained Patrick J. McDonnell, the vice chairman of business assurance for Coopers & Lybrand. "If you don't take leftovers from other firms, where are you going to get clients?"
The firm with the most leftovers on its plate, however, is BDO. From January 1994 through earlier this month, BDO was hired by companies representing 43 of the 546 resignations tracked by Public Accounting Report, an industry newsletter in Atlanta. Those 43 represent more than 15 percent, or nearly one out of six, of BDO's publicly held audit clients. In contrast, fewer than 2 percent of the Big Six's publicly traded clients are recent castoffs, and the Big Six have shucked more clients recently than they have added in castoffs.
"We pick up some; others pick up some," said Daniel L. Pavelich, BDO's chairman. "That's what happens."
Even after adjusting BDO's additions to reflect the 10 publicly traded clients it jettisoned over the same period, BDO still showed a net gain of 33 such clients, the highest of the firms studied by Public Accounting Report. All told, the firm now audits 279 public companies, roughly twice the number six years ago, the fastest rate of growth of any of the nine national firms.
BDO, which audits such well-known public companies as Barnes & Noble and Forest Laboratories of New York, plays down the disparities in the study as a statistical fluke. It says that the 43 companies it took on represent a meager 0.3 percent of the firm's 15,000 public and private clients and a modest fraction of its revenues. (Like all the accounting firms, BDO would not comment on specific clients or on the fees they generate.)
Besides, the firm contends that it and its rivals in what is known as the accounting industry's second tier are a natural choice for many small and mid-sized businesses because they can make a profit on them when the Big Six firms can't. And they beat the mom-and-pop firms on expertise. Edward E. Nusbaum, national director of assurance services for Grant Thornton, the seventh-largest firm, concurred. Many Big Six accountants "will not openly admit it, but if a company does not have the fees or growth or size, they just don't want to be bothered," he said. Since 1994, his firm has accepted 20 audit clients whose previous accounting firm had quit, for a net gain of 11 after deducting the 9 times that Grant Thornton broke things off with clients.
WHETHER to take on the auditing of a company abandoned by another firm is a puzzle faced by every firm in the accounting business. No firm wants to risk its reputation by linking up with a company whose bookkeeping practices could lead to scandal or the courthouse. By the same token, no firm lightly turns down an auditing contract that can easily turn into an annuity and may even bring lucrative consulting work in tow. A result of these conflicting pulls, at BDO and elsewhere, is a lot of hand-wringing and debate about where to draw the line.
It is not always an easy call. Despite tougher disclosure rules and a professional requirement that new auditors talk to their predecessors about the circumstances of the rupture, the extent of the departing firm's discomfort is often unclear to its would-be successor. Tougher still, if a company in crisis promises to purge the management team that caused all the trouble, why should a new accounting firm care about shenanigans that transpired before it came aboard?
BDO is by no means the only firm knocking on the door. Often, there is plenty of competition for the business. And while BDO takes a higher proportion of castoffs than its size may suggest, none of those 43 recent engagements has yet resulted in major litigation of any kind.
The smallest of the national firms, BDO recently moved from New York to Chicago. It was founded in 1910 by two brothers as Seidman & Seidman. A member of the Seidman family ran the firm until the mid-1970's, when L. William Seidman, the son of one of the founders, left his job as the firm's managing partner to join the Ford Administration. He went on to become chairman of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation until retiring in 1991.
BDO grew rapidly during the 1980's, though revenue growth has sagged in recent years, industry observers say. Last year, BDO's partners elected a new chairman, Mr. Pavelich, a no-nonsense audit partner from Spokane, Wash., who has been at the firm for more than 25 years.
Mr. Pavelich denies that his firm has been any quicker than its peers in taking on discarded companies, saying BDO subjects all new clients to rigorous acceptance procedures. "We take on engagements that make business sense and do not expose the company to unusual risk," he said.
Sheer controversy and poor financial health, on the other hand, do not seem to automatically disqualify a company from being considered as a client.
Consider Pinnacle Micro. The company had already settled a class-action lawsuit against it. But Coopers' departure prompted further investigation by the S.E.C., which had been looking into the company's previous restatements of its financial results. In resigning, Coopers expressed concern over how Pinnacle had accounted for certain engineering costs.
The bad news wasn't over. The day after BDO signed on, Pinnacle also became the object of a second class-action lawsuit accusing it of improper accounting practices. Pinnacle denies the accusations.
Pinnacle has restated some of its results, addressing Coopers' initial concerns. Now, the S.E.C. investigation is close to being resolved, the company said, but the lawsuit is still active.
The company has earned a clean bill of health from BDO and undergone sweeping management changes. Yet, investors have had little to celebrate: Pinnacle reported nearly $21 million of losses for 1996. Its stock closed on Friday at $1.22 a share, compared with $8 when Coopers left.
Another orphan picked up by BDO was the Chantal Pharmaceutical Corporation, which had hired Coopers & Lybrand as its auditor to dispel questions that arose over the distribution of Chantal's products and the company's exact relationship with its distributor. Coopers never completed its study because it said it could not obtain the information it needed from the distributor "on a timely basis," according to the S.E.C. filing. Nor could the firm and Chantal sort out how to recognize revenue. When Coopers quit last year, it had not rendered an opinion on the matter.
In February 1996, Chantal was hit with a class-action lawsuit accusing it of having inflated its results. It is fighting the suit. (Coopers was also sued over its involvement with Chantal.)
Chantal has now changed how it books its sales, and BDO has signed off on Chantal's most recent statements. But Chantal is still making news. The company, whose stock closed at 98 cents a share on Friday, recently named five new board members, and a group of dissident shareholders recently sought to oust management.
Another BDO client, Nutrition for Life International, lost the services of a Big Six firm, KPMG Peat Marwick, as its auditor in April 1996 after its multilevel-marketing practices came under heightened scrutiny by the S.E.C.
Nutrition for Life's chief executive, David Bertrand, says that when the company found itself without an auditor, it wanted to find a replacement in a matter of days so investors would not have to wait long to find out that it had a new accountant. The company said it approached two other Big Six firms, but only one was interested in the business, and wanted too much time to complete its investigation of the company. BDO "did their due diligence very, very rapidly," Mr. Bertrand said.
BDO denies that it took any shortcuts. Jerry Walsh, a BDO spokesman, said, "BDO Seidman's due diligence on Nutrition for Life was extremely thorough."
BDO was not the only small firm interested enough to move quickly, of course. Nutrition for Life says it also talked to three other firms, including Grant Thornton, and says it selected BDO largely on the basis of its expertise in multilevel marketing. Grant Thornton said it was unable to comment.
The S.E.C. inquiry was dropped, according to Nutrition for Life, but the company faces accusations in class-action lawsuits on both the state and Federal level that it was operating a pyramid scheme. The company, which denies the allegations, agreed to a preliminary settlement of both lawsuits for at least $3 million earlier this year.
NO one is accusing BDO of being careless in its choice of these clients, and the firm says its operating procedures are as strict, and its ethical standards as pristine, as anyone else's in the industry. It eagerly cited two recent cases in which it chose not to bid on business that it says would have brought in $1.6 million in fees.
Even more important, it noted that it is not involved in any major litigation surrounding any of the 43 companies in the Public Accounting Report study whose previous auditor had resigned. "Our litigation record and experience historically makes us among the most prudent and highly skilled technical firms in the business," said Scott M. Univer, BDO's general counsel.
BDO's track record in avoiding lawsuits, in fact, is better than that of many other national firms. During the flurry of litigation over the savings and loan crisis, for example, "they were one firm that didn't get sued," recalled Mr. Seidman, whose experience as a regulator led him to write a book about the savings and loan debacle, "Full Faith and Credit."
Even so, the firm has been sued occasionally over audits for clients other than the 43 tracked by Public Accounting Report.
BDO was hired in 1996 by Presstek Inc., a printing technology concern that is technically not a castoff because it dismissed its previous auditors at Deloitte & Touche after a disagreement over how to tell investors about an S.E.C. investigation into Presstek's stock.
BDO is now being sued by shareholders who say that Presstek overstated its earnings when BDO allowed Presstek to use a faulty way of accounting for the tax benefits from stock options. BDO contends that it should have never been named in the lawsuit and that the lawsuit is not relevant in looking at how it screens clients.
BDO recently settled with shareholders of another client, the New York retailer Leslie Fay Companies, agreeing to pay $8 million out of a $34.7 million settlement over its role in failing to detect accounting fraud at Leslie Fay. BDO maintains that its audits complied with professional standards and that it settled only for economic reasons.
Jennifer Convertibles was another audit client that got a bit messy. As the company's auditor back in fiscal 1993, BDO is facing a class-action lawsuit contending that Jennifer's results that year were suspect. BDO quit the account in September 1994, disclosing its own concerns about the company's accounting at the time. "We don't intend to settle," said Mr. Univer, BDO's general counsel. "We intend to win the lawsuit because the claims are not meritorious."
Certainly, if an auditor can collect enough in fees to devote extra bloodhounds to the job, it is easy to see why some firms would be willing to take their chances.
Arthur Andersen, for example, the nation's largest accounting firm, has accepted 31 discards since 1994, the largest number among the Big Six, according to Public Accounting Report. "Arthur Andersen is often seen as the white knight," especially in sorting out the problems of troubled companies, said Brian McGreevy, an industry analyst for the newsletter.
But it is often able to charge premium prices and tell management to clean house. At Northstar Health Services, a small health care company in Pennsylvania that has been the subject of a flurry of lawsuits and management upheaval, Andersen came in as auditor last year after KPMG quit. KPMG said it believed that the company lacked the necessary internal controls to produce reliable financial statements. Andersen would not comment, but Northstar said Andersen's fee for sorting through the company's accounting was in excess of $400,000 -- five times what Northstar said it paid for the most recent audit.
TO be sure, no fee is high enough to compensate a firm if it has to spend millions of dollars in settlements. Andersen says it employs a staff of about 20 to screen prospective clients' financial condition and internal controls and to investigate management. Of the 31 companies it took on whose previous auditor had resigned, about a third could be considered risky, said James Hooton, an Arthur Andersen partner. The firm says it demanded, and obtained, major changes in most of these new clients' accounting policies or management practices before it would take them on. None of its relationships with the 31 clients have resulted in any major litigation against the firm.
Andersen also pruned 47 clients from its own roster in the same period it was adding 31 orphans, for a net loss of 16.
In BDO's case, the firm says fees from the 43 orphaned clients it took on represent a tiny piece of its business. It, too, says it subjects all prospective clients to a rigorous review that encompasses everyone from the company's underwriter to the source who referred the business, often the company's lawyer or investment banker. The firm even hires investigators to look into the company's management, and there are extensive discussions with the previousauditor if the firm resigned.
BDO says it also pushes occasionally for changes at the top or new accounting practices as a condition of accepting discarded clients. Once changes have been made, it says, subsequent audits are usually straightforward.
What it all adds up to is that while BDO and, to a lesser extent, Grant Thornton have been adopting the orphans, the Big Six firms -- Arthur Andersen, Coopers & Lybrand, Deloitte & Touche, Ernst & Young, KPMG Peat Marwick and Price Waterhouse -- have become more discriminating. They account for the majority of all resignations and are taking fewer castoffs. While they took 23 percent of clients discarded by other firms in 1994, according to Public Accounting Report, they took only 20 percent of the rejects last year. The second-tier firms, by contrast, increased their share of discards from 15 percent in 1994 to 17 percent last year.
"WHAT was the pressure in the '80's?" Mr. McDonnell from Coopers asked about the Big Six attitude back then. "Grow the business. Hours."
What changed their minds? Getting sued, for starters. The Big Six estimated that they spent roughly $1 billion, about a tenth of their total revenues, in legal costs, judgments and settlements in 1993. That does not begin to cover the harm to their reputations. "The top six are so concerned about getting their images tarnished that they are not willing" to take on discarded firms, said Allan M. Rabinowitz, a professor of accounting at Pace University.
Coopers, for example, takes a much tougher stance than it did in the past, Mr. McDonnell contended, and the numbers bear him out. The firm has resigned from 71 engagements since 1994 and picked up only 27 orphans, for a net loss of 44, the largest of any firm studied.. And Coopers seems to be getting pickier. Since last fall, it has accepted only two clients rejected by other auditors. Coopers says it has also changed the way it compensates its partners, to reduce the pressure to bring in new business.
This new attitude has only created opportunities for firms like BDO. As an example of how BDO sometimes rushes in where others fear to tread, consider Spectrum Information Technologies Inc., a once obscure Long Island high-technology company that made big waves in October 1993 by hiring John Sculley, the former chairman of Apple Computer, to take the helm.
Spectrum had dismissed Arthur Andersen after the firm raised concerns over its internal controls, and replaced Andersen with KPMG.
Events at the company soon took a nasty turn, however, and in February 1994, Mr. Sculley resigned, saying that he had been deceived about the company's grave accounting problems and kept in the dark about an S.E.C. investigation. Spectrum responded by accusing Mr. Sculley of breaking his contract.
KPMG lost little time following Mr. Sculley out the door. Spectrum yet again had to hunt for an accountant, telling investors in a news release that it intended to hire another Big Six firm. But this time the going was a lot tougher. Coopers, for instance, said it declined to bid. Spectrum declined to comment.
But BDO went ahead. In May 1994, it took the job -- and since then, Spectrum has worked hard to clean house. A new chief executive and directors arrived in December 1994. Various legal actions were resolved against the company. And Spectrum emerged from bankruptcy earlier this year and engineered a 75-to-1 reverse stock split aimed at getting its stock out of the pennies. On Friday, the stock closed at $3.75 a share.
Still, helping the forsaken is a niche with less and less appeal. For second-tier firms, the quandary is especially difficult given that they do not get to audit their fair share of freshly public companies. Underwriters often insist that the signature on the books be a Big Six firm.
The two other second-tier national firms, Grant Thornton and McGladrey & Pullen, have tax and consulting practices to fall back on. But BDO is more reliant on auditing engagements, a more mature and typically less-profitable business. Just over half of BDO's $211 million in revenues comes from auditing, for example, compared with 44 percent for Grant Thornton and 34 percent for McGladrey.
The other second-tier firms "have successfully shown profit and growth in the last two years," said Jay Nisberg, an industry consultant in Ridgefield, Conn.
MR. PAVELICH, BDO's chairman, acknowledges that he would like to see the firm bring in more consulting projects and reduce its reliance on auditing. In the meantime, the firm says it is quite healthy and has the highest profits-per-partner figure in its history, though that feat was accomplished in part by slashing the number of partners by one-fifth.
And for now, BDO Seidman is the leading auditor for publicly traded companies whose previous auditor has resigned. Mr. Seidman, whose family name is still on the door of the firm, emphasized that as long as BDO's standards of audit are not compromised, there is nothing wrong with its strategy. "Those companies have to have an auditor to continue," he said. Somebody has to do it -- and that somebody appears to be BDO Seidman.

Every accounting firm drops a public client now and then, or picks up a client another auditor has dropped. "Clients With a Past"
Since January 1994, BDO Seidman has picked up 43 publicly traded clients whose previous accounting firms quit. None of the 43 engagements has yet resulted in major litigation against BDO. Some, like these examples, have rather daunting histories.
 
K-V Pharmaceutical
 
FORMER AUDITOR Coopers & Lybrand
RESIGNED May 21, 1996
 
WHAT HAPPENED Coopers said K-V's fiscal 1994 and 1995 reports should no longer be relied upon. The firm expressed concern over how K-V was amortizing certain costs and how it accounted for a transaction in 1996. K-V said its relationship with Coopers had become incompatible.
 
SINCE THEN BDO Seidman did not make K-V change its accounting practices and signed off on its fiscal 1996 financial statements, which showed gains in revenue and profits.
 
Chantal Pharmaceutical
 
FORMER AUDITOR Coopers & Lybrand
RESIGNED Aug. 5, 1996
 
WHAT HAPPENED Coopers and Chantal disagreed over how to account for some reshipped products during fiscal 1996; the company revised its financial statements. Coopers was also asked to examine the company's revenue recognition policy, but could reach no conclusion, and could not complete a report on Chantal's distributor because it said it was not being given necessary information on a timely basis. Chantal is also the subject of a class-action lawsuit filed by stockholders in February 1996, accusing Chantal of overstating its revenues.
 
SINCE THEN Chantal revised how it booked certain revenues, and BDO gave its fiscal 1996 financial statements a clean bill of health. The lawsuit is still pending. Dissident shareholders have tried to take over the company.
 
Nutrition for Life International
 
FORMER AUDITOR KPMG Peat Marwick
RESIGNED April 9, 1996
 
WHAT HAPPENED Without citing accounting disagreements, the firm resigned after Nutrition for Life announced that the Securities and Exchange Commission was looking at its multilevel marketing practices.
 
SINCE THEN The company says the S.E.C. has dropped its investigation. The company has reached preliminary settlements in class-action lawsuits accusing it of operating a pyramid sales scheme, accusations the company denies.
 
Spectrum Information Technologies
 
FORMER AUDITOR KPMG Peat Marwick
RESIGNED Feb. 8, 1994
 
WHAT HAPPENED John Sculley, former chairman of Apple Computer, was hired as chairman in October 1993. Mr. Sculley quit in February 1994, complaining that he had not been informed of questionable accounting methods or an S.E.C. investigation. KPMG resigned shortly afterward, having advised Spectrum that it needed to restate its financial statements.
 
SINCE THEN Spectrum named a new chief executive officer and board of directors in December 1994 and filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection in 1995. It emerged from bankruptcy in March 1997, resolved the major lawsuits against it and underwent a 75-to-1 reverse stock split.
 
Monterey Pasta
 
FORMER AUDITOR Arthur Andersen
RESIGNED Dec. 18, 1995
 
FORMER AUDITOR Deloitte & Touche
RESIGNED Oct. 18, 1996
 
WHAT HAPPENED Andersen and Monterey disagreed about the company's accounting policy in 1995; though they say they resolved their differences, Andersen resigned and Deloitte & Touche took its place in late December. Ten months later, after the company's chief executive and three board members resigned, Deloitte also resigned. Deloitte said it would re-evaluate its 1995 opinion to see whether it needed to refer to any doubts over the company continuing as a going concern.
 
Deloitte also alerted the company to possible violations of Monterey's policy on insider trading by current and former board members. The company found reason to believe one former director might have broken securities laws and a current director might have violated company policy, and turned the matter over to its outside attorneys, who are completing an investigation.
 
SINCE THEN BDO signed off on the company's fiscal 1996 financial statements.
 
Pinnacle Micro
 
FORMER AUDITOR Ernst & Young
RESIGNED March 30, 1995
 
FORMER AUDITOR Coopers & Lybrand
RESIGNED Feb. 20, 1996
 
WHAT HAPPENED In 1994, Ernst & Young discovered possible errors or irregularities in how the company calculated revenue. The firm resigned in 1995 and withdrew its fiscal 1993 opinion because of a lack of independent documentation and because it was unwilling to rely on management's statements. Pinnacle's next auditor, Coopers, resigned in 1996, advising the company that it should restate its financial statements. Pinnacle became the subject of an S.E.C. investigation over its accounting.
 
SINCE THEN Pinnacle brought in new management and restated its financial statements last year. The company says the S.E.C. investigation is close to being resolved. The com-pany became the subject of a securities-related class-action lawsuit, which is pending. Pinnacle's current auditor, BDO, gave its 1996 financial statements a clean bill of health.

HEADLINE: THE NATION: Road Dividers;
The Vehicle for Upward Mobility

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT

 BODY:
OVER the last quarter century, affluent families have become increasingly able to insulate themselves from the rest of society. Nearly four million Americans now live in gated communities. Private schools have become wildly popular. Even the humble sport of walking has changed, as treadmills at chic health spas supplant trails in public parks.
And then there are the Jeeps. 
 As the distance -- physical and financial -- between rich and poor grows, so does the appeal of the sport utility vehicle. Chevrolet Suburbans and Ford Expeditions amply protect their occupants from the outside world while holding the allure of vacations in beautiful and remote places where lesser cars cannot tread.
 
The Two-Taurus Road Hog
 Auto makers, practically dizzy from the profits, are planning a new generation of even bigger road hogs, including a jumbo 19-foot Ford -- two feet longer than an Expedition and roughly twice the weight of a Taurus.
Some environmentalists and energy experts warn that the American auto industry could be cruising for another bruising. They suggest parallels with the early 1970's, when dealers were caught with lots full of huge coupes, sedans and station wagons that nobody wanted when gas prices soared.
But the apparent parallels may be deceptive. While the actors in sport utility vehicle ads wear flannel shirts and hiking boots like a uniform, the people who buy the vehicles, more often wearing suits while driving them to work, are so well-heeled that they may go on buying their automotive leviathans even if gas prices climb again.
Buyers of large sport utility vehicles have a median household income of $85,600. Typical buyers of the Range Rover and Lexus LX450, two of the highest-priced models, have household incomes of $360,000. The average buyer of a Chevrolet Suburban earns slightly more than the average Cadillac buyer.
Higher gas prices have little effect on such buyers. Take, for example, someone who drives 10,000 miles a year and is choosing between a Jeep Grand Cherokee that gets 14 miles to the gallon in the city and a Ford Escort that gets 28 miles to the gallon. Even if gasoline prices rise by $1 a gallon, the extra cost in gasoline is only $357.14 a year, or $29.76 a month.
As Richard C. Porter, a professor of economics at the University of Michigan, explained, "The rich can afford not to plan for price shocks because prices are not going to be shocking."
One reason the oil crises of the 1970's hurt car sales so badly is that the distribution of income was more even then. Even the relatively affluent, who buy a lot of new cars, noticed at least some effect on their pocketbooks then from higher gas prices.
 
Gains at the Top
 But since then, the most affluent households have enjoyed large income gains. Adjusted for inflation, the average annual income of households at the 95th percentile climbed to $183,044 in 1994, the most recent year available, from $126,903 in 1973. During the same period, the median household income fell a few hundred dollars after adjusting for inflation, to $33,178 -- roughly the price tag on a well-equipped Suburban.
To be sure, many buyers of sport utility vehicles are not so affluent. Market research shows that a quarter of all households that buy the hulking GMC Yukon and Chevrolet Tahoe, for instance, earn less than $50,000 a year. Some of them might switch to cars during an energy crunch.
Even the rich might stop buying sport utility vehicles if gasoline ever becomes scarce again. During the first oil crisis in 1973 and 1974, sales of Cadillacs nose-dived when long lines formed at gas stations and buyers worried about how many miles they could drive before filling up again. Cadillac sales quickly rebounded when the lines disappeared, even though prices stayed high.
Any increase in Federal fuel-efficiency standards could also threaten sport utility vehicles, which are classified as light trucks. Current standards are lower for light trucks than for cars, an important reason why sport utility vehicles have grown so big, tall and heavy that they protect their own occupants well while posing a growing danger in collisions with cars, according to the Transportation Department.
Yet the vehicles' height offers another advantage for would-be masters of the universe: owners can literally look down on less affluent car drivers. In catering to prosperous buyers, dealers have a name for the high seating position, a name that appeals to snobbery.
They call it "the command position."

SECTION: Section 4; Page 16; Column 1; Week in Review Desk

HEADLINE: THE WORLD: Stolen Thunder;
China-Basher Bashes Bashing

BYLINE: By RICHARD BERNSTEIN; Richard Bernstein is a book critic of The New York Times. He has covered China for The Times and Time Magazine, and is the author of "From the Center of the Earth: The Search for the Truth About China" (Little Brown, 1982).

 BODY:
THE House of Representatives declined to cancel most-favored-nation trading status for China last week, but that does not mean the debate over the world's biggest country -- which will become a bit bigger when it takes over Hong Kong on Tuesday -- is going to die away.
Indeed, China has come to generate more heated commentary these days than any other foreign policy issue, more than NATO membership, more than the Middle East, more than Cuba or Nafta. 
 This marks a major change. For a quarter century, a bipartisan consensus prevailed on China. The tumultuous debate about the country now indicates that that consensus, which lasted almost uninterrupted from Richard Nixon's trip to China in 1972, has now broken down.
Why? I ought to know the answer to that question if only because, along with Ross H. Munro, I recently wrote a book, "The Coming Conflict With China," that challenged some of the assumptions on which the China consensus had rested.
Mr. Munro and I expressed skepticism about the belief that normal trade and cultural relations with China would make China more democratic and friendlier to the United States. We argued that China after the cold war and the collapse of the common Soviet enemy was looking more and more like an American rival, and very likely the chief American rival of the indefinite future.
That conclusion, I believe, remains valid.
And yet it has been disconcerting to see that as the consensus has broken down, the discourse on China has become radicalized.
A new book with the self-explanatory title "The Background to the Demonization of China," written by a group of Chinese former students in the United States, has been a recent best seller in China. The book seems to illustrate the very xenophobic hypersensitivity that Mr. Munro and I identified as a cause of worry about China. And yet some statements being made these days have made me at least understand the concept announced in the book's title.
When the House minority leader Richard Gephardt, for example, announced that China's export economy was driven by two million slave laborers locked away in prisons, it seemed that the debate was being detached from its factual moorings.
When Mr. Munro and I began conceptualizing our position in the wake of the Taiwan Strait crisis of a year ago (when the United States sent two aircraft carriers to the western Pacific during provocative Chinese military exercises near Taiwan), we believed we were going to nail down one end of an emerging spectrum of views on China.
Now we find ourselves clinging to a kind of middle position -- somewhere between those who advocate full engagement and those who see China, incorrectly in our view, as a new incarnation of the old Soviet expansionist menace that must be "contained" at all cost.
AMONG the statements I've heard in recent radio interviews, for example, was this one: "China doesn't just want to dominate Asia, it has a master plan to dominate the world, doesn't it?" Then there have been the many radio talk show hosts, especially in California, who portray China's interest in leasing a former naval base in Long Beach for its state-owned shipping company as a national security threat.
China's ambition, fueled by powerful nationalist yearnings, is to become the dominant power of Asia. It has engaged in reckless arms proliferation policies, seized islands in the South China Sea, threatened to retake Taiwan with military force and built up a Japan-style trade surplus with the United States using Japan-style protectionism.
But China does not entertain any ambition to dominate the world; nor does it have the resources to entertain such an ambition. On more specific issues, for a country that will export more than $60 billion worth of products to the United States, it is understandable for its shipping company to lease wharf space in California, and activities there will presumably be monitored by American Government agencies. Even on the human rights front, where there is no question that China's record is very bad, the idea that its economy rests on prison labor is a nightmare invented at the radical edge of the human rights movement.
Most of China's exports are made in the special economic zones created in coastal areas; they are not made in its jails.
THE breakdown of the China consensus seems to have several origins, one of them being the emergence of an odd coalition on the American political scene. It consists of leftist human rights advocates, rightist former cold warriors, Christian conservatives upset by China's abortion policies and by the persecution of Christians, and politicians striving to use China to draw attention to themselves in much the same way that Bill Clinton did in his first election campaign, when he criticized the Bush Administration for "coddling the dictators."
But Mr. Clinton's 180-degree turnabout is another element in the breakdown of the China consensus and in the shrill tone of the debate. The change, which was fostered in large part by intense business lobbying supported by China itself, helped create an impression that China needed only to hold firm and promise big profits for American business, and it could roll over the United States on questions that are important to it.
The image of China as a repressive, opportunistic country growing rapidly in both military and economic power and holding to practices and values inimical to those of the United States is not even seriously in dispute. The question is: What to do? Some have enlisted "The Coming Conflict" as support for a policy that would try to change China's behavior by condemnation and punishment alone, but that is not what Mr. Munro and I advocated.
What we did advocate was preparedness toward China and the prospect that its ambitions could swing the Asian balance of power in its favor, especially if it seizes more islands in the South China Sea or takes Taiwan by force.
But we also doubted that China could be bludgeoned into submission on human rights, or trade, or arms proliferation, or any of the other problems the United States has with it, with a policy of punishment and condemnation alone. That is the part of the argument that some of the more hard-line figures in the China debate have neglected even as they have enlisted our book in their cause.
Perhaps our title, chosen not to enlist anyone but to reflect the truth, played a role in that. The lesson here is that a writer cannot control the appropriation of his work, even by people he would want as allies, especially when emotions run strong.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TITLE: COMING CONFLICT WITH CHINA, THE (BOOK)

LENGTH: 1631 words

HEADLINE: THE WORLD: Geography and Independence Matter;
There Is (Was?) No Place Like Hong Kong

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

 BODY:
There does not appear the slightest probability that, under any circumstances, Hong Kong will ever become a place of trade. It is worse than folly to persist in a course begun in error, and which, if continued, must eventually end in disappointment and in national loss and degradation.
-- Robert Montgomery Martin, the British colonial treasurer, reporting home during a visit to Hong Kong in 1844. 
SO perhaps not every Brit who sailed into the glorious cavern of Victoria Harbor after the Opium War immediately grasped the magnificence of what his country had just stolen. Eventually, though, the defenders of the 19th century's biggest drug cartel figured the place out. And even today, at a time when business lives by modem, Hong Kong serves as living proof that in politics, trade and the creation of great cities, geography still matters.
 
Magic
 Though many have tried -- from Singapore to Vancouver to Taipei -- no one has ever managed to replicate the formula that transformed Hong Kong from a steep, humid, scarcely populated island into one of the world's capitals of commerce.
But what exactly is the magic of Hong Kong? Its success, of course, is all about proximity -- to the most complex, the most infuriating and now the fastest-growing emerging market on earth. The source of its dynamism, though, is far more complex. Hong Kong thrived precisely because it was on the edge of, but never mired in, the politics and turmoil of China. That separateness allowed it to create one of the world's most wired, global cities -- a narrow, ridiculously crowded place where Western law, Asian culture, Japanese design, American financing and greed from seven continents all mixed together to make money.
So China celebrates Hong Kong's return not only because it ends a century and a half of humiliation, but because the barren island it lost to gunship diplomacy returns to Chinese control tomorrow as the city of the future. The question is whether the magic can survive the transition.
When Britain's sovereignty ends amid fireworks and network-TV special reports at midnight on June 30 -- noon on the East Coast of the United States -- so does the detachment that long insulated Hong Kong from the political control, rampant corruption and endemic cronyism of modern-day China. But it may take decades to answer the question looming over the ceremonies -- whether as Hong Kong and China converge, the country will come to look more like the island or the island more like the country.
In the sunny study of his official mansion recently, Christopher Patten, the 28th and last Governor of Hong Kong, who will sail into the sunset tomorrow as President Jiang Zemin and his army take control, framed the issue this way: "In 10 years' time, will Hong Kong be more than just the richest city in China?"
"The way China treats Hong Kong," he added, "goes to the heart of what China will be like -- whether it can move from boomtown capitalism to a well-regulated, sophisticated economy that is able to accommodate political change."
Both China and the Hong Kong tycoons who have spent millions preparing the ground for its assumption of control say such comments are arrogant. It is Chinese energy more than British foresight that made Hong Kong, they argue, and that explains why Hong Kong's per capita income, an astounding $27,500 last year, long ago outstripped Britain's. Having battled Mr. Patten's five-year campaign to create democratic institutions, one of the city's pro-Beijing millionaires suggested recently that Mr. Patten's departure tomorrow night will be his greatest contribution to Hong Kong's future.
The markets don't exactly disagree: they hit heights last week so dizzying that the head of the colony's central bank, in his last act as a servant of the Crown, offered a warning to investors against "undue exuberance."
 
An Elusive Formula

The truth, though, is that no one is quite sure what will happen to Hong Kong because no one is quite sure which ingredients made it both Asia's greatest trading center and one of the world's most captivating cities. Maybe it's the majesty of the setting -- the glass set against rock, the winding streets, the cacophonous markets improbably etched into a mountain rising from the South China Sea. Or perhaps the style of the place -- the chic restaurants, the escalators that run at double speed so that no one is late to a deal, the Mao watches (his mechanical hand waves once a second), the impish spirit that gives rise to fast-food restaurants named the Deli Lama.
Or maybe it is the sight of starched investment bankers and dock workers simultaneously yammering into cell phones in English and Cantonese on the lumbering Star Ferry, where a commute across the worlds most fascinating harbor still costs only 13 cents.
Those are all images television viewers can expect to see today and tomorrow. But the formula that has made Hong Kong work -- first as a British outpost in Asia and then as a Chinese city with American and Japanese capital and British eccentricities -- is far more opaque.
The obvious factor is location: Hong Kong is a quick boat and train ride from the biggest labor force and the biggest market on earth, yet it has not had to pay the social costs of sustaining either. But there is more.
British rule of law created an advanced framework for capitalism in Hong Kong before anyone thought that capitalism comes in different flavors -- Asian, Western, global. British law meant the markets were regulated well enough so that investors were willing to take the risk of putting their money into companies. It meant companies were willing to sign contracts, knowing that they could seek enforcement through an independent judiciary where precedent mattered more than who your friends were.
 
Low Taxes, Iron Hand
 Those were only the start. Hong Kong's taxes are among the lowest in the developed world. Until Mr. Patten introduced his democratic reforms, which China now threatens to undo, the territory was ruled with an iron hand that limited domestic politics, eliminating a distraction from making money. (This is an inconvenient fact for those in Washington who argue that democracy is integral to Hong Kong's economic success.)
None of the country's productive resources went toward national defense -- that was Britain's problem. Nor was it spent on a burdensome social security system. That's why Hong Kong's phenomenal wealth -- count the Daimlers parked outside the bars of Wanchai -- is mixed with considerable urban squalor, a jarring contrast that the territory's new, Beijing-appointed leader, Tung Chee-hwa, says is no longer acceptable.
It's no wonder that Milton Friedman, the famed economist, once said that "If you want to see capitalism at work, go to Hong Kong."
There are many imitators, but none has quite pulled off another Hong Kong. A few years ago, Vancouver, another spectacular city on the sea, was a favorite site for jet-setting "astronauts" who insured themselves against China's takeover of Hong Kong by establishing residencies in that safe corner of the British Commonwealth.
The sleepy Canadian outpost benefited from an injection of capital, entrepreneurial spirit and great Cantonese restaurants. But Canada and Confucianism were a marriage of convenience. Astronauts earned their title by parking their families and their brokerage accounts on Vancouver's quiet streets and rocketing back to the fray.
Taiwan, where the British briefly considered building a Hong Kong when the island was still known as Formosa, also aspired to be a great financial center. It succeeded at creating tremendous wealth. But until recently it had no direct trade links to China. That limitation, combined with tremendous trade barriers, deep corruption, huge defense costs and deeply divisive politics, meant Taiwan never made the leap.
 
Singapore: Tough Option
 Hong Kong closest real rival is Singapore, another glittering, onetime British colony, which impresses first-time visitors with its cleanliness, efficiency, spectacular airport and corruption-free traditions. Its location, between Indonesia and Malaysia, takes second place to Hong Kong's.
But while Hong Kong is about risk, Singapore is about order. It is run by control freaks who believe prosperity can proceed quite nicely, thank you, without political freedom -- which is exactly why the Chinese so admire the "Singapore model." In response, American and British officials are telling the Chinese that politics and economics cannot be separated if the aim is a truly vibrant market that investors around the world will trust; they have to know they can wake up in Hong Kong and read about a financial scandal in Jiang Zemin's family, then flip on CNN and see uncensored footage of an uprising in Tibet.
China clearly has its own ideas about Hong Kong's future, and most involve Shanghai. For years now, China has been importing what it likes from Hong Kong -- the stock market, the glitz, the entrepreneurial spirit -- but it has left behind the political freedom, strict regulations and clean courts that Western investors demand. It is unclear whether the ultimate plan is to supplant Hong Kong as the international financial center or simply use Shanghai as a faucet to tap the savings of Chinese in a market that requires less disclosure and runs by Chinese rules.
And what of Hong Kong? Perhaps its tycoons are right: all this handwringing is the preoccupation of a Western press fixated on individual liberties instead of common prosperity. Or perhaps Hong Kong's first newspaper, Friend of China, was closer to the truth in 1842 when it said Hong Kong was destined to "revolutionize, or subvert, the existing state of things in China."

SECTION: Section 4; Page 14; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 533 words

HEADLINE: Red Star Over Hong Kong

 BODY:
Madeleine Albright's datebook nicely captures America's ambivalence about the return of Hong Kong to China at midnight tomorrow. The Secretary of State will attend the transfer ceremony, properly honoring China's historical claim to Hong Kong. She will skip the inaugural session of the new Hong Kong legislature, rightly snubbing an undemocratic body appointed by China's leaders.
There is good reason for Americans to be troubled by the transfer. A relatively free society is being handed over to a Communist dictatorship not quite a decade after the Berlin Wall fell. The inglorious history of Britain's claim to Hong Kong, established in 1842 after British forces overwhelmed China in the Opium War, justifies the transfer. A reasonable 1984 agreement between Britain and China compels it. The accord, known as the Joint Declaration, provides for a high degree of autonomy in Hong Kong until at least 2047, including the maintenance of political and economic freedom. 
 The question of the hour is whether China intends to keep that promise, once succinctly described by Deng Xiaoping, then the Chinese leader, as a commitment to "one country, two systems." The answer is not likely to come quickly or clearly. The relationship between China and Hong Kong is highly intricate, and not easily condensed into the videotape or headlines of a 24-hour news cycle.
Democracy supporters may be harassed by the authorities if they stage demonstrations in the days ahead, but that will not necessarily signal the immediate death of liberty in Hong Kong. Conversely, China's leaders may publicly reaffirm their pledge to live with a largely autonomous Hong Kong, but that will hardly guarantee the survival of an independent judiciary, professional civil service and free press.
In financial terms, Hong Kong has already been largely absorbed by China. Over the last decade the colony's bankers and businessmen invested heavily in China. and the economies of the two societies are now thoroughly interwoven. Self-interest suggests a fair degree of restraint is likely in both communities. But the preoccupation with profits among Hong Kong's civic leaders may also leave the city's political freedoms exposed to erosion. No one knows just how tight China's grip can get before it starts to choke off the entrepreneurial oxygen that has sustained strong growth.
Certainly, China's initial political moves in Hong Kong have been alarming. The new legislature is theoretically temporary, but China has left unclear whether it will eventually allow a freely elected body. Hong Kong's new chief executive, Tung Chee-hwa, also picked by Beijing, is a shipping magnate with a fondness for Singapore's authoritarian political system. China has warned that "subversive" dissent will not be tolerated, leaving itself wide latitude for repression. Last week's decision to garrison 4,000 Chinese troops in Hong Kong was a crude move to intimidate its citizens.
Beijing, by habit and ideology, is quite capable of squashing freedom in Hong Kong. But as China steps onto the world stage, it has an opportunity in Hong Kong to present a different face. Few nations get such a chance to define themselves.

HEADLINE: Doubts From Senators on NATO Plan

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
Twenty Senators have written to President Clinton expressing reservations about the possible expansion of NATO. 
 The letter promised an "intense" debate in the Senate, which must approve the entry of new nations into the alliance by a two-thirds vote. Signed by the chairman of the Foreign Relations Committee, Jesse Helms, a North Carolina Republican, and several prominent Democrats, including Bob Kerrey of Nebraska and Patrick Leahy of Vermont, it asked what military threat the expansion is intended to counter, and whether NATO memberships for fledgling democracies in Central Europe "force them to spend money on arms, when expenditures for the infrastructure critical to economic growth are more pressing?"

HEADLINE: Hong Kong Still Carries Britannia's Indelible Mark

SERIES: WAITING FOR CHINA

BYLINE: By EDWARD A. GARGAN 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, June 28

 BODY:
Over the last two centuries or so, Britain has ruled great chunks of the globe -- an empire, it was said, on which the sun never set.
Now, in a matter of hours, this easternmost shard of that empire will be relinquished, returned to China after 156 years of colonial suzerainty, leaving Britain with little more than a handful of islands, mostly in the Caribbean. Yet, the Hong Kong that returns to China is in many ways recognizable less as a place Chinese than a place indelibly touched by the ways of the West. 
 There are, to be sure, the obvious remainders of colonial rule -- coins with Queen Elizabeth's portrait still jingling in pockets, motorists hewing to the left side of the road, the local yacht club clinging stubbornly to its appellation "Royal," the tea and scones served on fine china at the Mandarin Hotel, and above all the use of English.
Yet, Britain has bequeathed Hong Kong more than visible trinkets of its reign.
Derek Davies, for decades the helmsman of Asia's pre-eminent news magazine, the Far Eastern Economic Review, assayed colonialism's central accomplishment in a speech he delivered not long ago titled, "Two Cheers for Colonialism."
"The British," he asserted, "were the Romans, the builders and administrators, no intellectual nonsense, although certainly they thought they were bringing civilization -- industry, modernity, the Christian religion."
"Harbors, roads, railways and imperial cities," were built, he continued, "in addition to less palpable but more important benefits such as the rule of law, the legal system, parliamentary democracy and a free press."
There are some here who are unimpressed by this heritage, preferring instead to see Hong Kong's entire colonial experience as an affront to Chinese history and civilization. Propounding this view most forcefully in the last year has been Zhou Nan, the senior Chinese official here, who invariably describes the return to Chinese sovereignty as the moment "when the yoke of colonialism" is shrugged off.
Indeed, Britain's resort to war from 1839 to 1842 to force the Chinese to buy opium from the firm of Jardine, Matheson and its wresting of the island of Hong Kong as booty from Beijing hardly presaged much in the way of morality or benevolence.
Official Chinese lamentations over British perfidy neatly overlook the venality and collaboration of a huge measure of Qing dynasty officialdom with the opium trade. Yet the fact remains that force of arms won Hong Kong for the British empire and cemented it, in 1898, with a 99-year lease on adjoining mainland territory.
In many ways, however, Hong Kong was different from other British colonial possessions, whether in the New World, Africa or the Indian subcontinent, virtually all of which are now long independent.
"I think Hong Kong is not a typical colonial situation," said Charles Aylmer, head of the University Library's Chinese Department at Pembroke College, Cambridge. "Rather paradoxically, I think the reason Hong Kong outlasted all the other colonies is because of the really strange situation with the lease."
"It is because everyone knew it was coming to an end that it lasted as long as it has," he said. "If it had been a simple occupied territory, there is no reason it would not have ended in 1949," the year Mao Zedong established the People's Republic of China.
The crude barbarity with which Hong Kong passed into British control was quickly supplanted by the rudiments of an administrative and legal system modeled on Britain's own. Democracy was not in the cards, though, for more than a century.
Still, Britain's last Governor, Christopher Patten, has been buoyantly defending the record of British stewardship. In his final appearance before Hong Kong's legislature, a fully elected body that China will dismiss on Tuesday, Mr. Patten summarized what he believes to be British colonialism's bequest to its last Asian territory.
"I think that Hong Kong is an astonishing Chinese success story," he told the 60-member legislature. "But I think it's a success story with British characteristics. I think that what Britain has helped provide as a result of the accidents of history is a framework within which people, particularly in the last 50 difficult years in this region, a framework within which Chinese men and women -- ordinary Chinese men and women -- have been able to do extraordinary things. I don't think any fair-minded observer would deny any role for British administrators or the rule of law in what has happened. But equally, any British observer, any of my predecessors as Governor, like this Governor, would stand back in awe at what Chinese men and women have managed to do here in Hong Kong given the chance, given the chance to be free in an economic and political sense."
More than any of his 27 predecessors, Mr. Patten in his five-year tenure moved Hong Kong toward a genuine measure of democratic governance and accountability. In 1995, for the first time, Hong Kong's Legislative Council was fully elected, directly and indirectly. Other local councils are all filled by elected members. It is one British legacy that will be nullified.
China will dismantle those institutions of governance on Tuesday. Indeed, the senior member of the new government's incoming Cabinet, Sir Sze-Yuen Chung, said this week that assemblies with appointed members marked "a step forward for democracy."
When the royal yacht Britannia, bearing Prince Charles and Mr. Patten, slips its mooring here on Tuesday, the Chinese flag will already be flying above them. But not all those who came here in the service of colonialism will follow.
Among those bits of empire that will remain are the likes of the stout and ruddy-faded Police Superintendent, Stephen Grinley Evans, who oversees a police team that watches the border for illegal immigrants passing into Hong Kong from China.
"I'm not ashamed to say I'm a bloody dinosaur," he said, laughing. "I came here from Scotland and I've been here since 1977. As long as they'll pay me, I'll stay."
And there is David T. Browning, a senior civil servant in the Customs and Excise Department who has seen the Union Jack lowered twice before on bits of empire.
"I joined the colonial service, partly for adventure and partly because I didn't really fancy staying in England," he said, hoisting a silver tankard of ale to his lips.
"I stayed on in Zambia after independence," he continued. "But I left the New Hebrides after it went independent." The New Hebrides are now Vanuatu. "A friend in London said 'Why don't you go to Hong Kong?' " he said. "Of course, I had in the back of my mind the impermanence of colonial regimes."
Now the last remaining British civil servant of 5,000 in his department, Mr. Browning said that Chinese rule would mean a rapid winnowing of the slightly more than 1,000 British citizens working in the Hong Kong Government.
"There are enough well-qualified, diligent, intelligent people here," Mr. Browning said. "The main thing is they have to maintain stability under the S.A.R." -- the Special Administrative Region, as Hong Kong will be known under Chinese rule. "I don't think it will be very many years before the expats leave," he continued. "Nobody's indispensable."
But for Governor Patten, his major regret, he insists, is not the disappearance of British civil servants -- for the civil service remains -- but the scuttling of democratic governance.
"I think there is a sadness," Mr. Patten said, "that this is the first time in recorded history that decolonization is followed by less democratization, not more."

(John Giannini for The New York Times)(pg. 1); The firing of the Jardine's gun at Causeway Bay is a tradition noted in Noel Coward's "Mad Dogs and Englishmen" -- "In Hong Kong they strike a gong and fire off the noonday gun." (John Giannini for The New York Times)(pg. 14)
 
Chronology: "Two Centuries In Hong Kong"
 
Creating a British Colony
 
1839-42 Opium War: Chinese attempts to curb British opium imports from India push the British merchants from the Chinese mainland to Hong Kong island; fighting breaks out. In 1841, the British raise their flag on the territory.
 
1839 POPULATION: 7,500
 
1842 Treaty of Nanjing cedes Hong Kong to Britain in perpetuity and opens five Chinese ports to British trade.
 
1856-60 Renewed fighting between Britain and China culminates in occupation of Beijing and destruction of the Summer Palace.
 
1860 POPULATION: 94,000
 
1860 Convention of Beijing adds part of the Kowloon peninsula to the colony of Hong Kong, grants foreigners travel rights throughout China, expands treaty ports and legalizes the opium trade.
 
1880 POPULATION: 160,000
 
1892 Sun Yat-sen, later known as the father of the Chinese Republic, graduates from Hong Kong College of Medicine.
 
1898 New Territories, encompassing most of what is now the mainland portion of Hong Kong, and 235 nearby islands are leased by China to Britain for 99 years.
 
1900 POPULATION: 263,000
 
Revolution and War
 
1905 Sun Yat-sen's movement to bring down the Qing Dynasty sets up an office in Hong Kong, which becomes a center for revolutionary activity against the imperial Government.
 
1911 The Qing Dynasty collapses. Sun Yat-sen is sworn in as President of the Republic of China in 1912.
 
1920 POPULATION: 600,000
 
1920 Chinese Communist Party is founded.
 
1925 Sun Yat-sen dies.
 
1928 Chiang Kai-shek becomes President of the Chinese Republic.
 
1931 Japan occupies Chinese province of Manchuria.
 
1937 Full-fledged war begins between China and Japan.
 
1940 POPULATION: 1.6 MILLION
 
1941 Japanese troops seize Hong Kong.
 
1945 Japan surrenders; Britain re-establishes control of the colony.
 
Rise of Communist China
 
1949 Chiang Kai-shek's forces, defeated by Communists, flee to Taiwan. Mao Zedong proclaims the People's Republic of China.
 
1951 After Chinese troops enter the Korean conflict, the United States and the United Nations declare a trade embargo on China. Hong Kong's economy begins shift to manufacturing and finance.
 
1960 POPULATION: 3 MILLION
 
1959-62 Great Leap Forward in China to transform the economy leads to famine; thousands of refugees flee to Hong Kong.
 
1967 Political chaos of the Cultural Revolution in China spills over into Hong Kong, where Communist sympathizers riot.
 
The Road Back
 
1972 China, admitted to the United Nations the year before, signals its interest in re-establishing sovereignty over Hong Kong.
 
1976 Mao dies.
 
1978 Deng Xiaoping comes to power, moving China toward economic reform and greater openness to the outside world.
 
1980 POPULATION: 5.2 MILLION
 
1982 China opens its first Special Economic Zone in Shenzhen, adjacent to Hong Kong. Britain initiates talks on the colony's future.
 
1984 Britain and China agree that all of Hong Kong will revert to Chinese rule. Under the slogan of one country, two systems, China pledges that Hong Kong will remain largely unchanged for 50 years.
 
1989 In Beijing, troops crush democracy demonstrations at Tiananmen Square, raising anxiety in Hong Kong over its fate under Chinese rule.
 
1996 POPULATION: 6.3 MILLION
 
1997 British colonial rule ends at midnight June 30. (pg. 14)
