LENGTH: 1563 words

HEADLINE: Arms Makers See Bonanza In Selling NATO Expansion

BYLINE: By JEFF GERTH and TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
At night, Bruce L. Jackson is president of the U.S. Committee to Expand NATO, giving intimate dinners for Senators and foreign officials. By day, he is director of strategic planning for Lockheed Martin Corporation, the world's biggest weapons maker.
Mr. Jackson says he keeps his two identities separate, but his company and his lobbying group are fighting the same battle. Defense contractors are acting like globe-hopping diplomats to encourage the expansion of NATO, which will create a huge market for their wares. 
 Billions of dollars are at stake in the next global arms bazaar: weapons sales to Central European nations invited to join the North Atlantic Treaty Organization. Admission to the Western fraternity will bring political prestige, but at a price: playing by NATO rules, which require Western weapons and equipment.
"The stakes are high" for arms makers, said Joel L. Johnson, vice president for international affairs at the Aerospace Industries Association, a trade group. "Whoever gets in first will have a lock for the next quarter-century."
The potential market for fighter jets alone is $10 billion, he said. Those jets will require flight simulators, spare parts, electronics and engine improvements. "Then there's transport aircraft, utility helicopters, attack helicopters," Mr. Johnson said -- not to mention military communications systems, computers, radar, radios and the other tools of a modern fighting force.
"Add them together, and we're talking real money," he said."
The first signs of a military spending spree in Central Europe have led the managing director of the International Monetary Fund to raise the issue with the Secretary of the Treasury. While the State Department says it is urging restraint on the potential NATO nations, the Pentagon is enticing them to buy American warplanes.
NATO leaders are to meet in Madrid July 8-9 to vote on expanding the alliance. The Clinton Administration says Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic should be admitted now.
Some NATO members say Romania and Slovenia should join now, too. But the Clinton Administration says Romania's democracy and Slovenia's military do not yet meet the alliance's standards.
After Madrid, the issue moves to the United States Senate, which must approve new member states by a two-thirds vote.
"To make sure the Senate knew this was an important aspect of U.S. security," Mr. Jackson said, his U.S. Committee to Expand NATO recently gave a dinner for a dozen Senators at the private Metropolitan Club, two blocks from the White House.
Over lamb chops and red wine, the Senators heard Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright explain NATO expansion. The guest list included Bernard L. Schwartz, chairman of Loral Space and Communications, a company partly owned by Lockheed Martin. Mr. Schwartz personally donated $601,000 to Democratic politicians for the 1996 election. Lockheed Martin itself gave $2.3 million to Congressional and Presidential candidates in the 1996 election, part of a five-fold increase in defense companies' donations to Democrats from 1992 to 1996.
Lockheed Martin wants its F-16 fighters to replace the old Soviet-made MIG-21's in the hangars of Central Europe. Norman R. Augustine, Lockheed Martin's chief executive, toured Hungary, Poland, the Czech Republic, Romania and Slovenia in April, drumming up business and supporting the largest possible expansion of NATO. In Bucharest, following through on an $82 million radar contract, he supported Romania's entry.
"Norm has an emotional commitment to NATO expansion," Mr. Jackson said.
In May, Bell Helicopter Textron's chairman, Webb Joiner, also promoted Romania's bid as he sealed a $1 billion deal to sell the Romanians marine attack helicopters. In Washington, the company's lobbyist, Dick Smith, said that Bell Helicopter Textron was fighting for Romania's inclusion by "doing everything we can to tell their story to our friends here."
Mircea Geoana, Romanian Ambassador to the United States, said, "The most interested corporations are the defense corporations, because they have a direct interest in the issue."
NATO was founded in 1949, at the dawn of the cold war, to enlarge the United States' power in Western Europe and contain the Soviet Union and its satellites in Central Europe. The end of the Soviet empire created the chance to expand the alliance.
"NATO enlargement is in our national interests," President Clinton said this month at West Point's commencement. "But because it is not without cost and risk, it is appropriate to have an open, full, national discussion before proceeding."
The Administration says the cost of a few nations' joining NATO may reach $35 billion over 10 years, with the United States paying about $2 billion. But the Congressional Budget Office says the cost may reach $125 billion over 15 years, with the United States paying up to $19 billion.
Deputy Secretary of State Strobe Talbott said in an interview that Central European nations would actually spend less on arms once admitted to NATO than if left out, thanks to the collective security offered by the alliance.
"Feeling more secure, they will feel less incentive to build up their armaments to deal with real or perceived insecurities," he said.
But Hungary, the Czech Republic and Poland all project rising military budgets in coming years, though that spending is still a small portion of their economies, according to a Senate Foreign Relations Committee report by Senator Joseph R. Biden Jr., a Delaware Democrat who supports NATO expansion.
Hungary expects that joining the alliance will increase its military spending by about 35 percent. Poland projects a 20 percent rise, though a United States Information Agency survey in Poland found that three-quarters of the people favored spending more money on health and education, not weapons.
Critics of NATO expansion say weapons spending could create political and economic problems in Central Europe.
"It's extraordinarily unwise for these countries to shoulder these costs when they must pay the costs of meeting their social needs," said Jack Matlock, a former United States Ambassador to Moscow.
"These countries are free to buy American arms," he said. "The question is how they pay for it. If the American taxpayer finances them, this would be a direct subsidy to the arms industry. If they pay for them themselves, it could lead to real distortions in these countries' own budgets."
The prospect of soaring arms spending by potential NATO nations, particularly Romania, also worries the International Monetary Fund, whose billions of dollars of loans in the region are conditioned on fiscal restraint, according to the fund's officials.
This month, the fund's managing director, Michel Camdessus, raised the issue of military spending in Central Europe with Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, according to a senior Treasury official.
At the same time, the fund's staff, in its annual review of the United States economy, reminded the Clinton Administration of its past promises to help prevent "excessive military spending" in emerging economies, including Central European nations. The fund's staff urged the United States to handle arms sales "in a way that avoids encouraging unproductive expenditures and heightening security tensions."
A senior Treasury official said the United States shared those concerns and had "urged NATO candidate countries to be cautious about buying big-ticket items."
Still, to entice those same nations to buy American weapons, the Pentagon has laid out a smorgasbord of grants, discount loans and free leases.
The Air Force and the Navy have offered Hungary, the Czech Republic and Poland five-year leases of F-16 and F-18 fighter jets, free of charge. That offer came after the three nations asked the Pentagon about the individual prices and availability of more than $8 billion in jets, according to documents obtained by Arms Trade News.
Eight billion dollars exceeds the three nations' combined annual defense budgets.
Last month, Romania became the first country to tap into a $15 billion defense export loan guarantee fund, created by Congress in 1995 at the urging of executives like Mr. Augustine. The Romanians want the money to buy $23 million in unmanned reconnaissance planes. And this year, Hungary, the Czech Republic and Poland received $30 million from a $60 million Pentagon grant program.
Beyond Congress, the White House and the Pentagon, NATO expansion is a hot issue in the growing subculture of nonprofit organizations, where interest groups form associations with civic-sounding names to advance their causes.
Mr. Jackson set up his U.S. Committee to Expand NATO committee last November. The committee's board of directors includes educators, business executives and lawyers. Its members donate their time; it accepts no corporate donations. One of its founders, Greg Craig, recently became the State Department's director of policy planning.
Now the Romanians, hoping to win NATO membership in the next round of candidates, are helping to set up two nonprofit foundations in Washington to advance their cause, said Ambassador Geoana. Financial support for at least one of the foundations will come from Bell Helicopter, Lockheed Martin and other arms makers, the Ambassador and company officials said.

HEADLINE: Albright Lays Cornerstone for Diplomacy With Vietnam

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: HO CHI MINH CITY, Vietnam, June 28

 BODY:
In an emotional ceremony in the courtyard of the last United States Embassy in South Vietnam, abandoned in April 1975 when the last helicopter took Ambassador Graham Martin from its roof, Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright today laid the first stone for a new American Consulate.
"This will become the symbol and substance of America's first diplomatic presence here in a quarter-century," she said, delicately avoiding direct mention of the war that killed some 58,000 American soldiers and about 3 million Vietnamese soldiers and civilians. 
 She is the first American Secretary of State to come here since the war, which ended when the Communists captured this city, then called Saigon, as the American Ambassador fled. The new consulate, she said, symbolizes "two peoples on a shared journey from tragic conflict to mutual respect."
But for all her efforts to concentrate on the future of American-Vietnamese relations -- meeting American businessmen and charitable organizations that help the Vietnamese -- the war shadowed her visit.
While emphasizing the need for more economic and political reform in Vietnam and talking up American investment, she was always careful to say that "America's highest national priority" remains an accounting for its 1,584 missing in action -- of whom only 48 cases, of men last seen alive, are still being intensively investigated.
While the United States is spending about $3 million a year to aid the Vietnamese, it is spending at least $10 million a year trying to resolve the fate of the last of the missing Americans. Asked whether that proportion was just, Ms. Albright said carefully, "I think one cannot discount the emotional importance of the P.O.W.--M.I.A. issue for the American people."
The need to reach the fullest possible accounting is important, she said, "so the past can be put behind us." It is always difficult to assess where money is well spent, she said, and America needs to spend more overseas anyway -- "but the truth of the matter is that this is a deeply emotional issue for the American people." It is also a deeply political issue for Washington, even as the war itself fades as an active memory for most Americans.
Political opposition held up Ambassador Pete Peterson's confirmation to come here, despite his service as a Navy pilot and his incarceration for more than six years as a prisoner of war in what was called the "Hanoi Hilton."
And political opposition -- "maybe best described as ambivalence," a senior American official said -- nearly held up the Ho Chi Minh City consulate itself. Ms. Albright and Mr. Peterson wanted to lay the foundation stone during her visit here, but bureaucratically, it was necessary to shift State Department funds for that purpose.
Senator Judd Gregg, Republican of New Hampshire and chairman of the Appropriations subcommittee that controls State Department spending, had blocked the shifting of the funds, reflecting the conservative Republican concern that the Vietnamese are not doing enough to help the United States account for its missing Americans .
But Mr. Peterson, a former Representative from Florida, reached Mr. Gregg on the telephone at a fishing camp, American officials said, and demanded, "What are you doing holding us up?" Ms. Albright also lobbied him, and Mr. Gregg relented 10 days ago, allowing today's ceremony to proceed.
"What has really moved me so deeply here," Ms. Albright said later, visiting a project that provides free prosthetic limbs and simple wheelchairs to Vietnamese crippled by land mines or polio, "is that it's so very hard to get away from the past."
"We all, whether American or Vietnamese, are never going to be fully able to put the past behind us," she said. "But I've been deeply moved by the desire of people to look toward the future."
"Symbolically there," she continued, "in the shadow of the embassy we gave so much for, to lay a bric for an American Consulate in Ho Chi Minh City -- where Americans are very welcome," she said, breaking off. "This morning was very symbolic of having the shadow of the past but being prepared to lay a foundation for the future."
The Vietnamese, who have publicly welcomed a stabilizing American role in Asia, seem very happy that the Americans are back. Watching Ms. Albright at the clinic help lift a young Vietnamese girl with polio into her new wheelchair, a Vietnamese Foreign Ministry official said, "We have so many needy here. We're very glad the Americans are back, this time to help."
Mr. Peterson, asked to comment, said softly, "There's incredible psychological and physical pain on both sides. So both peoples must look to the past for lessons, but then carry them on to the future."

HEADLINE: Auto Makers Lobby to Stop New Air Quality Regulations

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, June 27

 BODY:
After five years of worrying that the Clinton Administration might tighten air quality regulations, the auto industry's fears have finally come true.
President Clinton approved more stringent metropolitan air quality standards on Wednesday. A day later, in a more serious blow to the auto industry, the President declared that at an international conference next December in Kyoto, Japan, the United States would favor the adoption of binding limits on so-called greenhouse gases, which may contribute to global warming. 
 Auto industry officials had been fretting about both issues for months, and they have already begun lobbying Congress to block both Administration initiatives. International carbon dioxide limits are an unnecessarily drastic response to a global warming threat that has not yet been proved to exist, said Andrew H. Card Jr., president of the American Automobile Manufacturers Association, a Washington trade group representing Detroit's Big Three.
The metropolitan air quality rules, he said, "are ill-conceived, economically disadvantageous to the country and will put a particular burden on the auto industry and the people of Michigan."
Controls on the emissions of carbon dioxide, one of the greenhouse gases, represent a potentially dire threat to auto makers. Carbon dioxide is produced in large quantities when gasoline is burned in an internal combustion engine. Reducing carbon dioxide emissions means improving fuel economy or reducing the number of cars on the road.
Detroit auto makers in particular are already struggling to meet existing fuel economy standards as they sell ever more Jeeps and other gas-guzzling sport utility vehicles. Tighter standards could severely crimp profits.
Auto industry executives have been downright caustic in recent speeches about the possibility of carbon dioxide limits. "This sort of fuzzy thinking provides the warm illusion of saving the sky, which we're not even sure needs saving, but it won't have any impact at all on global warming," said Robert J. Eaton, the chairman and chief executive of the Chrysler Corporation. "It will just move jobs to those countries which are allowed to spew whatever they want into the air."
President Clinton chose on Thursday not to impose immediate carbon dioxide limits, but auto executives had never expected that he would do so and were not impressed. The main goal of industry lobbying has been to make sure that any carbon dioxide limits negotiated in Kyoto were voluntary, not binding. Detroit executives are convinced that only the United States would actually obey binding limits while other countries would flout them.
Auto makers have tried to market vehicles that can burn natural gas or alcohol or run on electric power from batteries. But demand has been remarkably weak, with Federal, state and local agencies often ignoring laws designed to force them to buy alternative-fuel vehicles.
Ford, the main producer of such vehicles, announced on Friday that it would broaden its lineup to 11 models this autumn, including several that burn ethanol, a type of alcohol. Yet Ford had combined sales of just 8,461 alternative-fuel vehicles across five model lines last year.
Behind the dismay in Detroit about the latest Administration moves lies a very real worry at General Motors, Ford and Chrysler that they will be put at a competitive disadvantage, against their foreign-company rivals. Ford and particularly Chrysler already produce more sport utility vehicles, mini-vans and pickup trucks than cars. But cars tend to be much more fuel-efficient and therefore produce less carbon dioxide.
G.M. will be producing more so-called light trucks than cars within several years, while most foreign auto makers overwhelmingly make cars and will continue doing so.
The metropolitan air quality standards could also hurt Detroit's Big Three. While the Big Three still have many of their factories in big Midwestern cities with pollution problems, Japanese and European auto makers have chosen to build their newer assembly plants in small Southern towns. Though these choices of factory sites were mainly aimed at forestalling unionization, the sites also tend to have clean air that is likely to meet the more stringent standards without any extra spending by the foreign auto makers.

HEADLINE: ON PRO FOOTBALL;
It's True: Even the Bills Could Move

BYLINE: By Thomas George 

 BODY:
Finally, a breakthrough, Ralph Wilson thought. Maybe his Buffalo Bills were a step closer to competing in the National Football League's stadium game -- in which the money generated by new or improved stadiums is used to pay soaring player salaries -- and a step farther away from leaving Buffalo.
Last November, Wilson presented officials of Erie County and New York State with a proposal for renovations to Rich Stadium that called for $60 million in public money. The increased revenue from parking, concessions and new suites and club seats would put Wilson in position, he said, to combat a steady decline in season-ticket sales. He would have the cash for big signing bonuses, necessary in today's N.F.L. for luring big-name free agents and retaining one's own instrumental players. 
 "We didn't hear anything from them until April," Wilson said. "We had three or four meetings after that, but we didn't get anywhere. That's why I was excited when Governor Pataki called last week."
George E. Pataki, New York's Governor, asked for a meeting last Wednesday in Albany between his top aides and Wilson's. Finally, Wilson thought.
There was one problem, though. When the sides sat down, the Bills were stunned to learn that Pataki's group had never seen Wilson's original proposal. Six hours of meetings produced little.
Back to square one.
The 79-year-old Wilson, who has owned the Bills for the last 37 years, is shaken.
"Our proposal is so good, when you look around the country at all the other teams and their stadium deals, that I was very confident," Wilson said. "Now, I'm beginning to weaken a little. I'm tired of all of the haggling. I don't think teams should move if they can make it financially. But if you can't compete financially, you ought to be allowed to move. You can't go bankrupt.
"I have not talked with other cities about moving the Bills. But as this drifts along, you know that is going to happen. This is not a matter of loyalty. I'm loyal to Buffalo and I've spent half of my life in Buffalo. This is not a matter of lining my pockets. It's a matter of simple economics and common sense."
It is a matter in which Buffalo could go the way of Cleveland and Houston and Los Angeles.
The Bills are part of the fabric of Buffalo. Bills fans embrace their team with a fervor that matches that of any city toward its pro sports teams.
But the Bills' 25-year lease at Rich Stadium, which is owned by Erie County, ends in July 1998. That means the Bills are under contract to play in Buffalo for only one more N.F.L. season.
Then what?
Pataki and Wilson are scheduled to meet July 12 in Albany. But for Wilson, the urgency is gone.
"We wanted this thing solved so stadium construction would be completed by the 1998 season," Wilson said. "Now, we've lost the construction timetable. Now, there is no rush."
Buffalo's lawmakers and taxpayers have to ask themselves whether the Bills are worth keeping for $60 million, especially given the harsh economic realities of western New York. Is this just another example of corporate welfare in sports?
Pataki has said that the state has to be "reasonable and prudent," but that it will do what it can to keep the Bills. Dennis Gorski, the Erie County Executive, has said through spokesmen that the county will work "as hard as we can to keep Mr. Wilson financially competitive."
The county wants a new lease of at least 15 years. Wilson wants one for five years, with options to increase the number of years based on revenue. The county owns the stadium. That is not unusual. And what Wilson is asking is not unusual.
When the Rams moved from Anaheim, Calif., to St. Louis, they got a $280 million stadium. When Art Modell took his Browns from Cleveland to Baltimore and made them Ravens, he received $75 million in moving expenses alone. When Al Davis took his Raiders from Los Angeles to Oakland, he gained at least $54 million in cash benefits.
So $60 million might appear to be a bargain. Or it might not, especially for a depressed region like western New York.
"I've already put millions over the years into improving a stadium that I don't even own," Wilson said. "I see cranes and buildings popping up in Denver, Seattle, Dallas, many places other than Buffalo. I understand the economic hardships and that is why, even though we've had playoff teams the last five years, that we have had slumping season-ticket sales. Trying to turn it around is like trying to turn around a battleship. You can do that with a destroyer. But with a battleship, it takes time."
The Oilers, the Ravens, the Redskins, the 49ers, the Seahawks, the Lions and the Buccaneers are among the N.F.L. teams that will move into sparkling new stadiums over the next few seasons, complete with a big increase in new revenues for their owners.
Wilson's other primary businesses are two television stations and a road-building company. He said that in recent seasons, in order to pay $16 million in player bonuses, he had to borrow from his other businesses. No more, he said.
So the answers for Wilson and Buffalo, though painful, are simple. For Wilson, it is giving in and creating an environment for a longer lease. For Buffalo, it is coming up with the cash or watching its team go. It is a matter of risk and reward. Is the reward worth the risk? If it is not for Buffalo, it will be for some other aspiring N.F.L. city.
And then you could add the Bills to the list of N.F.L. franchises you never imagined would bolt -- until poof! they were gone.

HEADLINE: After Tax Bills, Congress Faces The Long Haul

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
The Republican celebrations over tax bills were not finished before the Clinton Administration and Republican leaders began talking confidently about how to work out final legislation to balance the budget in 2002. But that route is easy compared with the effort required to keep the budget balanced and avoid abysmal deficits when baby boomers retire a decade later.
By the time the Senate finished passing its tax bill on Friday, work was under way on the formidable short-term issues of Medicare and capital gains. And officials at the White House and on Capitol Hill were already pondering how to deal with the long-range challenge. 
 The task of turning the two House bills, one on spending and the other on taxes, and the comparable Senate versions into compromises that the President would sign began on Thursday evening. Representative Bill Archer of Texas, who managed the House bill for the first important tax reductions since 1981, talked to Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and the White House chief of staff, Erskine B. Bowles, within minutes of Thursday's House vote.
The Administration likes Mr. Archer's version less than the Senate bill because it gives less to low-income taxpayers and is more generous on capital gains. But Mr. Archer said after the meeting: "We want tax relief for the American people, and this President wants to be able to sign tax relief for the American people. The pressure on both sides is to find a way to come together."
In some ways, the week's votes were easy. Cutting $135 billion in taxes for more than 50 million Americans is something politicians find agreeable. And unlike many past budget bills, this one has spending increases in the short run.
But the sort of discretionary spending caps that have worked since 1990 are here, too. Most important, just about all Republicans and most Democrats cast votes for Medicare savings that expose them -- fairly or demagogically -- to the risk of television spots accusing them of callousness toward the elderly. After the advertisements of 1996, that took political nerve.
But even though the process so far has a notable credibility among experienced budgeteers who have watched past plans fail, they are not convinced that Congress has the courage to face the long-term problems. Bob Greenstein, executive director of the Center for Budget and Policy Priorities, spoke of them as "the demographic tsunami of the baby boomers retiring and the mushrooming of the tax cuts at about the same time around 2011. Even if you have balanced the budget before then, deficits return and return with a vengeance," worse than in the mid-1980's.
At the White House, Gene Sperling, head of the National Economic Council, said that Washington's conventional wisdom held that only in a crisis can there be bipartisanship on long-term issues like Medicare and Social Security. But now, he said, "The political challenge for the President and others is how to get the country to address a long-term problem not because it is a crisis at the doorstep but because acting now is an opportunity to deal with something in a more sensible, more long-term and less disruptive way."
One central figure, Senator Pete V. Domenici, Republican of New Mexico, argued that the tough votes cast in the Senate to raise the Medicare age limit and charge higher premiums to the high-income elderly were a hopeful sign. Relaxing after what he called the "best week of my legislative career," Mr. Domenici, the Budget Committee chairman, said there was no doubt that Medicare would be harder to solve than Social Security. "We just have to face up to it or there will be an enormous problem."
The same sense of political imperative leads both Mr. Archer and Mr. Domenici to expect that the budget will not just be momentarily balanced in 2002 and then, exhausted, return to deficit status.
Mr. Archer acknowledged that "neither the Congress nor the President has repealed the business cycle." But he said he thought balanced budgets "will be the norm from now on," that no Congress would be willing to be the first to let deficits return. Mr. Domenici said: "I think we will grow accustomed in reasonably good times to being in balance. The tax base has grown enough so we can do that."
Several economists are more skeptical about the long run, although they share a striking willingness to consider three things possible in the near term. First and easiest, that Congress and the President will agree on the legislation. Second, that Congress will vote for the discretionary spending cuts it has promised to figure out. Third and hardest, that barring a serious recession, in 2002 the Federal Government will actually spend no more than it takes in.
Isabel Sawhill, a senior fellow at the Urban Institute, a research organization, said the plan could work "if the economy cooperates, but only if the economy cooperates."
Martha Phillips, executive director of the Concord Coalition, a bipartisan lobby for balancing the budget, said that she doubted the goal would be achieved, but that the robust economy could make it happen. "If it keeps up," she said, "we might accidentally balance the budget."
Mr. Greenstein, a critic who says the legislation shortchanges the poor, even concedes, "It's certainly possible, if the economy stays strong, the budget would stay balanced for a while."
But he said, from "2008 to 2017 tax cuts rise to those terrific levels." Ms. Phillips shared his concern, saying she feared that whatever was accomplished in the first years might be "swamped by the tax pieces in the out years."
She was encouraged by the Senate moves on Medicare, but noted that the "graying of America" would be marked in 2008, when the first postwar children begin to take early Social Security benefits at 62. "We have to get on with dealing with the generational issues," she said.
Ms. Sawhill was skeptical about the political ease of increased spending in the first years, spending that goes to education and children's health. Whatever the merits of particular proposals, she said, this style of budget balancing is like "a diet that begins with dessert." And even if a buoyant economy gets the budget through its 5-year path to balance, "we should be worrying about the following 10 to 15."
She conceded that the Medicare cuts amounted to "a little progress on tackling some of the difficult political issues." But she found them inconsistent with "putting in place, particularly on the tax side, a set of commitments that are eventually going to come out of somebody's side."
At least some rank-and-file Republicans have an opposite set of concerns. They are arguing about what to do with the budget surplus they expect to see.
Representative Mark W. Neumann of Wisconsin is writing legislation to deal with a budget surplus. Under his bill, which assumes 6 percent annual growth in Federal revenues, the current $5.3 trillion national debt would be paid off by 2026, using two-thirds of the surplus he foresees while the other third would go to tax cuts -- an additional $112 billion by 2002. He said on Friday he already had 90 co-sponsors, including Representatives John R. Kasich of Ohio, chairman of the Budget Committee, and Robert L. Livingston of Louisiana, chairman of the Appropriations Committee.
Ms. Phillips was scornful. "You can't divvy up the surplus," she said, "until you have it in hand."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Pampered Pitchers: A Debate Is Stirring

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Baseball's universal complaint is lack of pitching. So here is one way to alleviate the shortage: use fewer starting pitchers and return to the four-man rotation of yesteryear in place of the modern version of five.
"I think teams would love that," Lou Piniella, the Seattle Mariners' manager, said of the idea. "I think pitchers' agents would hate it. When you can win 20 games and make $6 million pitching every five days, why do you have to pitch every four and maybe cut a year or two off your career?" 
 Piniella, an old-timer at heart, was just getting started.
"It's gotten even more absurd," he said. "They've put these kids on pitch counts of 100 pitches pitching every five days. How do you develop good mechanics and good location and how do you learn to pitch yourself out of trouble and get your second wind throwing 100 pitches?"
Teams are playing with a double-edged sword, Piniella continued.
"They're so concerned about the lack of pitching," he said, "that they pamper these kids and they overprotect them and they don't learn how to pitch. It's the darndest thing. They're more protected than the manatees are in Florida."
Pampered and overprotected. First came the five-man rotation, then the pitch count. Pampered and overprotected. That says as much about the state of pitching today as ball one, ball two, ball three, ball four.
Since the 1960's, when baseball's first expansion prompted a change in pitching philosophy, starting pitchers working their way up from the minor leagues to the majors have been conditioned physically and mentally to believe they can't pitch more often than every fifth day. More recently, they have additionally been programmed to go only so far -- 100 pitches in many cases -- in the games they start every fifth day.
The product that has emerged from this dual practice is not one readily recognized by their predecessors.
"I've talked to some of the old-timers that threw hard and pitched for a long time," Piniella said. "My goodness, they threw 140, 150 pitches every fourth day."
Retired now for four years, Nolan Ryan probably qualifies as one of those old-timers, if not by age, then by state of mind. "I played so long people started looking at me and asking what is wrong with that grumpy old man," Ryan said recently, discussing his views on pitching and other parts of the game.
Ryan, who is 50, spanned a few pitching generations, beginning his major league career in 1966, the year that was Sandy Koufax's last, through 1993, the year in which Greg Maddux won the second of his four successive Cy Young awards.
Aside from his record seven no-hitters and 5,714 strikeouts, Ryan was noted for his durability. He pitched more than 200 innings in 14 different seasons and in two of those years exceeded 300. In still another season, he fell one inning short of 300. In pitching those innings, however, he was not unique, as he was with his no-hitters and strikeouts.
Among his contemporaries, for example, Gaylord Perry exceeded 200 innings 17 times in a 22-year career, working more than 300 innings six times in a seven-season span. The hardly svelte Mickey Lolich went 12 successive seasons pitching more than 200 innings, topping 300 in four straight seasons with a remarkable career high of 376 in 1971. Steve Carlton worked more than 200 innings for 13 consecutive seasons and 16 of 17, twice exceeded 300 and three other times went beyond 290.
But no pitcher has reached the 300-inning plateau since Carlton pitched 304 innings in 1980. In the 1990's, the most any pitcher has compiled in one season is Roger Clemens's 271 1/3 innings in 1991. Meanwhile, only 10 active pitchers have worked more than 200 innings in as many as five consecutive seasons during their career. And Maddux, who has registered nine consecutive 200-inning seasons, was the only one of the 10 who carried that kind of workload last year.
Barring a reversal of the trend that has rendered workhorse pitchers obsolete, baseball has less chance of seeing a 300-inning pitcher again than it does a .400 hitter. No one seems to be prepared to return to the days of the four-man rotation and the complete-game pitcher.
"When I came up, pitchers threw every fourth day," Ryan said. "Tom Seaver was the first guy I came in contact with that was adamant about pitching every fifth day."
That timetable was not for Ryan.
"I wanted the ball every fourth day," he said. "I can't ever remember going out, when I was accumulating all those innings, thinking I wish I had a couple more days. In those days you wanted the baseball."
 
'I Didn't Want Anybody Else in My Game'
 Ryan recalled another first. When he was a teammate of Don Sutton with the Houston Astros in 1981, he said, Sutton was the first pitcher who said: "My goal is to get them to the seventh inning and then let the bullpen take over. I've done my job."
"I never had that mindset," Ryan said. "It never crossed my mind. I didn't want anybody else in my game."
Today, starting pitchers generally are only the first of several, if not many, pitchers a manager uses in a game. With few exceptions, pitchers are not expected to last nine innings. Many are not even permitted to exceed a designated-pitch limit, 100 being the number that has become more magic than the one that clinches first place.
"How did someone come up with that?" Ryan asked. "There's no science behind that. I threw 242 pitches in a game, then came back four days later. I didn't do too well, but I didn't hurt myself."
Ryan and others believe pitchers have become spoiled.
"If they get to 100 pitches, they say, 'That's it for me,' " Ryan said. "They've done their job. It's out of their hands. It's a mindset."
By pitching less often and fewer innings, pitchers are being cheated of arm strength, control of the strike zone and pitching knowledge, critics of the current system believe.
A pitch limit, David Cone of the Yankees said, "doesn't allow young pitchers to work on getting through tough innings."
In his experience, Cone added, he has often found that he pitches with greater ease and rhythm later in the game than early.
"To me," he said, "pitch 100 to 120 is a lot less taxing than 1 to 20."
Tom Glavine, who was the first cog in the Atlanta Braves' intimidating rotation, said he has no doubt that pitch limits have had a direct effect on the development of pitchers.
"Whatever the reason, pitchers are getting pampered," he said. "When you keep cutting guys off, you don't give them an opportunity to learn how to pitch late in the game or how to pitch when they're a little tired. That makes a big difference."
Furthermore, Glavine said, "Every time you go out with a pitch count, I don't care what a pitcher tells you, you know in the back of your mind 'I'm only throwing X number of pitches' and you end up thinking more about that than you think about just going out and pitching."
 
Economic Crisis Arises on the Mound
 Why do managers and pitching coaches treat pitchers so delicately these days?
"Because of the investment involved now," said Leo Mazzone, the Braves' pitching coach, who does not pamper his pitchers. "A lot of people cover their rear ends by not allowing pitchers to throw."
Ray Miller, another noted pitching coach now working for Baltimore, agreed that the matter has become an economic concern.
"The Pirates just signed Kris Benson for $2 million, said Miller, whose previous job was with Pittsburgh. "You want to take him to A ball and start throwing him every four days. But he's a big power guy and everybody's going to say: 'My god, if he gets hurt, it's my job. We better stay with five. Count his pitches. Make sure he doesn't get hurt.' "
A team's medical staff is influenced by economics as well, Miller said.
"In my first 10 years in the big leagues," he said, "if a pitcher had something bothering him. the trainer said go to the doctor's office tomorrow morning. The guy said it's not that bad and he pitched through it. Now you have an orthopedist in the clubhouse every day. This pitcher is making $3 million a year. If he says he's a little tender, the doctor is going to say I think you should rest him. He's not going to say pitch through it and have the guy go out and get hurt."
Phil Garner, the Milwaukee Brewers' manager, offered his own recent experience with Cal Eldred as proof of the prevailing mindset. Eldred, the American League's top rookie pitcher in 1992, averaged slightly more than seven innings a start in his first two and a half years in the major leagues. Then, in 1995, he developed an elbow problem, had ligament surgery on his right elbow and missed more than a season.
"I think everyone is scared to death of pitching a kid too much," Garner said. "We lost Cal Eldred, and part of the consideration is I threw him too many pitches. I'm not going to let that happen again. Whether that's right or wrong, and I've done research with several different doctors to try to determine if it was too many pitches, I don't want that to happen to a Jeff D'Amico or to Cal again."
But in exercising such caution, some baseball people believe, teams are risking the very thing they're trying to avoid.
"You end up trying to do so much to avoid arm injury," Mazzone said, "that you raise the risk of arm injury because the less you throw -- and when you do throw, you throw hard -- raises the risk."
Glavine readily agreed with his coach. "Everybody errs on the side of caution and that means keep throwing to a minimum," Glavine said. "The only problem is I don't think that's doing them any good. It's probably hurting them more."
 
Cy Young Winners, Despite Little Rest
 Since the Braves developed the starting rotation that has produced five of the last six National League Cy Young awards, they have come the closest of any team to using a four-man rotation, at times using the starters with three days rest but at other times inserting a fifth starter when the schedule doesn't provide a day off.
Whether Glavine is pitching every fourth or fifth day, though, he keeps busy between starts, throwing a baseball in some manner virtually every day. When he is on a five-day schedule, he plays catch the day after he starts to get loose, throws from a mound on the side the next two days, and then plays catch in the outfield with Mazzone the day before he starts again, going through his sequence of pitches in the process.
"It keeps my arm stronger," Glavine said, explaining the reason for his routine, "and the more you can throw a ball, the more you're going to develop touch with your pitches, the more you're going to develop feel for your pitches. The only way you're really going to be able to totally understand your mechanics and totally understand what it feels like when you throw the ball right or don't throw the ball right is to practice it."
A runner, the left-hander added, trains by running, a weight lifter by lifting weights. "Why if you're a pitcher, can't you throw?" he said.
Glavine recalled that it was Mazzone's throwing program that enabled him to strengthen his arm and overcome a naggingly sore arm he endured throughout the 1992 season. In a similar manner, Earl Weaver's pitching policy in Baltimore benefited Jim Palmer.
George Bamberger, who as Weaver's pitching coach helped produce 18 20-game winners in 10 years, told about how they nursed Palmer and his sore arm in 1968.
"Here's a guy who is hurt and doesn't know if he's ever going to play again," Bamberger, now retired, said. "We start pitching him in a four-man rotation and he wins the Cy Young award three times and 20 games eight times. A four-man rotation certainly didn't hurt Jim Palmer."
Palmer and the other three pitchers in the four-man rotation all emerged as 20-game winners in 1971. Palmer and Pat Dobson each pitched 282 innings, Mike Cuellar 292 and Dave McNally 224. No team has had four 20-game winners since, and it has been 10 years, since 1987, that the four busiest pitchers in either league collectively have thrown as many or more innings.

HEADLINE: The B & B Alternative Spreads In Western Suffolk and Nassau

BYLINE: By REGINA MARCAZZO 

 BODY:
WHEN Diane Whalen bought her house on Oak Street in Port Jefferson Village, she envisioned opening a bed-and-breakfast in the 19th-century Victorian structure.
More than six years later the house has become the Brick Hill Bed and Breakfast. 
 "The house is just very conducive to a bed-and-breakfast," said Ms. Whalen, who has filled her quarters and four guest rooms with antiques.
Brick Hill is one of several new B & B's on Long Island; it is the second in Port Jefferson Village.
Although B &B's have long been popular on the East End, they have been opening in greater numbers in western Suffolk County and in Nassau, appealing to many as a desirable alternative to hotels and motels. The lodging option also works well in smaller areas that lack rooms for visitors.
While the number of B & B lodgings are growing, the total number of such rooms offered all over Long Island would not add up to the number of rooms in a large hotel, according to Robert Lipper of Metro Publications, who publishes a magazine distributed in Island hotel rooms. "It offers a quiet more secluded atmosphere and it's very personable. It's more like home."
B & B prices range from $50 to almost $300 a night in the Long Island Lodging Guide's spring 1997 edition. A number of facilities require a two-night minimum.
Two Suffolk Towns, Brookhaven and Huntington, adopted bed-and-breakfast ordinances in the spring.
"There's been an increase in bed-and-breakfasts over the years," said Mr. Lipper, who listed 38 in his Lodging Guide last year and lists 52 in the spring 1997 edition.
The guide lists 13, compared with 9 last year, west of the East End.
"A lot of these are business people," Mr. Lipper said of the guests. "It's not the traditional vacationer."
"Our perspective," said the president of the Long Island Convention and Visitors Bureau, Michael Davidson, "is that there is a portion of the traveling public that likes nontraditional places to stay and that's where the bed-and-breakfast world comes from. It opens up opportunities. We think it's a good thing. We're becoming aware of more and more of them. I think they need to be supported by local government."
Wendy and Richard Duvall have been welcoming guests to the Country Life Bed and Breakfast in Hempstead for 15 years. The guests, they say, include foreign tourists who are looking for tranquil refuges close to New York City, doctors and patients involved with nearby hospitals and business people on temporary assignments.
The Duvalls' first guests, a British couple who visited on their honeymoon 15 years ago, have returned many times and have become like a part of the family. "After they left they called us Mom and Dad," Mr. Duvall said.
The Duvalls go to bed-and-breakfasts when they travel and share stories and recipes with other owners.
"We've had amazing people here," said Mr. Duvall, who said he had learned about World War II from many guests. He has also learned about taking apart musical instruments and had famous singers knock on the back door. An Italian organ builder stayed for a month two years in a row while taking apart and putting back together an organ that the guest's grandfather had built for a church in Garden City.
"He would give us daily reports on it," Mr. Duvall said. "He built some of the greatest organs in the world."
Nassau County has one other listing in the guide, My Country Home in Syosset.
The Lindenmere in Center Moriches, at the estate formerly owned by Ferdinand E. Marcos, the Philippine strongman, is the first bed-and-breakfast to open since the Town of Brookhaven passed its ordinance in April to promote them.
"It's too large for two people," said Jennie Magaro, who with her husband, Dr. Peter Magaro, bought the estate last year and readopted the former name.
With 12 guest rooms, the Lindenmere offers a bay view and a pool house. Mrs. Magaro said she believed that B & B's were a good alternative to other lodgings for small seaside towns like Center Moriches.
"To me it's really a salvation for a bunch of small towns," she said. "How many people want the upkeep of a house this size? It's not just for our benefit. I think it's a really nice alternative to preserve old properties."
Brookhaven will permit B & B's in historic and shoreline communities like Center Moriches and will require owner occupancy and no more guest bedrooms than the number already in existence, minus one for the owner.
"This planning mechanism," said Supervisor Felix Grucci Jr., "will help us protect and preserve property values and promote the efficient use of the town's existing stock of single-family dwellings while providing some measure of economic relief to families of modest income."
The five-year permits are not transferrable and require that guests stay for no longer than 14 days. The characteristics of the houses have to remain intact, with no special entrances and one kitchen. B & B signs can be no larger than six square feet.
Pat Darling said the Holly Berry Bed and Breakfast, which she has operated in Port Jefferson since 1995, was filled nearly every weekend through the summer.
An application for a third B & B is pending, but the village has imposed a six-month moratorium on approvals to look closely at the ordinance, according to Village Clerk Caroline Savino. "It was after the second application that the village realized that the law needed refining," Ms. Savino said.
"I think the greatest shortcoming is people not thinking of us as being a business," Ms. Darling said. "They're a tremendous investment if you do it right."
She devotes much time to details like serving candlelight breakfasts and decorating the rooms in themes, and she changes the decorations on her picket fence seasonally.
"It is my giant doll house," said Ms. Darling, who had a Holly Berry doll made and plays host to grandmothers, mothers and young girls for special weekends.
In Centerport Jean and Jim Vavrina were waiting for the Town of Huntington to pass an ordinance permitting B & B's.
The president of the Centerport Harbor Civic Association, Gloria Wertheimer, said her group had sent a questionnaire to its members for reactions on the Vavrinas' proposal. Of 100 questionnaires, 2 were returned, with one concerned about parking and another opposed to the entire plan.
"We really felt it was going to be quite harmless," Ms. Wertheimer said.
Councilman Steve Israel, who introduced a B & B ordinance in May, said, "If a bed-and-breakfast makes sense historically, environmentally, culturally and economically, then our Town Board should allow it to be considered on a case-by-case basis."
Under the measure a bed-and-breakfast has to be the main residence of the owner, and it must be limited to two bedrooms. No guests can stay for more than seven days, and the Town Board can revoke the permit at any time.
The house cannot harm the character of the neighborhood, and it has to have adequate parking. The bill also bars B & B's within 500 feet of each other. The Vavrinas have owned their house on Centershore Road, in a historic district, for 15 years. Mrs. Vavrina had registered her bed-and-breakfast as a business with Suffolk County and took a course in food service before finding out that she was not be able to open the business.
"Fifty percent of tourists in western Suffolk seek lodging," Mr. Israel said. "It helps our museums. It helps our local restaurants. It helps our merchants. There are a lot of historical properties that are deteriorating."
Bed-and-breakfasts are not always welcome. Neighbors are sometimes skeptical about parking, lengths of stays and other potential problems.
When Mark Russo applied last year to turn a former adult home on Woodbine Avenue in Northport into a B & B the Village Zoning denied the application.
"It's perfectly set up to run as a lodging facility," said Mr. Russo. "Northport is severely lacking in lodging facilities. It's about crossing all the legal 'T's' and dotting all the legal 'I's.' "
Although he is awaiting a decision from the village, Mr. Russo plans to open a three-bedroom B & B this weekend. Called the Hillcrest Manor, Mr. Russo is featuring Victorian and Provincial furniture, artwork by five artists and breakfast prepared by a gourmet chef and baker. He will charge $70 to $100 a night depending on the time of the week.
Mr. Russo expects to receive a summons from Northport Village and a subsequent court date. A zoning board of appeals decision on a May public hearing asking for approval for a multiple dwelling for occupancy of 10 or for a 10-bedroom lodging house is pending, according to village officials.

HEADLINE: A Chancellor's Fate

BYLINE: By KAREN W. ARENSON 

 BODY:
For seven years, W. Ann Reynolds has served as Chancellor of the City University of New York. How much longer she will continue is anyone's guess.
In recent weeks, criticism of her performance by trustees has mushroomed, and the chairwoman of the board has talked publicly about considering possible successors. 
 But if board members are so unhappy with her, why do they not just dismiss her, rather than continue their public attacks, attacks that some feel are undermining the university itself?
Procedurally, dismissing the Chancellor is not difficult. She serves at the pleasure of the board, and has no employment contract as Chancellor. Nor is there any due process. All it takes is a majority of the board -- nine votes -- deciding to make a change.
Until now, however, that majority did not seem to exist. Although the board leaders -- the chairwoman, Anne A. Paolucci, and vice chairman, Herman Badillo -- have led the attacks and are often seen as speaking for the board, they hold only two votes. There are 16 voting board members, including a student, and a faculty member who does not have a vote.
If Dr. Paolucci and Mr. Badillo can make the situation uncomfortable enough that the Chancellor resigns, they do not need more support. But in a vote, they would need seven other trustees. And while a loose coalition has formed around Dr. Paolucci and Mr. Badillo, the nine trustees that Gov. George E. Pataki and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani named last summer have not always voted together.
The appointment of two new trustees this month could change the arithmetic. Some trustees predict that the new trustees will most likely join the Paolucci coalition, since one, Kenneth E. Cook, was sponsored by the same Conservative Party that backed Dr. Paolucci's appointment, and the other, Alfred B. Curtis Jr., was a loyal official in the Giuliani administration, with which Mr. Badillo has close ties. But it is too early to say definitively how they would vote.
Mr. Cook could not be reached for comment at his home on Friday. But Mr. Curtis, who was youth commissioner under Mayor Giuliani and is now president of the United Nations Development Corporation, said he is still trying to learn as much as he can about CUNY and is spending two or three hours a night poring over documents.
"My gut feeling is that something has got to give, and that we have got to make some decisions and move ahead," he said. "We need to return to a relationship of trust between the chancellor and the board."
He added, however, "I haven't arrived at any conclusion. I need to understand the full intricacies of the board and its politics before I can reach a decision."
But for other trustees, the blowup last month over Hostos Community College was a catalytic event. At its May meeting, the board believed it was reinforcing existing policy when it voted to require students at Hostos and all of the community colleges in the City University system to pass its writing test in English before graduating. The trustees later discovered that five of the six community colleges did not require the test for graduation, and many blamed the Chancellor for the embarrassment that resulted.
But even as their attacks on the Chancellor have escalated, Dr. Paolucci and Mr. Badillo have said they need more time to evaluate Dr. Reynolds, and that they will watch what she does this summer.
"Certainly by the end of the summer, we should have very clear movement on the issues we have outlined," Mr. Badillo said.
He said he and other trustees have clearly delineated areas where they want change, including reducing the time students spend in remedial classes, reducing grade inflation and fixing problems at Hostos.
"I want Ann Reynolds to take the leadership," Mr. Badillo added.
Another test, trustees say, will be the report they have asked the Chancellor to give them by Aug. 1, laying out the community colleges' graduation requirements and their policies on the English writing test, and also what actions they should take against the community college presidents for not having been forthcoming about their policies at the May board meeting.
"The report she gives us on Aug. 1 may be the litmus test," said Ronald J. Marino, the trustee who requested the report. "If she just gives us a couple of pieces of paper and calls that a report, it is not good enough."
But Edith Everett, a longtime board member and strong supporter of the Chancellor, called the report a foolish test and said the community college presidents should be punished for not being able to explain their campus procedures.
"Their behavior is irrational," she said, referring to the other trustees.
A spokeswoman for Ms. Reynolds, Jay Hershenson, yesterday reiterated her intention to work "cooperatively and constructively" with all trustees.
And at least one trustee, James P. Murphy, the former board chairman who has been working to bring the two sides together, held out hope that rapprochement is possible.
"My sense is that they can work together if they try to," he said. "And I think there are others on the board who feel the same and would prefer their best efforts at trying to work together rather than the kind of chaos that develops if that doesn't happen."

CORRECTION-DATE: July 1, 1997, Tuesday

 CORRECTION:
A news analysis article on Sunday about the fate of W. Ann Reynolds, the Chancellor of the City University of New York, who has been sharply criticized by some trustees, misstated the position of one trustee on whether community college presidents should be punished for failing to inform the trustees about testing requirements at their colleges. The trustee, Edith Everett, said the presidents should not be punished.
Because of an editing error, the article also referred incorrectly to Jay Hershenson, a spokesman for Ms. Reynolds. He is a man.

HEADLINE: Hold the Jokes, Please: Aeroflot Buffs Its Image

BYLINE: By ALESSANDRA STANLEY 

DATELINE: MOSCOW

 BODY:
AEROFLOT has long held a worldwide reputation for rude service, disgusting food and scary landings. Comedians have made Aeroflot jokes for decades. Now the Russian airline is making fun of itself.
In its first big multimedia advertising campaign, Aeroflot has chosen a new image for itself: a flying elephant. 
 All over Russia, in magazine advertising and on TV commercials and billboards, a huge, gray, heavily wrinkled pachyderm will soon soar beatifically across blue skies.
The advertising slogan, taken from a familiar Russian idiom, translates loosely as "light on its feet" but the underlying, tongue-in-cheek message to skeptical consumers is to suspend judgment: elephants can fly, and so can Aeroflot. It is a startling, offbeat pitch, and a daring departure for one of the former Soviet Union's most somber and ponderous institutions.
Aeroflot, now officially known as Aeroflot Russian International Airlines, has shrunk drastically since the Communist state collapsed, bringing the world's largest airline down with it.
As it struggles to remold itself to fit Western standards of promotion, safety and service, Aeroflot offers a telling glimpse into the opaque inner workings of Russian business -- both the ways in which it has changed and the ways it has not.
Aeroflot is preparing to sell about 10 percent of its shares on the United States market in September, part of an ambitious drive to raise several billion dollars to start replacing its aging fleet with new Ilyushins, Boeings and Airbuses to compete better in the world market.
But the airline is lobbying the Government to keep protectionist restrictions on Russian air space, locking Moscow into a regulatory dispute with Washington that could sabotage Aeroflot's own triumphantly announced alliance with Continental Airlines.
The state retains 51 percent of Aeroflot's stock, but in many ways Aeroflot looks like a modern Russian corporation with all the trimmings: fancy offices in New York and London, a glossy annual report, an offshore subsidiary in Switzerland and a site on the World Wide Web (www.aeroflot.org).
The Export-Import Bank of the United States and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development have agreed to lend Aeroflot a total of nearly $1.5 billion over 10 years to purchase new planes.
Because Aeroflot also wants to raise money from Western investors, Price Waterhouse is preparing an American-style audit of Aeroflot, due to be published in July, to help it meet the detailed disclosure requirements for an offering of American depository receipts. Though the eventual underwriters have yet to be selected, Salomon Brothers is the lead consultant on the deal.
Yet within Aeroflot, a Soviet mind-set lingers. Internal company charts and graphs still refer to competing Western companies like Delta and Lufthansa as "capitalist airlines."
Aeroflot, with 115 planes, transported 3.8 million passengers last year -- a fraction of the 5,400 planes and 138 million passengers it boasted in 1991. (In comparison, Delta has 552 planes and carried 91.3 million passengers in 1996.) Aeroflot had an after-tax profit of $24.5 million in 1996, slightly less than had been projected but a vast improvement over years of profits that may well have been more wish than reality. But in order to replace about 70 percent of its aged fleet, the company needs a lot more money than it currently earns. The debt-ridden Russian Government cannot help.
"The times when the state provided Aeroflot with everything from planes to napkins are gone," said Valery Okulov, a former Aeroflot navigator who was appointed last month as the company's general director. Mr. Okulov should know: his wife, Yelena, is the elder daughter of Boris N. Yeltsin, the Russian President.
Mr. Okulov, who hopes to lure back passengers, Russian and foreign, who fled Aeroflot in droves as soon as other, more reputable Western carriers became easily available, recently laid out his company's goals at a news conference. "We are making efforts to renovate our planes," he said. "We pay more attention to advertising. We are trying to be more flexible on tariffs and improve service and safety."
Aeroflot and Russian aviation reached a nadir in 1994, when a record number of crashes on domestic routes drove the United States Embassy to instruct employees not to travel around Russia by air. Aeroflot's most infamous disaster also occurred that year, when an Airbus A310 crashed on its way to Hong Kong after the pilot allowed his 13-year-old son to take over the controls. All 75 people aboard were killed.
The Embassy's warning was lifted after the Russian Government agreed to work with the Federal Aviation Administration to improve its standards, and crash rates have fallen in the last two years. But a 1996 book, "Flying Blind, Flying Safe," by Mary Schiavo, a former inspector general for the Department of Transportation, which advises passengers to "avoid Russian-made planes at all costs," caused a furor here. Public opinion, at home and abroad, remains deeply skeptical about aviation safety in Russia.
For good reason. Not one of Russia's 3,000 airports meets the top Western standards for radar and tracking equipment, while nearly all of the primary American airports meet those standards. Even the biggest Russian airports are filthy, poorly equipped, congested and chaotic.
Aeroflot's extreme downsizing occurred shortly after the Soviet Union collapsed, as newly independent republics and newly empowered regions of Russia laid claim to planes and equipment in their territories. The state was forced to break up Aeroflot's monopoly and to spin off almost all its domestic routes to more than 300 small, regional airlines known as "Babyflots."
But some things have not changed: flights within the former Soviet Union, whether Aeroflot's or its progeny's, are still often canceled or delayed, and they still feature bad food, surly service and alarmingly dilapidated cabins.
On a flight last summer to Stavropol in southern Russia, a passenger was scoldingly instructed to fasten his seat belt. When he held up a broken buckle, the flight attendant shrugged and ordered him to tie the two ends together.
In Soviet times, air travel was relatively cheap, and planes would often be filled with farmers carting huge bundles of produce -- even chickens and other livestock. The soaring costs of airplane tickets have driven those customers back to trains and buses, but it is still not unusual to see pet poodles and Labradors boarding Aeroflot planes alongside their owners. And, before takeoff, air-conditioning in summer is as rare as heat in winter.
ON international flights, however, service is noticeably better. To most major destinations, Aeroflot passengers can fly on a modern Airbus or Boeing jetliner. (Aeroflot now has 10 Airbus A310's, two Boeing 767's and six new Russian Il-96-300's. It also hopes to buy 20 Ilyushins -- outfitted with American Pratt & Whitney engines -- as well as 10 Boeing 737's. It plans to lease 5 more A310's in the next five years.)
Passengers on international flights receive service comparable to that on Western airlines. There are choices -- chicken or beef -- for meals. Smiling attendants are snappily dressed. There is a groaning beverage cart, a glossy in-flight magazine, duty-free shopping and movies. On some flights, there are first-class or business-class cabins, which are usually filled with newly rich Russians.
Fares are generally lower on Aeroflot than for its rivals, but there are exceptions: A regular round-trip economy ticket to New York from Moscow costs $1,700 on Delta and $1,137 on Aeroflot. But Finnair, the Finnish airline, which offers flights to New York via Helsinki, charges $984 -- one reason that last year, it carried three times as many Russian passengers abroad from Moscow as Aeroflot.
To undercut the competition, Aeroflot recently introduced a tantalizing special offer: From June 16 to July 6, the airline promised to sell round-trip tickets to Moscow from New York for $220, according to ads appearing in New York, and then the passenger could choose another round trip to one of several other cities -- Paris, Rome, Tokyo, Beijing and others among the 100 worldwide that Aeroflot serves -- for another $220. The ads noted that space was limited, and the red tape and ticketing delays were so severe at swamped Aeroflot booking offices that customers who actually secured the special fares are now treated with the envious awe usually reserved for lottery winners.
Aeroflot, however, has one selling point that no other major carrier offers: Smoking is permitted in special sections.
That is one difference Aeroflot intends to convey subliminally to Russian customers in its new ad campaign. One commercial, planned to debut in late summer or fall, features foreign passengers in business class, debating Russia's future. As the camera sweeps across the cabin, it glides over a beverage cart and a telltale cloud of blue smoke.
"We can't say it, but we can show it," explained the ad campaign's creative director, Yuri Bokser, of Premier SV, the Russian advertising firm that is producing Aeroflot's $7 million ad campaign. "Russians want to smoke."
But it will take more than a smoking section for Aeroflot to bloom. Aviation experts say the area with the most potential growth is the domestic market, and Aeroflot lost most of its domestic routes both to smaller Russian rivals and to foreign carriers like Lufthansa, which flies to many Russian cities, bypassing Moscow. Aeroflot is trying to recapture some of its lost routes, but it is currently focused on the international market, where it has few advantages over foreign competitors.
"The only real asset they have is landing rights in Moscow and St. Petersburg," said James R. Fenkner, director of research at Centre Invest Group, a consulting and securities company in Moscow that recently completed a less-than-bullish brokerage report on Aeroflot. Mr. Fenkner says he thinks that Aeroflot shares, currently selling for about $95 on the Russian stock market, are overvalued.
"Aeroflot is concentrated on the international market, where they have to compete with companies that are already operating very effectively."
AEROFLOT flies to 94 countries, and offers seven flights a week to New York. So does Delta Airlines, the only American carrier currently flying nonstop to Moscow from New York. That market may soon become more crowded: Transaero, a six-year-old privately owned Russian airline that boasts newer planes and better service, plans to offer a Moscow-New York flight by next year. It has made deep inroads into Aeroflot's domestic market and already flies to Los Angeles.
To wedge itself deeper into the trans-Atlantic market, Aeroflot signed a landmark code-sharing agreement with Continental this spring that would allow Aeroflot to sell blocks of seats on Continental flights between Moscow and Continental's hub in Newark. The arrangement would give Aeroflot passengers one-stop booking and lower fares to 70 cities Continental serves from Newark -- as well as frequent-flier miles on Continental.
But that deal is imperiled by Russia's refusal to grant other American carriers landing rights in Russia through their European partners, an arrangement known as third-country code-sharing. Delta, for example, wants to increase its service to Russia by routing Moscow-bound passengers through Vienna on Austrian Airlines, with which it has a code-sharing agreement.
Moscow has blocked this and a few similar arrangements.
"There has been a very bald refusal to meet the provisions of the U.S.-Russia bilateral agreement," said Paul Gretch, director of the office of international aviation at the United States Department of Transportation. "Until they approve third-country code-sharing, the U.S. will not agree to the Continental-Aeroflot deal."
Aeroflot is equally adamant that Russia should not allow more competitors in -- even at the risk of jeopardizing its agreement with Continental. "We are not going to open the sky in the foreseeable future either to American or other airline companies," Mr. Okulov said sternly. "At least I hope not."
The chief financial officer of Aeroflot is Nikolai Glushkov, who is also acting chairman of Logovaz, a leading media and oil conglomerate founded by Boris A. Berezovsky. Mr. Berezovsky, who handed control of Logovaz over to Mr. Glushkov when he became deputy secretary of the National Security Council, is a business leader who helped to bankroll Mr. Yeltsin's re-election campaign last summer. Mr. Berezovsky's companies are a symbol of the cozy co-dependence between big business and the Kremlin. Russian newspapers have raised questions about whether Logovaz's ties to Aeroflot are in the best long-term interest of the airline.
The Russian Government denies any plans to sell its Aeroflot shares in the near future, but in an interview Mr. Glushkov said he thought a sale could take place as soon as next year. He acknowledged that Logovaz planned to buy as many shares as it could when the sale occurred, but said that neither he nor Logovaz had yet bought any stock. They are well-positioned, however: A Logovaz team manages Aeroflot, and the company that registers sales and purchases of Aeroflot shares is Fondovaya Registratsionnaya Kompaniya, an offshoot of Sibneft, the big oil company in which Mr. Berezovsky has a large stake.
The price of Aeroflot shares quadrupled in the last six months, from $30 in December to more than $120 last month, before dropping to $83 this week after a decline in the overall Russian stock market. The recent gains occurred partly because unidentified companies have been buying employee shares; one Aeroflot spokesman said that as many as 15 percent of employee-held shares had been bought by outsiders. The company says no single buyer controls more than 2.5 percent of the stock.
IF and when the state's shares are sold, economic analysts wonder whether a new owner like Logovaz will make Aeroflot attractive to foreign investors. "If the company is effectively controlled by Logovaz, minority shareholders may not do so well," Mr. Fenkner said.
Aeroflot is run Russian-style, from the top down. Top directors make decisions with little input or preliminary research by middle-level executives. The daring new advertising campaign is a case in point: When Aeroflot decided it needed a face lift, its directors did not hold a competition or spend months weighing various proposals. They made a gut decision.
Mr. Berezovsky had put Mr. Glushkov in touch with Sergei Lisovsky, a protege who controls advertising time on ORT, the network in which Mr. Berezovsky owns a large stake. Mr. Lisovsky also owns the Premier SV advertising company, one of the most aggressive in Russia. Mr. Lisovsky brought Mr. Bokser to Mr. Glushkov, who listened a few minutes, then gave a green light. "He just said, 'Fine, let's do it,' " Mr. Bokser recalled admiringly. "It was so un-Soviet."
Others high in the company see something all too reminiscent of Communist-style decision-making. "There is no glasnost in the selection of proposals from advertising agencies," Mr. Okulov complained at his news conference. "Some of our ads harm the company's image."
When he was still deputy general director, Mr. Okulov is said to have preferred a Russian bear as a more dignified, nationalist image-builder for the company, but his aides say that though he has not warmed to the flying elephant mascot, he has no plans to kill it now that he is the boss.
Perhaps the most vivid sign that Aeroflot is becoming more of a Western-style airline is the attitude of its regular customers. Citizens of the old Soviet Union often viewed foreign travel as a Government-granted privilege and Aeroflot personnel as the eyes and ears of the state. Now Aeroflot passengers can be as blase or petulant as any frequent fliers in the United States.
"Before, a trip abroad was something exceptional for our passengers -- they behaved, they were even afraid of doing something wrong," said Irina Gudalina, 38, who has been an Aeroflot flight attendant for 21 years and was taking a refresher course last week at an Aeroflot training school in Moscow. She makes $152 a month, plus a per diem payment of about $45 for each day she works.
"Now there is freedom but no culture," she said. "They feel free to do anything they want. Some passengers are downright scornful -- one will throw a few bucks on my trolley and say, 'I smoke where I want.' "
