Chronology: "Taking the Measure of the Elephant"
Aeroflot Russian International Airlines, the successor to the giant Aeroflot airline of the old Soviet Union, is remaking itself to meet new challenges. The airline is seeking to raise money to modernize its fleet by selling about 10 percent of its shares on the United States market in September.
 
AEROFLOT THROUGH THE YEARS
 
Once it controlled all civil aviation in the Soviet Union, and the Cyrillic logo on its planes was a familiar feature in news photographs taken at airports around the world. Now its domestic monopoly, its mammoth fleet and its cold war role are all consigned to the past. Here are highlights of Aeroflot's long history.
 
1928 -- Russian Volunteer Airfleet and Ukrainian Airways, both started a few years before, are combined to form a single Soviet state airline, Dobroflot.
 
1932 -- Dobroflot is reorganized to become Aeroflot. Within three years, Aeroflot's network of routes extends across the Soviet Union to the Black Sea, the Caucasus and Central Asia.
 
1941 -- After Germany invades the Soviet Union, Aeroflot is absorbed into the Soviet air force for the remainder of World War II. For decades afterward, most Aeroflot aircraft are designed to be quickly converted to military use, if needed.
 
1956 -- Aeroflot initiates the world's first civilian jet airline service, between Moscow and Irkutsk in Siberia. The Tu-104 aircraft had the same engines and wings as the Tu-16 bomber.
 
1968 -- The world's first flight by a supersonic airliner, a prototype of the Soviet Tu-144, takes place; the prototype of the English-French Concorde does not fly until the following year. America's supersonic jetliner program is canceled by Congress in 1971 before the Boeing aircraft ever flies.
 
Aeroflot begins service between Moscow and New York, the first direct air link between the United States and the Soviet Union since the cold war began after World War II.
 
1972-73 -- Aeroflot is plagued by accidents, 10 in two years, including the crash of a supersonic Tu-144 at the 1973 Paris Air Show.
 
A crash of a three-engine Tu-154 leads to a drive within Aeroflot to match the airline's Western competitors in simulator training. Before, almost all flight training for pilots was carried out in actual aircraft, where practicing some kinds of emergencies is not possible.
 
1975 -- Aeroflot announces it will begin supersonic airline service on Christmas Day between Moscow and Alma-Ata. But the service does not actually get under way until almost two years later.
 
1980's -- Aeroflot offers air service to every continent (including, on occasion, to Antarctica).
 
1981 -- The Reagan Administration cuts off Aeroflot service to the United States after martial law is declared in Poland. Flights do not resume until almost five years later, following a 1985 summit meeting of Presidents Reagan and Gorbachev in Geneva.
 
1984 -- The supersonic Tu-144 is withdrawn from service permanently after seven years, mainly because of high operating costs.
 
1992 -- After the dissolution of the Soviet Union, the Government of the Russian Federation decides to split up Aeroflot. More than 300 small regional airlines, known as Babyflots, emerge to take over most domestic routes. Aeroflot retains most international routes and is reorganized as Aeroflot Russian International Airlines.
 
1997 -- Aeroflot plans to raise billions of dollars on the international equity markets in the autumn to pay for modernization of its fleet.
 
Graphs: "AEROFLOT AS A CARRIER"
In 1995, Aeroflot carried roughly the same number of international passengers as Continental and had a fleet about the size of Air Nippon Airways. In recent years, Aeroflot has had trouble increasing its passenger business, measured in passenger kilometers flown, an industry gauge that combines customer counts and flight distances. It has had better luck with freight, but only a fifth of Aeroflot's revenue comes from hauling cargo. Graphs show passenger and freight traffic, 1991-95. Charts show size rankings of airlines (1995) and the kind of passenger fleet owned or leased.
 
"AEROFLOT AS A BUSINESS"
Aeroflot has managed to increase revenue in recent years, but its profit margins have shrunk drastically, as have pretax profits. One reason: Aeroflot planes now fly only three-fifths full, on average, a load factor that is much lower than that of airlines like Air Canada or Continental, which carry similar numbers of international passengers. Aeroflot changed its accounting methods after 1994, somewhat reducing the profits it reports. Graphs show total revenue, net profit, and load factor.
 
(Sources: International Air Transport Association, "Aeroflot: An Airline and Its aircraft" (Paladwr Press), Centre Invest Securites, Aeroflot)(pg. 13)

PUB-DATE: June 29, 1997

SECTION: Section 3; Page 2; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1191 words

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By JAN M. ROSEN

 BODY:

SUPREME COURT
Liberty for Cyberlibertines
In a sweeping endorsement of free speech in cyberspace, the Supreme Court declared unconstitutional a Federal law that was intended to protect minors by making it a crime to put "indecent" material on the Internet. The decision delighted a coalition of Internet users, the computer industry and civil liberties groups. In other decisions affecting business, the court gave Federal prosecutors and securities regulators a major victory, ruling that insider trading laws apply to people with confidential information even if they have no connection to the company involved. The court also struck down a landmark $1.3 billion class-action settlement for victims of asbestos exposure; upheld a New Deal-era Federal marketing program that requires agricultural companies to help pay for industrywide advertising, and denied prison guards employed by private companies the immunity from prisoner lawsuits enjoyed by guards on public payrolls.
 
THE MARKETS
Forget Stocks. Cut Velvet.
Stocks had a zigzag week that left traders' psyches as battered toward the end of the week as prices were on Monday, when they plunged 192 points on fear that the Japanese might sell United States Treasury securities. Tokyo hastily declared that remarks by Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto had been misinterpreted, and the market climbed back up. By the end of the week, prices were back at last week's levels. The Dow Jones industrial average was off 108.79 at 7,687.72, and the technology-heavy Nasdaq composite index was down 8.95 at 1,438.15. A much better investment, it seems, was made by Diana, Princess of Wales. At a packed Christie's auction in Manhattan, an off-the-shoulder, ink-blue dinner dress in which she danced at the White House with John Travolta sold for a record $222,500. Altogether the auction of 79 dresses owned by Diana raised $3.25 million for cancer and AIDS charities. The dresses originally cost about $5,000 each, so the gain was more than eightfold. 

THE ENVIRONMENT
Tightening Pollution Rules
President Clinton approved significantly tighter pollution limits on deadly soot and choking smog, while offering states and cities substantial flexibility in reaching the new goals. Ending a fierce behind-the-scenes battle, Mr. Clinton sided with the head of the Environmental Protection Agency, Carol M. Browner, against the concerns of his economic advisers. They had balked in the face of industry complaints that the rules would cost far more than they were worth. Earlier in the week, European leaders were openly critical of the United States at a United Nations-sponsored conference, demanding that Washington be far more aggressive in reducing emissions of greenhouse gases.
 
TOBACCO SETTLEMENT
A Bargain at $368.5 Billion?
Will there indeed be a $368.5 billion tobacco settlement, or is it all a pipe dream? President Clinton called the agreement, which is intended to pay for public service advertising and medical costs, flawed but a beginning. It might hobble the Government's regulation of nicotine, he said. Critics contended that it was too favorable to the tobacco industry, and a panel of public health experts named to advise Congress labeled it unacceptable. The state Attorneys General who negotiated the landmark pact defended it. "It's the best anyone has put forward," said Christine Gregoire of Washington." If it is accepted, Philip Morris will provide the bulk -- $6.5 billion -- of the initial payment of $10 billion.
 
PERSONAL COMPUTERS
They Call Them 'User Friendly'
Today's personal computers have too many features and demand that users learn far too much. Calling the machines "user friendly" because of their endless choice of fonts and screen patterns "is tantamount to dressing a chimpanzee in a green hospital gown and earnestly parading it as a surgeon." The technophobe who uttered these words? None other than Dr. Michael L. Dertouzos, head of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology's computer laboratory. "People should really revolt," he said. "I think we should take up arms." But children, of course, can master the monsters. So William H. Gates, the co-founder of Microsoft and the nation's richest person, is creating the Gates Library Foundation. It will spend $200 million over five years to bring new computers and the Internet into the nation's public libraries.
 
DIGITAL TV
Giving Broadcasters a Break
In April, Federal regulators lent each of the nation's 1,600 television stations a second channel so they could do parallel broadcasts -- standard and digital. Once the transition to new high-definition digital broadcasting was complete, the extra channels were to be vacated, and the Government would auction them for a projected $5 billion. But under provisions that lobbyists have managed to work into House and Senate budget bills, the broadcast-television industry could keep the additional channels indefinitely, while possibly delaying the introduction of digital television broadcasts far beyond Government-set deadlines.
 
THE ECONOMY
Confidence at a 28-Year High
Americans' confidence in the economy continued to climb this month, suggesting that consumer spending may rebound. The Conference Board said that its index of consumer confidence reached a 28-year high of 129.6, up from May's revised index of 127.9. And little wonder. The Commerce Department reported that the economy grew at a slightly faster pace than estimated in the first quarter, at a 5.9 percent annual rate, but inflation remained in check. Also easing fears of inflation, durable-goods orders unexpectedly fell six-tenths of 1 percent in May. Home resales in the month also confounded analysts' expectations, increasing 4.4 percent.
 
THE HAND-OVER
Red Chips Over Hong Kong
As the sun sets on 156 years of British rule, the Hong Kong stock market's Hang Seng Index rose 68.77 points, or five-tenths of 1 percent, to a record 15,196.79, led by the red chips -- the shares of China-backed investment companies. China Everbright International Ltd. jumped 36 percent, and Cosco Pacific Ltd. soared 18 percent. Chinese companies, eager to draw on Hong Kong's expertise in global marketing, see enormous business opportunities when Beijing takes over on Tuesday.
 
MERGERS
Compaq's $3 Billion Deal
Compaq Computer agreed to swap stock worth $3 billion for Tandem Computers, which makes fault-tolerant systems -- large mainframe-like machines used by banks, airlines and telecommunications centers that cannot risk computer failure. Compaq's goal is to grow beyond PC's -- where it is already No. 1 -- to become the world's third-largest computer company after I.B.M. and Hewlett-Packard. In other big merger moves, Union Pacific Resources began a hostile $4 billion offer for Pennzoil, and Wachovia agreed to buy Central Fidelity Banks of Richmond for stock worth $2.3 billion. The Zurich Group plans to acquire Scudder Stevens & Clark for $1.67 billion in cash and stock, and Nationsbank was reported to be negotiating to buy Montgomery Securities for as much as $1.2 billion. Other bidders were also expected.

HEADLINE: MAKING IT WORK;
Bird Made Him a Sleuth

BYLINE: By CHARLIE LeDUFF 

 BODY:
IT was in 1991, at the Golden Nugget flea market in New Jersey, in an overcast field of rusted tools, that the falcon fell in the lap of Ara Chekmayan. It was a mahogany-colored statuette about a foot tall, weighing five pounds and made of resin, for $8. On the underside of the base was inscribed the serial number 90456 WB.
"I suspected right away what that WB meant," said Mr. Chekmayan, 57, an Oscar-nominated director for his 1972 documentary "Children of the Darkness" and a three-time Emmy winner. "It meant Warner Brothers." 
 The director thought he had a quarter-million-dollar find, a means to finance a feature film. Mr. Chekmayan, a resident of Douglaston, Queens, thought he had found gold, but it may only be fool's gold.
You see, Mr. Chekmayan thought the statuette had been one of those used in John Huston's 1941 film noir classic "The Maltese Falcon," based on the novel by Dashiell Hammett. In that story, super-sleuth Sam Spade (Humphrey Bogart) finds himself in the middle of an international hunt for a black-enameled bird. Beneath the enamel is thought to be a priceless statue of gold and jewels. A story of shadowy characters and double crosses, the Maltese falcon turns out to be a fake made of lead. So, Mr. Chekmayan, in effect, thought he had the real fake falcon, the holy grail of movie props. And why not? Flea markets and estate sales are packed with people who mine mountains of stuff with the dream of uncovering a lost Picasso or Civil War daguerreotype. True, there's a sucker born every minute, but every now and then someone hits. The new craze is movie memorabilia.
Fueled by theme restaurants like Planet Hollywood and a robust economy, even the big auction houses that once turned up their noses at movie memorabilia have been doing a brisk business since the late 80's. "There is a certain cachet and nostalgia for things Hollywood," said Vredy Lytsman, a spokeswoman for Christie's.
Memorabilia ranges from a $50 B movie hairpiece to a poster from the 1932 Boris Karloff classic "The Mummy," which fetched $453,000 in March at Sotheby's. In fact, last year one of the statuettes believed to have been used as the Maltese falcon, may have gone for as much as $1 million to an unknown European collector, more than three times its weight in gold, making it the most costly prop ever. The exact price has been kept secret.
"It's the most expensive 'fake' in the world," said Ronald Winston, the New York jeweler who bought that statue two years ago for nearly $400,000. It was discovered in the apartment of the actor William Conrad, star of the "Cannon" TV series, after he died in 1994. Mr. Winston turned the prop around and sold it "at an enormous profit" to the mystery collector, who keeps the bird to himself. "The mystique is that it's a fake," he said. "It's supposed to be gold but it isn't."
Before the Conrad piece surfaced, there was thought to be only one falcon, a 47-pound lead statuette owned by a Beverly Hills dental surgeon. The bird traveled the world in 1992 as the featured piece of a Warner Brothers retrospective. Dr. Gary Milan, the owner of that bird and considered the world's expert on the film, said: "Mr. Chekmayan can prove nothing, and since I own the falcon that has been in the movie, I have an interest in demanding that all pretenders prove themselves."
In 1993, Christie's East, believing Mr. Chekmayan had the real McCoy, listed the statue at $50,000. Then the auction house pulled the bird just weeks before bidding, after the studio threatened to sue Mr. Chekmayan or Christie's East if they advertised the falcon as being tied to the movie.
Mr. Chekmayan has spent thousands of hours and as many dollars to authenticate his find. But that doesn't add up to a hill of beans. What he needs is acknowledgment from Warner Brothers. His evidence: publicity photos of the bird taken with the actors on the set; the serial numbers on the resin falcon are consistent with the other two falcons; a 1941 internal memo stating that a plastic model was requisitioned eight days after production began (resin was an early form of plastic), and the 1994 interview that Mr. Chekmayan's brother did with Meta Carpenter Wilde, taped shortly before her death. Mrs. Wilde, the script supervisor, who took meticulous notes for the director John Huston, stated that the resin falcon was made because Bogey was too slight a man to cart a chunk of lead around the set.
"When we were on walking shots," she said, "on a moving shot, Bogart would have a smaller falcon, a six-pound one. Why should he carry around a 47-pound thing?"
Still, the doctor and the studio continued to claim that just one bird existed.
Mr. Chekmayan crisscrossed the country gathering evidence. His parents died. He slipped into a deep depression and dropped out of the movie business. Then, in 1994, William Conrad died. Suddenly, there were two lead birds.
"It looked fishy," said Glenn Brown, an auction house consultant who specializes in movie memorabilia and the organizer of the Profiles in History auction in October 1995 that was to feature the resin falcon. "Days before our auction, we were threatened with a suit and the bird was pulled," Mr. Brown said. "The crowd booed the doctor. It's a cutthroat world. If you think you have a piece worth a half million, you want to protect it."
There are hundreds of phony falcons, and a thousand mopes. The Library of Congress has a fake (according to Warner Brothers) and a sculptor from New Jersey made hundreds before being shut down by the studio.
A Warner Brothers executive said there is no sure way of telling who, if anyone, possesses the real falcon. Prop records were lost in the early 70's. "Basically it goes on faith," he said, speaking on condition of anonymity.
The studio now sanctions both the doctor's and the Conrad falcon.
As for Mr. Chekmayan's bird, Dr. Milan and the studio continue to dismiss it, even though the doctor initially offered $5,000 for the "curiosity." As for Mrs. Wilde, "She was an old woman," Dr. Milan said.
Now Mr. Chekmayan is left holding the bird, virtually worthless, a passage from Hammett stuck in his head. "If you lose a son it's possible to get another, and there's only one Maltese falcon." Perhaps. But it's the stuff that dreams are made of.

CORRECTION-DATE: July 13, 1997, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
A chart on June 29 with an article about Ara Chekmayan, who owns a statue of a falcon that may have been used in the film "The Maltese Falcon," misstated a plot detail in Dashiell Hammett's novel of the same name. In the novel, the falcon was found in a Paris shop in 1911, not 1931.

HEADLINE: BOOKS OF THE TIMES;
What Caliber Is Your Parachute? A Career Fable

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER LEHMANN-HAUPT 

 BODY:

THE AX
By Donald E. Westlake
273 pages. Mysterious Press/Warner Books. $23. 
 Here's a chilling little fable for the end of the millennium. Burke Devore, the 51-year-old protagonist of Donald E. Westlake's grim new thriller, "The Ax," has been downsized by Halcyon Mills, the paper company for which he has been product manager for 16 years.
Burke knows paper, even the kind you eat: as he explains, "a special kind of paper-source cardboard is used in many commercial ice creams, as a binder." He could "take over almost any managerial job within the paper industry, with only minimal training in a particular specialty."
But with downsizing general all over America, there are too few of those jobs for too many people. And Burke is really not quite the most qualified of candidates, not even for the job with the mill in Arcadia, N.Y., that is starting up a line in which Burke specializes. Ralph Fallon already holds that position. And if anything were to happen to Ralph, there would be others who would probably be hired ahead of Burke.
So Burke -- increasingly worried about the mortgage on his Connecticut home and the welfare of his wife, Marjorie, and their teen-age children, Billy and Betsy -- decides to take extreme measures. He writes an advertisement for a trade magazine announcing that B.D. Industrial Papers of Wildbury, Conn., is looking for a manufacturing line manager. As the resumes pour into the post office box Burke has given as the address of this phony company, he winnows them in search of his most qualified competition.
When he has narrowed the candidates down to six, he packs a Luger pistol he has found among his father's World War II souvenirs and sets out in his Plymouth Voyager to visit the home of the first one, Herbert Coleman Everly. When Everly comes out to get his mail, Burke aims the Luger at him and pulls the trigger. "The Luger jumps in the window space and the left lens of his glasses shatters and his left eye becomes a mineshaft, running deep into the center of the earth."
This leaves five competitors to go. Then Burke will kill Ralph Fallon, and the Arcadia job will be his.
You find this hard to believe? So did I at first. So does Burke himself, a basically decent man whose eyes begin to sting with tears of pity when a police detective investigating the murders shows Burke photographs of two of his victims.
But as Burke proceeds with his awful plan, he keeps tightening the screws of his logic. "The equation is hard and real and ruthless. We're running out of money, Marjorie and I and the kids, and we're running out of time. I have to be employed, that's all. I'm no self-starter, I'm not going to invent a new widget, I'm not going to found my own paper mill on a shoestring. I need a job."
And as he turns the screws, they bite deeper and deeper into the framework of these times. It all began with automation, Burke reflects. Automation attacked blue-collar workers, but because they were unionized "the pain of the transition was somewhat eased."
But now "the child of automation," the computer, was attacking middle management, the white-collar workers, the supervisors, "and none of us are unionized." Burke continues: "This is a transition we're in now, where middle management will shrink like a slug when you pour salt on it, but middle management won't completely disappear. There will just be fewer jobs, that's all, far fewer jobs." And Burke will have one of them.
With such irrefutable reasoning, Burke gradually seduces the reader, and you find yourself almost rooting for him as he works his way along "the learning curve" of murder. Besides, his bleak philosophy actually proves constructive, in a perverse sort of way. When his son, Billy, is arrested for robbing a computer store, Burke uses his new-found cynicism to get the boy out of trouble and thereby wins back the affection of his wife, who had been threatening to leave him because of his growing remoteness since becoming unemployed.
Will Burke succeed at his gruesome project, or will the bloody mayhem he's committing eventually catch up with him? To answer this question is, of course, what ultimately keeps you devouring Mr. Westlake's taut prose. And in the process you can't help being infected by the biting anger of a book whose deeper message really transcends its story.
As Burke muses when approaching his final crisis, "Today, our moral code is based on the idea that the end justifies the means." He continues: "The end of what I'm doing, the purpose, the goal, is good, clearly good. I want to take care of my family; I want to be a productive part of society; I want to put my skills to use; I want to work and pay my own way and not be a burden to the taxpayers. The means to that end has been difficult, but I've kept my eye on the goal, the purpose. The end justifies the means. Like the C.E.O.'s, I have nothing to feel sorry for."
Jonathan Swift would have been delighted with Burke Devore's modest proposal. And readers of "The Ax" will be darkly entertained by the way he goes about, uh, executing it.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Donald E. Westlake (Arturo Patten/Mysterious Press)

HEADLINE: Let It Be;
Ira Magaziner Argues for Minimal Internet Regulation

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
The wild gray mane is tamed. The manic 20-hour workdays are behind him. The health care debacle of President Clinton's first term seems all but forgotten.
But Ira C. Magaziner is still thinking big. Really big. The man behind the intricate architecture of the doomed effort to reorganize health care has spent the last 15 months studying the Internet, or, as he more grandly styles it, the Global Information Infrastructure. 
 And the man who produced a plan for health care management so complex as to be a byword in a town infatuated with complexity, has a new sort of answer for how to cope with vexing problems posed by the Internet, from international copyright protections to the overabundance of digitized smut.
What to do? The answer, offered in a White House report due for release this week: "Not much."
Mr. Magaziner believes the Internet community should be allowed to flourish free of most Government interference. Industry self-regulation would take the place of the Federal nanny, he says.
Is this the same Ira Magaziner who conceived the devilishly complex, 1,342-page scheme to remodel the entire American medical system? The same Ira Magaziner who tried to transform down-at-the-heels Brockton, Mass., into a workers' paradise? The same Ira Magaziner who as a radical student leader pressed Brown University to drop nearly all of its curriculum requirements?
"It is absolutely fascinating that Magaziner has ended up being the great champion of Internet freedom, given his history on health care," said Jeffrey Eisenach, president of the conservative Progress and Freedom Foundation in Washington. "This is a man-bites-dog story of epic proportions."
Communications industry officials who participated in the Magaziner review expressed equal surprise about Mr. Magaziner's transformation and they praised him for approaching the subject with an open mind.
Tom Evslin, who runs AT&T's on-line enterprise, said that he was impressed by the rigor of Mr. Magaziner's study of the electronic world.
"Clearly he had steeped himself in this industry and was a force within the Administration for this de minimis regulatory approach," Mr. Evslin said. "He came down in favor of letting industry solve the industry's problems. The whole effort was built on a coherent intellectual framework."
Last week, the Supreme Court came to much the same conclusion as Mr. Magaziner about the Internet as a free-wheeling forum for global speech and commerce in striking down the Communications Decency Act. The court ruled that the 1996 law to regulate indecent material on the Internet violated the Constitution's free-speech protections and threatened to choke off the potential of the information revolution.
Mr. Magaziner has an explanation for his seeming change of heart from statist central planner to laissez-faire capitalist. Mr. Magaziner has an explanation for just about everything.
But it takes some explaining.
"I view myself as a pragmatist, a problem solver," Mr. Magaziner, 49, said in an interview in his imposing corner office in the Old Executive Office Building next to the White House. "Health care is a very different industry than telecommunications. If you believe, as I do, that access to affordable health care is a right, then you're setting up the notion that the Government is guaranteeing something to people, which it should."
As Ross Perot used to say, stay with me here. Mr. Magaziner has given this subject a lot of study and he wants to make sure his approach is understood.
"You can't have a pure market in health care. Markets are the most efficient allocation of goods and services, but there is not an even buyer-seller relationship between a patient and a doctor," said Mr. Magaziner, who made a small fortune as a management consultant analyzing complex systems, from the city management of Providence, R.I., to the economy of Sweden.
"There has to be prepayment; you can't get seriously ill and then start shopping around for treatment," he continued. "And remember, the Government is already financing close to 50 percent of health care in this country. All that makes the solution to the health care dilemma very different from this."
The Internet, by contrast, is still in its infancy, not an established business like medicine. It has grown up in a decentralized fashion with worldwide choices and very few barriers to entry.
The relationship between buyer and seller -- the consumer at his personal computer and the company doing business over the Internet -- is essentially equal, Mr. Magaziner said.
"It is as close to 'friction free,' to use Bill Gates's term, as one can get," he added.
All of this argues for a hands-off approach by Government, Mr. Magaziner concluded after immersing himself in the culture of cyberspace for more than a year.
As he had in formulating his health care plan, Mr. Magaziner called on representatives of Government and industry, research centers and interest groups, to formulate his new policy. The position paper of his task force, called "A Framework for Global Electronic Commerce," is now in its 16th draft. It will be the basis for a new Administration policy to be announced by President Clinton and Vice President Al Gore at a public event now scheduled for July 1.
Mr. Magaziner's philosophy is spelled out on page 3. He writes that business conducted over the Internet is growing rapidly, but could be throttled by excessive Government intervention.
"For this potential to be realized fully," the draft report states, "governments must adopt a nonregulatory, market-oriented approach to electronic commerce, one that facilitates the emergence of a transparent and predictable legal environment to support global business and commerce. Official decision makers must respect the unique nature of the medium and recognize that widespread competition and increased consumer choice should be the defining features of the new digital marketplace."
Stripped of Mr. Magaziner's bureaucratese: Let a thousand Web sites bloom.
The study group concluded that Government involvement would be necessary in a number of areas -- negotiating international agreements on tariffs and taxation, for example, or protecting intellectual property from electronic piracy. Official oversight may be needed to protect against fraud and the exploitation of children as well, the group concluded.
The task force also accepted law enforcement's insistence on retaining some access to encrypted data, the one area where the computer industry is almost unanimous in opposition to the policy.
One participant, while criticizing the encryption policy, praised Mr. Magaziner for conducting a robust debate and listening to industry.
"He seemed very open and curious," said Solveig Bernstein, a telecommunications analyst at the libertarian Cato Institute. "I must say that Cato was certainly very much opposed to some of the ideas he came up with in the context of health care reform. In speaking to Ira, I felt he was sincerely trying to do the right thing and trying to learn about market solutions. That's unusual in someone with a regulatory bent."
This, too, represents something of a rehabilitation of Mr. Magaziner's image. As director of Mr. Clinton's health care task force, Mr. Magaziner endured as much criticism for the secrecy of the process as for the complexity of the product.
Much of the work of the health-care task force was conducted behind closed doors. Mr. Magaziner even tried for a time to keep the names of the members of the health care study group secret. He was sued by one health care advocacy organization, which contended that Mr. Magaziner had lied in a sworn statement about the composition of the White House task force he directed.
A court later found that the allegation was unfounded.
The Internet inquiry, by contrast, has included nearly every group with an interest in the subject and its hearings have been held in public. Drafts of its report have circulated on the Internet since December and hundreds of people have commented on it.
Mr. Magaziner has an explanation for this change, too.
He said that he had advocated an open process for the health care study but was overruled by senior White House aides, who believed that opening the hearings to the press and to interest groups would have brought intense pressure on the participants and slowed down the process. The group was under orders from President Clinton to produce a comprehensive plan in 100 days to redeem a 1992 campaign promise.
"The process was flawed and we ended up with a bill that was more regulatory than we wanted," Mr. Magaziner said with a surprising note of humility. "I blame myself. I could have put my foot down."
The Internet project gave him an opportunity to start at the beginning and take as long as he needed to complete his research, rather than being driven by artificial deadlines, he said.
Musing further on the differences between the highly structured world of health care and the free-wheeling cyberuniverse, Mr. Magaziner said that he tried to find a solution that would fit the reality of the industry.
"If there's ever an arena that should be market driven, this is it," Mr. Magaziner said.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Ira Magaziner, 1993; Ira Magaziner, 1997; Photo illustration by Lee Yarosh/The New York Times
 
OLD PHILOSOPHY
Health Care Reform
 
Centralized health care insurance-buying cooperatives.
 
New Government regulatory organizations.
 
Detailed 1,342-page plan to revamp the health care industry, including numerous mandates on providers and insurers.
 
Extensive legislative changes required to enact plan.
 
System of cost controls to insure affordable health care for all.
 
NEW PHILOSOPHY
Internet Policy
 
Decentralized global communications network.
 
Industry self-regulation with minimal Government oversight.
 
Broad 30-page framework document that prescribes a "market-oriented" approach to electronic commerce.
 
Little new legislation or regulation required by proposal.
 
In an electronic marketplace, competition will drive down prices.

HEADLINE: Basseterre Journal;
Death, Drugs and Courtroom Drama Trample on a Tranquil Caribbean Isle

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: BASSETERRE, St. Kitts and Nevis, June 26

 BODY:
The scandal that toppled a Government and has led to a 27-year-old laborer's being tried three times on the same murder charge began in the summer of 1994.
More than a ton and a half of cocaine, on its way from Colombia to the United States, disappeared from a hiding place on an isolated beach here. Within days Vincent Morris, a son of the Deputy Prime Minister, had been killed, along with his girlfriend. Two of his brothers, Dave and Dean, were charged with illegal possession of guns and cocaine. Then Jude Matthew, the Superintendent of Police, who was in charge of the investigation into the Morris killing, was also slain, pumped full of bullets from a pistol as he sat in his jeep in his driveway. 
 The laborer, David Lawrence, was eventually charged with killing Superintendent Matthew. But the Morris brothers were released, prompting charges of favoritism that led to a prison riot. Troops from the Eastern Caribbean Regional Security System had to be sent to this normally tranquil island nation of 40,000 people, the smallest in the Western Hemisphere, 250 miles southeast of Puerto Rico.
During Mr. Lawrence's first trial, there were reports of threats being made and bribes being offered to jurors. The trial ended with a hung jury, as did his second, in December 1996. Mr. Lawrence's third trial, which began late last month in a sweltering courtroom here in the capital, offered more of the same. Testimony had barely begun when the police arrested six people and charged them with jury tampering, and the judge took the highly unusual step of ordering that the jury of eight women and one man be sequestered.
Then, on June 20, the trial ended with the jury once again announcing it was unable to reach a verdict.
The rest of the Caribbean has been watching the continuing drama in horrified fascination. It has also drawn the attention of the United States, which is struggling to contain the increased flow of drugs through the Eastern Caribbean into Puerto Rico and sees the twin island federation as perhaps the most vulnerable link in the drug interdiction chain.
"Of all the places in the English-speaking Caribbean, St. Kitts came closest to devolution into a narco-state, and is still under a great deal of pressure," an American official in the region said.
"This Government is doing its best," he continued. "But they have limited resources, and the drug traffickers have insinuated themselves into the legitimate economy through ownership of hotels, beverage franchises and car rental firms, so even if the Government is willing to work with you, there is a question of how much ability and freedom they have to do so."
For the Government now in power, much is at stake. Running on a platform of law and order, the Labor Party won an election in 1995 called three years early as a result of the scandal over the missing cocaine and the violence it generated. It has since stepped up cooperation with the United States and brought in a former Scotland Yard official to be interim chief of the demoralized police force.
"This case is important for transparency," Prime Minister Denzil L. Douglas said in an interview before the end of the third trial. "Of course this is a matter for the jury, but the right signal needs to be sent to criminals, to the local and international community and to the police that this Government will do everything to insure that justice is sought when one of its own is killed in the line of duty."
One name that has emerged in both the jury tampering and drug smuggling cases is that of Charles Miller, an influential local businessman. He is wanted in the United States on cocaine trafficking charges stemming from the 1994 shipment and was one of those arrested last month on the jury tampering charges. He is said by law enforcement officials here to have threatened Mr. Matthew, the police superintendent, the day before his death.
In an interview, Mr. Miller denied any involvement in Mr. Matthew's death and dismissed both the American drug trafficking indictment and the jury tampering charges here as "part of a political plot." He described himself as an aggressive but honest entrepreneur who imports soft drinks and frozen chicken and vegetables and wants merely to be a public benefactor.
"That kid never worked for me. I just gave him shelter when his own mother threw him out," he said, referring to Mr. Lawrence. "The problem is that I am too black, too young and too successful for the local elite."
Mr. Miller, 37, acknowledged that he was aware of the 1994 drug shipment and that he had even discussed the disappearance of the drugs, which he said was the fault of the Morris family, with "a bunch of guys who spoke English with a heavy Spanish accent." But he says his role was that of a peacemaker, not the leader of a conspiracy to ship the drugs to the United States.
"The Colombians had a serious problem: they were looking for a 1,500-kilo shipment of cocaine that had gone missing, and someone told them to talk to me," he said. "The only crime I am guilty of is telling the son of the Deputy Prime Minister to give back that cocaine and get it off the island so that peace could reign in our land."
Little more than a year ago Mr. Miller and three confederates were arrested here, with the United States quickly requesting their extradition to face drug charges in Miami. One man charged with Mr. Miller, Clifford Henry, has since been found guilty in Miami and sentenced to life in prison, but the extradition of Mr. Miller was foiled when a magistrate here refused to allow tapes of intercepted telephone conversations to be used as evidence.
"We were surprised by that outcome, because we were convinced by the Americans that they had enough information, enough evidence, with regard to the charges that were being laid," Mr. Douglas said. "We felt for a while that, well, everything had been lost," but the decision is now under judicial review.
The Prime Minister would not say if his Government would seek to try Mr. Lawrence a fourth time. But an American official who is watching the case said the stakes on the little island are larger than ever.
"If you lose a case like this, what's the message?" he asked. "Basically, that the drug folks are immune and can control the judicial system. They already feel they can act with a great deal of impunity, and if this case is lost, it will obviously have an impact on morale and the ability to go after these guys."

HEADLINE: Mir Astronauts Now Facing Uncharted Territory

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, June 29

 BODY:
Vasily Tsibliyev, the commander of the Mir space station, has had his brushes with danger. The veteran Russian Air Force pilot, 43, has made five space walks and spent long periods in space. He had endured a dangerous fire, which filled Mir with smoke and splattered chucks of molten metal around the space station.
But the commander, who carries a plastic rabbit from his daughter as a good luck charm, has his worries about his new assignment: repairing the stricken Mir, which was severely damaged last week when a supply vehicle crashed into it. 
 "I have never done this kind of work," he bluntly warned officials at Russia's ground control center. "Without training, it will not be possible to do this job."
By all accounts, restoring Mir to a functioning space station is a tricky venture into uncharted territory. Not only has the crew never trained for the mission, but they will also use special equipment that is only now being built.
The job will also require unusual dexterity, including maneuvering through a narrow portal to the damaged module while wearing a bulky space suit.
To choreograph the intricate maneuvers, earth-based Russian astronauts will experiment on an underwater mockup at Star City, the astronaut training center outside Moscow. Their results will be transmitted to Mir so the crew can practice before putting them to the test. If the Mir crew fails, it may even be necessary to abandon ship.
Michael Foale, the American member of the team of three astronauts in it, will be waiting in the Soyuz spacecraft docked alongside while the repairs are attempted in case the crew has to make a quick getaway and blast off to earth.
Life on Mir has never been glamorous. Mr. Foale has likened it to camping out in a dirty, old car. Others have not been so kind. Launched by the Soviet Union in 1986, Mir was originally intended to stay in space for only five years. After 11 years, it has had more than its share of problems.
In February, an oxygen canister caught fire, filling the station with smoke. In March, oxygen generators failed. In April, the cooling system began leaking antifreeze.
But Russia has powerful political and economic incentives to keep Mir in operation. The space station is not only a symbol of Russia's prowess in space, but is also a source of income for its fiscally strapped space agency. The United States is paying the Russians $470 million to train astronauts on Mir as part of a program to develop a new international space station.
NASA officials insist that the training and scientific experiments make the Mir missions worthwhile. But that assumes that the major problem on Mir caused by the collision last week -- the lack of adequate power to carry out scientific experiments -- can be remedied.
The crisis erupted on Wednesday when an unmanned Progress supply craft crunched into the 140-ton Mir while Mr. Tsibliyev was practicing a docking maneuver. The collision punctured Spektr, one of the station's seven modules and the module that housed medical experiments.
"I heard the big thump and a thud," Mr. Foale later recounted. "Almost immediately, we heard a hiss and felt pressure falling."
The crew rushed to seal off the damaged module, disconnecting cables that carry power from Spektr's three undamaged solar arrays to the rest of the station and closing the hatch.
"Since that exciting moment, we've been dealing with all the problems that resulted from that," Mr. Foale said. The main problem has been a power shortfall, as Spektr's solar arrays accounted for much of Mir's energy supply.
As the crew shut down air conditioning and other units to conserve energy, the space station grew hotter, humid and dark. Mir had trouble maintaining its orientation in orbit when the computer that fired the station's jets lost power. So the crew fired the rockets on the Soyuz to stabilize their position.
By today, life was getting back to normal -- or as normal as things can be on the aging Mir.
By turning the space station, the crew was able to point the solar arrays more directly at the sun, and recharge the station's batteries. The temperature and humidity dropped, and the crew was able to turn on the system that removes carbon dioxide from Mir's atmosphere.
Ground controllers also sent a signal activating Mir's gyroscopes to improve control of the space station. Some power was even used to run a greenhouse, an experiment to see how seeds grow in space.
Even so, Mir has only 50 to 60 percent of its power supply, which is not enough for most of the scientific experiments NASA and the Russians have planned, a NASA official said. The future of the space station depends on fixing the power problem.
The trick is to reconnect the cables from the Spektr module to the rest of Mir without letting the air escape from the space station. That was a problem that bedeviled the Russian and NASA scientists as they struggled to develop a plan.
The final plan is to launch a new Progress supply vessel about July 5 from the Baikonur Cosmodrome in Kazakstan. The Progress will transport equipment including a new laptop computer for Mr. Foale, lights and bolt cutters.
It will also carry a special plate to which the electric cables in Spektr can be connected. That piece of equipment was to be completed today at a factory near Moscow and flown to Baikonur on Monday.
Another key piece of equipment is already on board Mir. It is a cone-shaped device used for docking, Viktor D. Blagov, the deputy Mir mission controller, said in an interview.
The idea is for Russian astronauts to conduct an internal space walk. After donning their space suits, they will enter the section of Mir next to Spektr. The rest of the space station will be sealed off.
The Russian astronauts will then remove the hatch to Spektr, and at least one of them will maneuver through the tight portal and enter Spektr.
The astronauts will take the cables in Spektr and run them through the center of the cone-like device, which is open at the top. The cone will be inserted in the portal between Spektr and the rest of Mir.
The special plate will be used to seal the top of the cone. Cables from Spektr will be attached to one side of the plate. Cables to carry power to the rest of Mir will be attached to the other, Mr. Blagov said. That will enable the power to pass through while maintaining an airtight seal.
The section next to Spektr will then be repressurized. Once it is determined that air is not leaking, the section will be opened up to the rest of the space station.
During the difficult repairs, Mr. Foale will be zippered up in his space suit and waiting in the Soyuz space craft. That way the entire crew can make a quick evacuation if an emergency develops.
Today, the crew appeared relaxed in a Russian television broadcast. Commander Tsibliyev said his wife was not unduly worried. His flight engineer, Aleksandr Lazutkin, praised the training he has received. Mr. Foale said he would be prepared to go up in space again.
But the crew seemed less sure of itself when it was first informed about the repair mission during an off-camera session.
After the crew was informed of the plan, reporters in the Russian command center heard one of the Russian astronauts mutter, "It's very problematic."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Aleksandr Lazutkin, Michael Foale and Vasily Tsibliyev yesterday, in their first television link since the crash. (Reuters)(pg. A1)
 
Diagram: "THE MISSION: Repairing the Station"

The Spektr module was punctured Wednesday and a solar panel damaged during a collision with an unmanned cargo ship. To seal off the depressurized module, the crew had to disconnect cables that carry power from Spektr's four solar panels to the rest of the station, three of which were operational. Here is how Russian space officials plan the operation.
 
*1 Progress will dock with Mir several days after the launch. Repairs should begin between July 10 and 14.
 
*2 The two Russian astronauts will enter the node in their spacesuits and seal it off from the rest of the station.
 
*3 They will open the hatch to Spektr. At least one of them will maneuver through the tight portal.
 
*4 They will install in the portal a specially made plate to which electric cables can be connected. This will allow them to reconnect power while keeping Spektr sealed off.
 
*5 They will repressurize the node, making sure that air is not escaping.
 
*6 The American astronaut will wait in the Soyuz capsule wearing his space suit. That way the entire crew can quickly escape in the capsule in an emergency.

HEADLINE: The SBC Deal Shelved, What Tack For AT&T?

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

 BODY:
For the AT&T Corporation, the big question is: Now what?
On Friday, the nation's largest telephone company suspended negotiations to merge with SBC Communications -- talks that AT&T never formally acknowledged, though they consumed the company's top executives and drew fierce fire from Washington regulators. 
 Now, AT&T must regroup and fashion a strategy to compete on its own in a deregulated market. That could be even harder than engineering a $50 billion merger. With every other phone company gunning for its long-distance franchise, AT&T has no choice but to compete for local-service customers. But by entering into talks with SBC, analysts and industry executives said, AT&T acknowledged that it cannot do it alone.
"This was a Hail Mary pass," Daniel Reingold, a Merrill Lynch analyst, said. "AT&T was attempting to declare peace on one front so it could defend all its other fronts."
Whatever the intrinsic merits of a deal, the company's peculiar handling of the negotiations may have tarnished both its credibility and morale. AT&T never confirmed the existence of talks, though its chairman, Robert E. Allen, defended the concept of a merger between AT&T and a regional Bell company in the face of withering criticism from regulators. SBC was noticeably silent on the subject.
Mr. Allen may also have undermined his president and designated heir, John R. Walter, by making it clear that SBC's chairman, Edward E. Whitacre, could become the chief executive of a combined company, according to executives who were involved in the talks.
That Mr. Allen was ready to tolerate these negative repercussions is evidence to many experts of just how desperately he wanted a deal.
"AT&T would have been willing to restructure this deal any which way to make it happen because they know that it would be such a formidable alliance," said Gene Kimmelman, co-director of the Washington office of Consumers Union.
A spokeswoman for AT&T declined to comment yesterday on the company's talks with SBC.
Still, the financial logic of a merger is so compelling that many experts suspect that AT&T may yet try to resuscitate talks with the San Antonio-based company -- which offers local phone service to more than 30 million home and business customers in seven states west of the Mississippi, including Texas and California.
"My suspicion is that the talks could reignite," said a former high-level AT&T executive, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "If it is a really good idea, AT&T will keep pursuing it."
Failing that, AT&T could try to forge a deal with another regional Bell company, like BellSouth, or with GTE.
Mr. Reingold calculated that a merger of AT&T and SBC would have added $16 billion to the value of the companies -- roughly a 15 percent increase in the market value of each partner. Of the $16 billion, he said $7 billion would come from less price competition in both local and long-distance service in SBC's territory. AT&T and SBC would no longer try to undercut each other's rates.
The remaining $9 billion would derive from a combination of cost-cutting, new business opportunities and the value for each partner in not losing market share to the other, Mr. Reingold said.
For AT&T, the economics of going it alone look unappealing. The company would have to wage a 50-state war for local phone customers against existing monopolies who would sooner or later be allowed into AT&T's core long-distance market. Analysts estimated that it would cost the company tens of billions of dollars to get into local service.
And after such expense, it is not clear that AT&T would emerge the victor. Mr. Reingold contends that the Bells -- with their hard-to-duplicate local networks and direct connections to customers -- would be able to retain customers more effectively than AT&T.
That is not to say that AT&T lacks choices. The company has one of the most robust balance sheets in the telephone industry. It could establish a presence in the local phone market overnight by acquiring a competitive provider of local service, such as the Teleport Communications Group.
AT&T could also develop new technologies, like its recently announced fixed-wireless service, to insinuate itself into the market. And the power of its brand name cannot be overestimated.
To capitalize on these strengths, though, people familiar with AT&T said Mr. Allen must return to a single-minded focus on the phone business. An employee who declined to be identified said that the reports about an SBC merger and Mr. Walter's future had been the stuff of incessant hallway gossip.
"SBC is a distraction," the employee said. "What will save AT&T is a real strategy."
AT&T may not regain its focus unless Mr. Allen settles the questions about Mr. Walter. Mr. Allen recently promoted another AT&T executive, John D. Zeglis, to vice chairman -- sowing doubts about Mr. Walter's future. Executives familiar with AT&T said Mr. Zeglis had been elevated partly because he had been offered the chairmanship of a utility company in the Midwest.
Mr. Zeglis may not be Mr. Walter's only rival for the top job. George M. C. Fisher, the chairman of the Eastman Kodak Company, had been AT&T's first choice as AT&T's president, people with knowledge of the company said. While Mr. Fisher turned down the offer, he recently joined AT&T's board.
Further complicating matters is that AT&T is pushing through a two-year, $2.6 billion cost-cutting campaign. So the same employees who must redouble their efforts to lead AT&T into a brave new world are worrying about being laid off or reassigned.
If AT&T's future seems murky, its minuet with SBC has muddied the picture for the rest of the telephone industry, too. Several industry experts said that longtime AT&T competitors like MCI Communications and Sprint would have to reassess their strategies -- even if the merger of AT&T and a Bell company remained only a rumor.
Indeed, MCI's chairman, Bert C. Roberts Jr., said in a speech last week that if AT&T and SBC were to merge, his company "will be taking names" from the Baby Bells that would like to tie up with MCI.
Some experts likened the merger talks to the opening of a Pandora's box, something that could forever change the competitive landscape. "The fact that this deal fell apart does not change the fundamental dynamic of massive consolidation in telecommunications rather than head-to-head competition," Mr. Kimmelman of Consumers Union said.
While officials at the Federal Communications Commission were gratified by news of the deal's collapse, they said the industry could still veer off the road to competition.
"It won't help if we prevent the reassembly of Ma Bell, only to allow similar deals in the future," said Blair Levin, chief of staff to the F.C.C.'s chairman, Reed E. Hundt.
Mr. Hundt was the Government's most outspoken critic of the talks, declaring in a speech that a merger of AT&T and a Bell company would never withstand antitrust scrutiny. But Mr. Hundt was not around to savor his role in scuttling what would have been the largest corporate merger in history. Instead, he was traveling in India over the weekend.

HEADLINE: CLINTON TAX PLAN BACKS G.O.P. IDEAS

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
President Clinton will announce on Monday a revised tax-cutting proposal that his aides describe as an effort to give middle-class taxpayers somewhat deeper reductions than those passed overwhelmingly by the Senate on Friday, but one that accepts a number of the main concepts pressed by Republicans.
In a presentation at the White House, Mr. Clinton is expected to endorse, for the first time, tax proposals that would create education savings accounts that are similar to individual retirement accounts. 
 He is also expected to make proposals that edge toward a broader cut in capital gains taxes than he has previously endorsed, said officials who have been briefed on the broad outlines of Mr. Clinton's plan. But he will not agree -- at least for now -- on the House and Senate proposals to reduce those taxes to 20 percent, from 28 percent, the officials said.
Aides to Mr. Clinton declined today to provide details of his proposals, hoping that he will regain the public relations advantage for his tax plans by making the proposals at the start of a weeklong Congressional recess. But they said his broad effort would be toward pushing more of the $85 billion in net tax cuts down to the bottom 60 percent of taxpayers.
Under tax legislation passed on Thursday by the House, roughly 18 percent of the $85 billion in tax cuts would go to the 1 percent of Americans with the highest family incomes, according to Treasury Department estimates.
The Senate bill, passed on Friday, would channel about 13 percent of the total tax cuts to the same group. Mr. Clinton's proposal, the officials said, would cut that 13 percent by more than half, though White House officials acknowledge that the figure is subject to change as the negotiations proceed next month.
Nonetheless, the concessions the White House has made so far -- including virtual agreement to a sharp reduction in capital gains and estate taxes -- make it clear that the final bill will reverse many of the effects of the major tax increase for the top 1.2 percent of taxpayers that Mr. Clinton pushed through in 1993.
Mr. Clinton's political strategists had guessed that the richest Americans would not battle higher income tax rates if one result was enough deficit reduction to send the country and the stock market into a prolonged economic expansion that disproportionately benefited the rich.
The bet paid off, though Mr. Clinton was roundly criticized last year by fellow Democrats who had voted for those increases after he suggested that he thought he had raised taxes on the rich too much.
Mr. Clinton's aides now argue that the tax increase cut the deficit sharply and helped pave the way for the Dow Jones Industrial Average to rise to 7,800, more than double the level in 1993. Whether he can rightly take credit for that increase, a practical result is that many wealthy Americans are now sitting on huge capital gains -- which they are eager to liquidate at lower tax rates.
Although Mr. Clinton will not endorse a 20 percent capital gains tax rate on Monday, the body language given off by his aides in recent weeks suggests that he will ultimately agree to that figure. But they insist that he will veto any bill that also allows investors to subtract the effects of inflation on those capital gains. Such indexing is included in the House version of the tax bill, but not in the Senate version.
Mr. Clinton's proposal will open a round of three-dimensional chess on Capitol Hill that promises to dominate politics here for the next month. Starting a week from now, House and Senate negotiators will begin trying to work out differences between their respective tax bills. But because House and Senate leaders want to avoid a veto, the White House will play a major role in the negotiations.
Mr. Clinton's new proposal is described by aides as an effort to build on some provisions of those bills and limit the effects of others. The Senate tax bill, for example, creates classes of individual retirement accounts that are available to all taxpayers. Mr. Clinton's proposals will put income caps on those I.R.A.'s because Treasury officials argued that the Senate version would simply encourage rich Americans to move existing savings into tax-sheltered accounts.
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said in an interview today: "It's a question of how you look at the goals of tax relief. You could look at tax cuts as an effort to provide middle-class tax relief, or you could look at them as an effort to promote growth in the economy. What I have come to think in recent weeks is that those two criteria would lead you to roughly the same outcome."
Put another way, Mr. Rubin is arguing that the existing bills in Congress provide too many tax benefits to people who are rich enough to save anyway. The only way to encourage long-term savings for economic stimulus is to reorient those incentives to families making under $50,000 to $60,000 a year, the bottom three-quarters of the nation's taxpayers. Those are the families deciding between saving and spending.
Representative Bill Archer of Texas, the chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee and the principal author of the House tax bill, reiterated today in a letter to Mr. Clinton that the Administration was using figures that "artificially inflate people's incomes" to make it appear that too large a portion of the cuts were going to the rich. Mr. Archer argued that "71 percent of our tax relief goes to those who make between $20,000 and $75,000 a year."
Those statistical arguments stem from the fact that Mr. Archer's committee and the Administration are using very different measures of total income to make their case. The Treasury bases its estimates on a measure called family economic income, first put forward in the Reagan Administration. That measure takes into account not only salary and interest earned on savings but also pension benefits and other forms of remuneration that most families cannot immediately spend.
The Republicans are using more traditional measures of income. But in defining tax cuts, they are using statistics to make it appear that the wealthy do not benefit disproportionately. For example, they measure the effects of tax cuts only over the next five years, while many of the biggest gains for the wealthy would come after 2002.
Mr. Clinton is clearly hoping that at the end of the negotiations, he will be able to divert more of the tax cuts to families seeking to make use of education tax credits for attending community colleges. The Administration is also concerned that the education savings accounts that emerged from the House and Senate bills give too much aid to families that can save long in advance of college and too little aid to those who cannot afford such savings.
The White House also expects a long battle over the tobacco tax. The Senate plan would raise $15 billion over five years by increasing the tax on cigarettes by 20 cents a pack, to 44 cents, starting on Oct. 1. About $8 billion of those revenues would be committed to providing medical coverage for uninsured children. The House plan has no such provision.

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Gore Warms Up to Iowa as He Warms Up for 2000

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: DES MOINES, June 29

 BODY:
When Senator Al Gore ran in the Democratic Presidential primaries in 1988, he never found his footing in this crucial early battleground. So he dismantled his flagging campaign here and condemned the early caucuses in "the small state of Iowa" as "madness."
But this weekend, Vice President Al Gore left no doubt of his political awakening. "I love Iowa," he assured voters. Recalling the movie "Field of Dreams," Mr. Gore told the audience in the rural community of Prairie City: "Is this heaven? To which Kevin Costner replies, 'No, it's Iowa.' " And to fit in, Mr. Gore changed his outfit at least three times in one day -- from standard blue suit and red tie to casual shirt and khakis and back again -- depending on the audience. 
 Despite his protestations about its being "way too early," Mr. Gore is running for President. And he is running hard. As Mr. Gore's 16-vehicle motorcade snaked around this state on Saturday, the route looked like a road map of Democratic constituencies vital to combatants in the caucuses here in 2000: unions, farmers, environmentalists and Democratic organizers. (He traded his White House-issue limousine for a Chevrolet Suburban sport vehicle.)
This was Mr. Gore's first trip to Iowa since last year's election, and his aggressive -- and early -- courtship underscores how, unlike in 1988, the Vice President views this state as crucial to his Presidential ambitions. Mr. Gore's advisers also said they were preparing for a relentless competition with Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the minority leader, who is already mobilizing his operation in the state, and who won the caucuses here in 1988. (Mr. Gore placed dead last, with less than 1 percent.)
It did not matter that Mr. Gore did not overtly announce his run for President: his carefully orchestrated appearances here, replete with American flags and picturesque backdrops for the cameras, had more the feel and content of partisan campaign events than White House courtesy visits. Sounding less buttoned-down than when he is in the capital, Mr. Gore liberally addressed audiences as "y'all." And he referred repeatedly to the fruits of his Administration's economic policies helping bring about Iowa's "astonishing 3.3 percent unemployment rate."
At stop after stop, Mr. Gore buttered up one of his traveling companions, Representative Leonard L. Boswell of Iowa, telling audiences that the freshman Democrat was "fantastic" and "wonderful" -- and off to one of the swiftest starts of any congressman in history. It was not that Mr. Gore was rewarding an old friend. That is the problem: Mr. Boswell is a longtime ally of Mr. Gephardt, and Mr. Gore wants to win him over.
(Mr. Boswell insisted that it was too soon to take sides. Reminded of his prediction that Mr. Gephardt could take the state, Mr. Boswell replied, "He's won here before.")
Not easily discouraged, Mr. Gore plunged into campaign overdrive on Saturday, careful not to skip any crucial constituent groups.
In Des Moines, he asserted at the state convention of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees that the Administration would avoid budget cuts that unduly fell on workers. He promised to protect the 40-hour workweek and pledged to "fight for more exports from Iowa." For good measure, Mr. Gore reminded the audience that his father was the first labor secretary in the state of Tennessee.
In Johnston, at the headquarters of Pioneer Hi-Bred, an agriculture research company, Mr. Gore trudged through corn and soybean fields. He scored points with farmers by mentioning that as Vice President he broke a tie in the Senate to preserve ethanol subsidies. As if he has the time to commute regularly from the capital to tend to his farm, Mr. Gore said, "Tipper and I live on a farm in Tennessee when we are not in Washington."
In Prairie City, at the dedication of the Neal Smith Prairie Learning Center at a wildlife refuge, Mr. Gore looked on with a smile as Mr. Smith, a former congressman, proclaimed him "a world leader in environmental affairs." Mr. Gore, who was essentially raised in Washington, but spent long summer stretches at his family's farm, tried to connect with the locals by saying that "Prairie City is a lot like my hometown of Carthage, Tenn."
And in Indianola, at a Democratic field training conference at Simpson College, Mr. Gore delivered a stump speech worthy of a candidate in the final throes of a campaign. After thanking the audience for re-electing him and Mr. Clinton, saying it was "really important," the Vice President implored the workers to look toward 1998 and beyond: "Fight and organize and register people to vote!"
Mr. Gore was looser on the stump than his critics give him credit for. He was not defensive when asked at the taping of a public television program about the uproar over his fund-raising activities in the 1996 campaign. He was especially comfortable at the two events that were stocked with his loyalists: the union conference at the beginning of the day and the Democratic meeting at the end. He larded his speeches with his self-effacing humor, as in his recitation of the historic list of unknowns who were also two-term Vice Presidents. (He did stumble when he praised the Democratic workers, saying "you really are the foot soldiers," before changing his mind in the next breath and saying, "you are the generals.")
For all his efforts, Mr. Gore was not able to deliver as fiery a speech as did Senator Paul Wellstone, Democrat of Minnesota, who is toying with running for President and who addressed the union convention this morning, a day after the Vice President's speech. Though Mr. Gore had been cheered loudly, the remarks by Mr. Wellstone, an unrepentant liberal, were received more enthusiastically; his speech was interrupted more than once by foot-stomping standing ovations.
But Mr. Gore got off one good quip when he recalled to the union audience his fleeting campaign here in 1988: "It's no secret that I ran for President in 1988, although it seemed like one at the time."
Not this time around. Mr. Gore's worst kept secret is his Presidential campaign in Iowa.

HEADLINE: Mexican Conglomerate Abandons Cuban Phone Venture

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: MIAMI, June 29

 BODY:
Hamstrung by American economic sanctions and its own financial problems, Grupo Domos, the Mexican conglomerate that has been the largest foreign investor in Cuba, has relinquished its stake in the Cuban telephone company and withdrawn from the island.
For the last three years, the telecommunications company based in Monterrey has been minority partner in a $750 million joint venture with the Cuban Government in that country's telephone monopoly. But Cuba's phone system once was owned by American interests, and that became an issue last year after the United States Congress approved a law intensifying the 35-year-old American economic embargo against Cuba. 
 The legislation, enacted after Cuba shot down a pair of American-registered civilian light planes over international waters, requires foreign companies to report to the United States any property they have in Cuba that was confiscated from American owners by the Communist Government of Fidel Castro. Those companies can then be sued for compensation in American courts.
Last summer, Domos was one of three companies identified by the Clinton Administration as violating the measure, known popularly as the Helms-Burton law, after its two main sponsors. As a result, top Domos executives, shareholders and members of their families have been denied visas to visit or study in the United States.
Cuban exile groups here, as well as some American Government officials, are celebrating the withdrawal of Domos and citing it as proof that Helms-Burton is an effective tool in weakening Mr. Castro and the Cuban economy. But business analysts maintain that Domos made the decision for reasons that are far more diverse and complicated.
"This should not be seen as a commentary on the validity of the investment," said John S. Kavulich, president of the U.S.-Cuba Trade and Economic Council, a New York-based group that monitors the Cuban economy. "Grupo Domos's problems stem in large part from the fact they weren't a company with substantial assets going into the deal."
Officials at Cuba's Ministry of Foreign Investment and Economic Cooperation did not respond to inquiries requesting reaction to the company's move, first reported Friday in The Miami Herald. Executives at Domos also did not return telephone calls seeking comment.
But both Mexico and Canada have severely criticized Helms-Burton, describing the law as a restraint on trade and investment that violates the North American Free Trade Agreement and other international treaties. The Clinton Administration's European allies have also attacked the measure and threatened to file a formal complaint against Washington with the World Trade Organization.
The Domos deal was announced amid great pomp at a ceremony in Havana in 1994 in the presence of both Mr. Castro and the Mexican President at the time, Carlos Salinas de Gortari. The agreement was then seen as opening the way to future Mexican involvement in refurbishing and expanding Cuba's cement, glass, mining, oil and tourism industries.
Later that year, however, the Mexican peso collapsed, plunging the investment into jeopardy. A weak peso made it increasingly expensive and difficult for Domos to obtain the dollars it needed to fulfill its obligations under the agreement, and it missed several deadlines, according to Cuban Government officials.
The passage of the Helms-Burton law last year only added to the company's problems. "They were having trouble getting financing even before Helms-Burton came along, but potential lenders became even more gun shy after that went into effect," said a European banker who follows Cuban economic policy closely.
Worried that plans to modernize the moribund Cuban telephone system, which had been expropriated from the ITT Corporation of New York in 1960, were in danger, the Cuban Government began looking for other foreign investors. The Italian telecommunications company Stet S.p.A. eventually came in as a partner, and has indicated that it intends to remain in Cuba despite the Helms-Burton law, Cuban officials and industry executives said.
American officials and industry executives said ITT, which has $130 million in outstanding Cuban claims, and Stet have been talking intermittently for several months about an agreement to compensate the American company for its confiscated holdings.

Not Fade Away

As baby boomers age, more and more people are restoring pictures, aided by new technology, and sending them to family and friends. [D6.]
